[1] First Act
[2] First Scene
[3] Hamlet Castle
[4] September 8, Tuesday, 10:30 AM
[5] Below, shimmering in the pale blue morning mist, is the Hudson River, a small white sail swiftly gliding across its surface, and a steamboat rocking upstream.
[6] The car steadily climbs up the narrow winding slopes, with two people inside, both peering out of the window. In the distance, a medieval castle appears amidst the misty veil, with its massive stone walls, arrow slits, and ancient church spires standing out in the lush forest.
[7] The two passengers exchange a glance. "I feel like I've been transported back to the 18th-century Connecticut during the American Revolution," one of them says, shivering nervously.
[8] The other, a burly man, gruffly replies, "Those armored knights, right?"
[9] The car abruptly stops at the entrance of an ancient-looking bridge, where a rosy-cheeked old man emerges from a small hut with a thatched roof. He points to a wooden sign hanging on the door without saying a word, which reads in ancient script:
[10] No Trespassing
[11] Hamlet Manor
[12] The larger man leaned out of the window and shouted, "We're here to see Mr. Philo Vance."
[13] "Yes, sir," the old man replied, approaching them. "May I see your passports, please?"
[14] The two visitors exchanged a puzzled look, and the shorter man shrugged while the taller one sighed impatiently. "It's Mr. Vance who invited us," he said.
[15] "Oh, I see," the bridge guardian replied, scratching his head of gray hair before disappearing into his hut. He reappeared shortly after, announcing, "Apologies, gentlemen. Please follow me." He hurried to the bridge entrance, pulled open the iron gate, and stood respectfully aside. The car crossed the bridge and accelerated onto a smooth, clean gravel road.
[16] As the car passed through a lush oak grove, it came to a wide open space. The castle, resembling a sleeping giant, lay quietly in front of them, surrounded by low granite walls that hugged the rolling Hudson hills. As the car approached, a heavy, iron-bolted gate creaked open, revealing another old man standing on the other side, beaming with joy and waving at them.
[17] The car turned onto another stretch of road lined with flowering shrubs, clearly tended to with care. The evergreens on either side, perfectly spaced and uniform in size, stood like sentinels. Beyond them lay a few small, thatched-roof farmhouses scattered amidst broad flower beds, resembling fairy tale cottages. In the center of the garden stood a pair of stone sculptures of antelopes, their heads raised high, spouting water into the air...
[18] Finally, the car arrived at the castle. Another elderly man greeted them at the entrance, a giant drawbridge stretching across the shimmering moat. At the other end of the bridge, a tall, iron-bolted oak door opened, revealing a short, well-dressed servant with a ruddy complexion and a broad smile. He bowed low, his face beaming with joy, as if he were being amused by a secret joke.
[19] The two visitors were startled, their eyes wide with surprise. They quickly got out of the car and hurried across the iron bridge.
[20] "Are you Mr. Bruno the Prosecutor and Officer Sam?" the round-bellied servant bowed deeply, a wide smile on his face, and led the two men into the 16th century.
[21] They entered a grand hall that was so expansive it commanded respect. The ceilings were adorned with massive beams, and armored knights guarded the ancient artifacts hanging on the walls. On the far wall, a giant, grotesque comic mask hung, its grin more unsettling than the Valhalla Hall of the Norse gods where the spirits of fallen warriors were honored; opposite it, a similarly sized tragic mask hung. Both were carved from ancient oak. Between the two masks, a colossal, intricately crafted iron chandelier hung from the ceiling, its giant candles casting a warm glow that seemed to have no connection to electricity.
[22] A door on the far wall opened, and an ancient-looking hunchbacked man entered, balding and sporting a full beard and wrinkled face. Bruno and Sam couldn't remember how many times they had exchanged bewildered glances. Sam muttered, "Why are all these old men here?"
[23] The hunchbacked man greeted them cheerfully, "Welcome to Hamlet Manor, gentlemen." His voice was peculiar, with a staccato rhythm and metallic undertones, as if he had never spoken before. Turning to the servant, he said, "Falstaff, there's nothing to worry about here."
[24] "Falstaff..." Bruno murmured, "It's impossible. He can't really be named Falstaff!"
[25] The hunchbacked man tugged at his beard. "Yes, sir, his real name is Jack Pinna, an actor. But Mr. Rene called him Falstaff... Come this way."
[26] The hunchbacked old man led them through the hall, towards the door he had just entered. He pressed a button on the wall, and the door silently slid open - it was an elevator! In a place that looked like an ancient palace, there was an elevator! Bruno and Sam shook their heads, following the hunchbacked old man into the elevator. After a while, the elevator gently stopped. Another small door opened, and the hunchbacked old man said, "We're here. This is Mr. Lane's living room."
[27] Majestic and elegant, only these two words can describe... Every item in the room exudes the charm of Elizabethan England. The materials visible are leather and oak, or oak and stone. The fireplace is twelve feet wide, with a patina of age and smoke, giving it an antique bronze glow. The fire is still burning quietly. Bruno's brown eyes lit up at the sight of the fire, feeling his body suddenly warm up. After all, the air outside is still a bit cold.
[28] Under the hunchbacked old man's guidance, the two men comfortably sat in the ancient armchairs, unable to help but look at each other again.
[29] The hunchbacked old man leaned against the wall, stroking his beard, and then his eyes lit up, and he spoke loudly, "Mr. Lane is here."
[30] The two men quickly jumped up from their chairs, and a tall, slender man stood at the door, staring at them intently. The hunchbacked old man bowed deeply, a strange smile appearing on his leather-like, weathered face. Bruno and Sam followed suit, bowing in unison.
[31] Mr. Rene slowly walked into the room, extending his white and powerful hand. "It's an honor to have you two here, please sit down."
[32] Bruno looked at the pair of very calm gray-green eyes and spoke, only to find that the eyes were staring intently at his lips. "Mr. Rene, thank you for taking the time to see me and Sam," Bruno stammered, "We... We don't know how to say it, but your mansion is truly amazing."
[33] "Mr. Bruno, at first glance, you may find it a bit amazing, but that's only because you look at it with a 20th-century perspective and with a certain impatience for modern life, which creates a wonderful sense of temporal displacement." Mr. Rene spoke calmly, just like his calm eyes, but Bruno felt that it was the most profound and thought-provoking voice he had ever heard, "However, after living here for a while, you will really grow to like it, just like me. One of my colleagues said that Hamlet Manor is a set, a large set combining this beautiful hillside, but for me personally, it is alive, breathing, and living, as if it were directly cut out of the most beautiful world of ancient England and placed in 20th-century New York... Quasi!"
[34] The hunchbacked old man walked over to Mr. Rene, who patted his hump. "Gentlemen, this is Quasi, my closest friend, and I can assure you that he is a master of makeup, and he has been in charge of my makeup for the past forty years."
[35] Quasi bowed to Mr. Rene again. In such simple words and actions, the two guests clearly felt an odd warmth: the two strange figures may look very different on the outside, but they have a thick, unbreakable emotional bond. Thus, Bruno and Sam spoke at the same time, and Mr. Rene's gaze jumped between their lips. After a while, Mr. Rene's face, which was previously expressionless, broke into a smile: "I'm sorry, you have to say one by one, my ears are completely deaf. Of course, I can only focus on one person's lips at a time—reading lips is a recent achievement that I am quite proud of."
[36] Bruno and Sam apologized hastily and returned to their seats. Rayne also pulled a similarly antique chair from the fireplace and sat down facing them. Sam noticed that Rayne had placed his chair between the guests and the fireplace, creating a barrier to block the light and not make the guests feel uncomfortable, while Rayne himself was now hidden in the shadows. Quasi had retired, and Sam could see him sitting in the farthest corner, like a brown statue.
[37] Bruno cleared his throat. "Disturbing Rayne, Inspector Sam and I feel very presumptuous. Thinking back on that telegram, we should never have sent it. Of course, if it weren't for your letter that solved the Cranmer case, we wouldn't dare be so bold."
[38] "Mr. Bruno, that's too kind. You're too modest." The clear and powerful voice came from Rayne's throne-like chair. "I did it, not because it was unprecedented, but because I remembered Edgar Allan Poe's series of letters to the New York newspapers, providing clues to the case of Mary Rogers. As for the Cranmer case, according to my analysis, the truth was easily confused with three unrelated facts, hindering the investigation. Unfortunately, the police were misled by these three facts. I believe you're here to discuss the Lestrade murder case?"
[39] "Rayne, you're absolutely right. You're a genius, and we know you're a busy man."
[40] "Not at all, Mr. Bruno. In such a significant drama, I'm always happy to play a small role and make time for it. It was only after I retired from the stage that I truly realized that life itself is the most dramatic. The stage is limited, and the creative space is limited. The characters in the play, as Morkowski commented on dreams, are 'just fanciful thoughts in the mind of a fool.' But the people in life are more real and substantial. They expand the drama with their emotional ups and downs, and they will never be 'as airy as nothing, more fleeting than the wind.'" The two visitors were completely mesmerized by the magic of Rayne's voice. "But people in life are more real and substantial. They expand the drama with their emotional ups and downs, and they will never be 'as airy as nothing, more fleeting than the wind.'"
[41] "I understand, Bruno said slowly. "Now that you've said it, I can fully understand."
[42] "Crime, especially violent crime, comes from a person's desire to control - it is the most exquisite crystallization of human life, and its ultimate form is murder. In my life, I have performed with the most outstanding brothers and sisters in this field,” Rein said sadly. "Mordicci, Edwin Booth, Ada Rehan, and all other brilliant actors have also performed in the most passionate drama. Now, I believe that if I have the opportunity to perform the real passion drama of life, I have the ability to contribute my unique talents."
[43] "I have killed countless people on stage. Before committing a crime, I always struggle with guilt and torment my conscience. I have also played some less noble roles, like Macbeth. I have also played Hamlet. However, like a child watching a simple spectacle for the first time, I now understand that the real world is full of Macbeths and Hamlets, an old saying but so true..."
[44] "I was like a puppet controlled by a master before, but now I am driven by a powerful force within me, and I want to control the strings in this drama that is greater than any fictional drama," Rein said, indicating his ears. "In fact, my physical disability helps me focus my energy. I can enter a silent, still world by closing my eyes and avoid any physical interference..."
[45] Sam seemed somewhat confused, as if he was caught in an emotion that did not align with his usual realistic nature. He blinked repeatedly, wondering if this was - he joked to himself - some form of hero worship.
[46] Bruno coughed twice, and Raine's eyes quickly fixed on Bruno's lips. "Oh, Mr. Raine, I'm afraid our little case doesn't—eh, it's really just an ordinary murder case—eh, it's not really suited to your—eh, your magnificent detective picture—"
[47] "It seems I haven't made myself clear," Raine's voice tinged with a smile. "An ordinary murder case, Mr. Bruno? But—really, do you think I'm looking for colorful, extraordinary murder cases?"
[48] "Well, anyway," Sam suddenly interjected, "whether ordinary or colorful, really, this is a quite challenging case, Mr. Bruno, I think you'll find it interesting, don't you know if you've seen the related reports in the newspaper?"
[49] "Yes, but the newspapers say it's not very clear, and there's not much valuable information. I hope to understand the case through undistorted information. Inspector, can you please tell me the case from beginning to end, without omitting any details or background information, no matter how irrelevant or unimportant it may seem? Simply put, tell me everything."
[50] Bruno and Sam exchanged glances. Then, Bruno nodded, and Sam's face grew tense. His already ugly face now looked even more grim, with a look of impending storm clouds.
[51] The towering walls around them began to blur, and the fire in the hearth dimmed, as if to match the atmosphere. The entire Hamlet Manor, including Raine, all the antiques, ancient times, and these ancient people, were now submerged in Sam's rough voice.
[52] Second Act
[53] Grant Hotel Suite
[54] September 4, Friday, 3:30 PM
[55] Last Friday afternoon—as recounted by Inspector Sam and supplemented by District Attorney Bruno—in the living room of a suite in the Grant Hotel, a building constructed of reinforced concrete at the intersection of 42nd Street and 8th Avenue, a man and a woman were intimately entwined.
[56] The man's name was Harry Longstreet, a tall middle-aged man whose robust physique had been ruined by years of alcohol and debauchery, with a sickly dark red complexion. He wore a plaid shirt. The woman's name was Theresa Brown, a Latin American actress with long black hair, dark eyes that shone brightly, a beautiful mouth, and a radiant aura. Longstreet's wet lips kissed Theresa, who hugged him tightly.
[57] "I really hope they don't come."
[58] "As long as this old man cares about you, right?" Longstreet let go, showing off his arm muscles like an athlete, "But they will come - they will definitely come. I called John DeWitt to get up, I swear to you, little beauty, he wouldn't dare to squat down."
[59] "Why do you want DeWitt's gang to come here if they don't want to come?"
[60] "I like to see the old buzzard look embarrassed. The old guy hates me, and I like it. Damn, I wish he'd go to hell."
[61] He roughly sets the woman down from his lap and walks over to the buffet table at the other end of the room, pouring himself a drink. The woman looks at him lazily, like a cat.
[62] "Sometimes," she says, "I can't figure you out, but it doesn't matter how you mess with him. It's your business, dear. Go ahead and drink up!"
[63] Longstreet grumbles and drains his glass in one gulp. As he raises his head, Theresa casually asks, "Is Mrs. DeWitt coming too?"
[64] He puts his whiskey glass back on the buffet table. "Why not? Just stop bringing up that woman, Theresa. I've told you a hundred times, I have nothing to do with that woman, never have."
[65] "I don't care about your entanglements," she laughs. "But you do seem like the type to try to seduce Mrs. DeWitt – and who else is coming?"
[66] He made a face. "A bunch, oh, God, I can't wait to see DeWitt's long face. And his friend, that Ehring guy who lives in Seengelwood - an old hag, always crying about his belly. He acts like his belly is always bothering him, sweetheart, unlike your dear Lansdtritt. And DeWitt's precious daughter, Jenny, will be here too. She hates me too, but her dad will make her come, especially since her boyfriend, Kit Lord, who looks like Frank Merriwell, will be here to stir things up. It's going to be a fantastic party."
[67] "Lord is a nice boy!"
[68] Lansdtritt glared, "Of course, a nice boy. Damn, an arrogant, meddling son of a bitch. Having him around the office all the time is unbearable. I should have kicked him out sooner... well, whatever. He sighed, "There's one more - it'll make you laugh. A Swiss cheese-loving guy," he chuckled uncomfortably, "called Louis Impraliere, I've told you about this guy, he's DeWitt's Swiss client... and of course, Mike Collins."
[69] The doorbell rang, and Cherise quickly got up to open the door.
[70] "Bolux, you old coot! Come in!"
[71] The visitor was a well-dressed old man with a dark complexion, combed hair, and a waxed mustache. He hugged Theresa intimately, and Lorrie, unsteadily, stood up and made a threatening sound in his throat. Theresa blushed and pushed away the visitor, smoothing her hair.
[72] "Do you remember my old Bollocks?" Her voice was happy, "Bollocks, the great Bollocks, the master of mind-reading this century. Come on, you two shake hands."
[73] Bollocks shook hands with Lorrie and promptly went to the table set with drinks. Lorrie shrugged and was about to sit back in his chair when the doorbell rang again, and he had to get up again. Theresa ran to the door and let in a group of people.
[74] The one leading was a middle-aged man with gray hair and a beard, looking a bit hesitant. Lorrie's face lit up when he saw the newcomer, and he strode over to greet him with a big step, as if each step brought out more passion in his heart. He firmly shook DeWitt's hand, and DeWitt blushed with pain and disgust. The two of them stood out in stark contrast in appearance: DeWitt looked indifferent and melancholy, as if he was always wavering between decision and hesitation; Lorrie was tall, robust, confident, arrogant, and domineering.
[75] It wasn't until Lorrie went to greet the people behind him that DeWitt was able to escape Lorrie's enthusiastic harassment.
[76] "Fern, it's so good to see you," Lestrade said to a Spanish woman whose beauty had faded and whose figure was beginning to spread. Underneath her makeup, she still had traces of her former beauty. Fern DeWitt was a petite brunette leaning on the tall, young blond Christopher Lord, who nodded coldly in greeting. Lestrade ignored Lord completely and shook hands with Ehring and the well-dressed Swiss man, Inperial.
[77] "Mike!" Lestrade rushed forward and patted the shoulder of the man who had just entered. Mike Collins was a strong Irishman with piggy eyes and a clear air of hostility. He responded with a couple of polite words, but his gaze darted around the room like a venomous snake. Lestrade grabbed his arm and looked him in the eye. "Listen, Mike, don't cause any trouble here," he warned sternly in a low voice. "I told DeWitt to handle things properly, so go over there, pour yourself a drink – be a good boy."
[78] Collins shook off Lestrade's hand and walked over to the table with the drinks.
[79] The waiter arrived, and the ice clinked in the amber-colored glasses. DeWitt and his group sat there awkwardly, politely but stiffly. DeWitt sat on the edge of his chair, pale and expressionless, mechanically sipping his drink and clenching the glass so tightly that his fingers turned white.
[80] Lestrade suddenly dramatically wrapped his arm around Cheryl and loudly announced, "Ladies and gentlemen, as you all know, today is a good day for my old Harry and for DeWitt-Lestrade and all our friends and family. Now, I am honored to introduce – the future Mrs. Lestrade!"
[81] There was a stir in the room. DeWitt stood up, a little awkwardly, and bowed to the actress before stiffly shaking hands and congratulating Lestrade; Immerial walked up and, like a soldier, stood with his feet together and kissed the actress's well-manicured fingers. DeWitt's wife, beside her husband, clutched her handkerchief and forced a smile. Burrowed at the sideboard, Boulux now stumbled over and tried to put his arm around Cherie's waist, but Lestrade pushed him away without apology. Boulux muttered some drunk words and then returned to the sideboard.
[82] The ladies admired the diamond engagement ring on the actress's left hand. At this point, several waiters brought in the cutlery...
[83] After a simple snack, Boulux turned on the tape recorder, and music played. Everyone danced as usual, except for Lestrade and Cherie, who were genuinely enjoying themselves. Lestrade was like a child, half-jokingly trying to put his arm around Jenny DeWitt, but the blond Lord intervened coldly and took Jenny away to dance, and Lestrade chuckled. Cherie stood by, smiling sweetly but clearly with storm clouds brewing.
[84] At 5:45, Lestrade turned off the tape recorder and happily announced, "I forgot to tell you, at my house in Seinegelwood, I've arranged a little dinner for you all as a surprise." He shouted, "Everyone, everyone has to come! You too, Mike, and you over there, Boulux, or whatever your name is - come along and show us your mind-reading trick." He looked at his watch like an owl, "It's still in time to catch the bus, come on, everyone!"
[85] DeWitt stammered an explanation that he had to attend a date that evening, and it was his treat. Lestrade got angry: "I said everyone has to come!" Immerial shrugged and still smiled; Lord looked down on Lestrade - when he looked at DeWitt, there was a hint of confusion in his eyes...
[86] At 5:50, the group left Cheryl's suite, leaving behind a mess of cups and plates. They took the elevator down to the hotel lobby. Lunsdorf asked the waiter for a newspaper and instructed him to call a taxi.
[87] Then, the group stepped out onto the sidewalk – standing at the entrance facing 42nd Street. The doorman blew his whistle frantically, trying to hail a taxi, but the lanes were filled with immovable vehicles. Dark clouds loomed overhead, with occasional flashes of lightning. The hot, dry weather of the past few weeks had evaporated all the moisture, and a torrential downpour was imminent.
[88] Indeed, it started to rain, and it came down hard and fast, as if the sky had cracked open. The hurried pedestrians and congested traffic were thrown into chaos.
[89] The doorman blew his whistle even louder, trying to hail a taxi, while giving Lunsdorf a rueful smile. The group ran to the corner of Eighth Avenue, seeking shelter under the awning of a jewelry store.
[90] DeWitt leaned in close to Lunsdorf, saying, "By the way, how about handling Weber's complaint as I suggested?" DeWitt handed him a letter.
[91] Lancelot had his right arm around Cheryl's waist, and he took out a silver eyeglass case from his left breast pocket, put on his glasses, and put the case back in his pocket. He took a printed letter from the envelope and casually read it, while DeWitt stood by, half-closing his eyes.
[92] Lancelot looked dismissive. "Don't bother with him!" He threw the letter back at DeWitt, who failed to catch it, and it fell onto the wet pavement. DeWitt's face turned pale as he bent down to pick up the letter. "Whether Weber is happy or furious, I've made up my mind, and this matter is over. Don't bother me with it anymore."
[93] Polux suddenly exclaimed, "The subway train is coming, let's take that."
[94] Amidst the bustling traffic, a red-headed lion-like subway train came rolling by. Lancelot took off his glasses, put them in the case, and carefully put the case back in his left breast pocket, leaving his left hand inside. Cheryl snuggled up to him again, and Lancelot waved his right hand. "Screw the taxi," he shouted, "Let's take this train."
[95] The subway train screeched to a halt, and a crowd of people, drenched like wet chickens, rushed towards the opened rear door. Lancelot and his group also struggled to enter the throng, pushing their way towards the entrance of the train. Cheryl still held onto Lancelot's left arm, while Lancelot's left hand remained in his left pocket.
[96] They climbed the steps, and the conductor shouted hoarsely, "Hurry up! Get on, get on!"
[97] The rain had soaked everyone's clothes.
[98] DeWitt was sandwiched between the bulky bodies of Ehren and Ingrilli, pushing his way forward. Ingrilli gallantly cleared a path for DeWitt's wife with a wink and a foreign accent, saying, "It's an honor to be part of such an unusual banquet in America."
[99] Act III
[100] 42nd Street Streetcar
[101] September 4th, Friday, 6:00 PM
[102] Now, they all crowded around the back door, almost suffocating in the damp, hot, foul air. As they passed the ticket taker's seat, they elbowed and pushed their way through. The towering Lansdtritte made his way into the carriage first, forcing Cherie aside and having to let go of his left arm to follow the rest of the group.
[103] The ticket taker shouted and pushed, trying to get the passengers into the car, and struggled to close the folding yellow double doors. A crowd of people stood at the back door, waving their change, but the ticket taker ignored them and focused on closing the doors and signaling the driver to start the car. Some people who couldn't get on board stood helplessly in the rain, drenched to the bone.
[104] Lansdtritte's body swayed with the jolts of the tram, his right hand waving a one-dollar bill over the heads of the other passengers to the ticket taker. The carriage was already stuffy, and despite all the windows being tightly closed to keep out the rain, the humidity inside was high, making it even more difficult to breathe.
[105] The ticket taker shouted and struggled to get the money from Lansdtritte's hand. The passengers pushed and shoved, making Lansdtritte roar like an enraged bear. Finally, he managed to get his change and made his way through the crowd to meet his companions. In the middle of the carriage, he found Cherie and the others. Cherie clung tightly to his right arm, and he held onto the handrail to balance himself.
[106] In the pouring rain, the electric car slowly made its way towards Ninth Avenue, inching forward with great difficulty through the chaotic traffic, its engine roaring loudly.
[107] Lansdorf reached into his pocket to retrieve his eyeglass case, and in that moment, he cursed and quickly withdrew his hand. The silver eyeglass case came out smoothly, but Lansdorf's left hand was bleeding from several cuts. He felt dizzy and his face began to contort as he struggled to breathe. "It must have been cut by something... Damn it, what could it be..." His breathing became labored. The electric car suddenly jolted to a stop, and everyone fell forward. Lansdorf instinctively grabbed the handrail with his left hand, and Cheri clung to his right arm. The electric car suddenly surged forward a few feet. Lansdorf wiped the bleeding area with a handkerchief, put it back in his pants pocket, took out his glasses from the case, and put the case back in his pocket. He took the unread newspaper from under his right armpit, as if he was going to open it—he seemed to be engulfed in a growing fog.
[108] The electric car stopped at Ninth Avenue, and the noisy passengers pounded on the closed door, but the ticket taker shook his head. The rain continued to fall, and the electric car slowly resumed its journey.
[109] Lansdorf suddenly let go of the handrail, and the unread newspaper fell to the ground. He pressed his hand to his forehead, gasping for breath and groaning in pain. Cheri held his right arm tightly, turning her head and looking around in panic...
[110] The electric car reached the intersection of Ninth and Tenth Avenues, still inching forward in the maze-like traffic, one step forward, one step back, one step forward, one step back.
[111] Lestrange gasped for breath, his body stiffening into spasms, his eyes wide with terror, and then, like a punctured balloon, he collapsed onto the lap of the young woman in front of him.
[112] The woman had black hair and dark eyes, with heavy makeup, and was quite attractive. She was chatting with her male companion, a tall, middle-aged man standing to Lestrange's left. Seeing the situation, he grabbed Lestrange's limp arm and shouted angrily, "Hey, get up! Do you think you're in the right place?"
[113] But Lestrange slid off the woman's lap and fell heavily to the floor.
[114] Theresa screamed.
[115] Suddenly, the entire car was silent, and then all the passengers on the tram began to stir, craning their necks to look over. The group traveling with Lestrange began to push their way over.
[116] "What's going on?"
[117] "It's Lestrade! He's collapsed!"
[118] "Drunk?"
[119] "Hey, look out – she's fainting!"
[120] Collins managed to catch Cherrie as she collapsed weakly.
[121] The heavily made-up young woman and her burly escort were truly alarmed, their faces pale and speechless. The woman jumped aside, clutching the man's arm, her face paling as she looked down at Lentz on the ground. "Oh my God," she suddenly shrieked, "someone do something! Look at his eyes! He - he…" She trembled violently, burying her face in her companion's chest.
[122] DeWitt stood aside, his hands clenched tightly. Ehren and Lord together lifted Lentz's heavy body onto the young woman's former seat, and the Italian man next to them immediately rose to help, laying Lentz down slumped in the chair. Lentz's eyes were now glazed over, his mouth slightly open, and he breathed weakly, with white foam beginning to appear at the corners of his mouth.
[123] This commotion spread throughout the car. After a loud command, all the passengers cooperatively moved to the sides, clearing a path for a burly police officer with an officer's stripes on his sleeve. The officer happened to be on this train, standing next to the driver's seat at the front.
[124] The officer roughly pushed through the crowd gathered around Lentz, bending down to examine the fallen Lentz. Lentz's body convulsed once more, and then there was no movement. The officer stood up, frowning. "Dead, looks like it," he said, suddenly looking at Lentz's left hand, which had several dried-up, swollen blood trails on the palm and fingers. "Looks like murder. You lot, stay back."
[125] The officer looked at the group of people who had traveled with Lentz with a suspicious gaze, and they instinctively huddled together, as if protecting each other from an enemy.
[126] The officer shouted, "No one is allowed to get off the car - understood? Stay put! You!” He spoke imperiously to the driver, "The car is not allowed to move, go back to your driver's seat, keep the windows closed - got it?” The driver obeyed and left. "And you, ticket seller, run to the corner of 10th Avenue and find the traffic police on duty. He must immediately contact the district police and, of course, contact Inspector Sam at headquarters. All noted? I'll open the door. I won't allow anyone to sneak away while the door is open."
[127] The officer personally led the ticket seller to the rear door, opened it with his own hand, and as soon as the ticket seller ran into the rain, he closed the door. The ticket seller ran quickly towards 10th Avenue. The officer ordered a tall, ugly-looking man, "You take care of it, no one is allowed to touch the door, understood?"
[128] The man felt honored and nodded repeatedly. The officer returned to Lestrade's corpse. There was a long line of immobile cars behind the trolley, with continuous honking and cursing, and the passengers in the car could only watch helplessly as a group of people outside.
[129] "Wait!" The officer went to open the rear door himself and let in a traffic police officer. The officer saluted and said, "Officer, I'm the on-duty officer on 9th Avenue. I heard there was an incident here. Do you need any help?"
[130] "It seems that someone was killed on the car," the officer closed the door, giving the tall man a meaningful look, whose reaction was perfectly in sync, "Of course I need your help. I've already sent someone to notify Inspector Sam at the district and headquarters. You go to the front door and make sure no one gets on or off the car. Keep an eye on the front door."
[131] The two of them walked forward. The officer returned to the spot where Lengstritter's body lay, while the traffic policeman made his way to the driver's seat.
[132] The officer crossed his arms and looked at the body. "Who was the first to find it?” He shouted loudly, "Who was sitting in these two seats?” – A young lady and a middle-aged Italian man spoke at the same time – "One at a time, you first. What's your name?”
[133] The young lady was still trembling. "Emily Jervis, I – I'm a stenographer, heading home from work. This man – he fell on my lap just now, so I got up and gave him my seat.”
[134] "And you, sir?”
[135] "My name is Antonio Fontana. I don't know anything. This man fell, so I got up and gave him my seat.” The Italian man replied.
[136] DeWitt stepped forward, now appearing calm. "Officer, I have a clearer understanding of the situation. This man is Harry Lunsford, my partner. We were on our way to a dinner party -"
[137] "Dinner party? Oh?" The officer glanced disapprovingly at the crowd. "A dinner party, everyone eating and drinking together, a very pleasant and harmonious gathering, right? Sir, I suggest you save your breath and tell everything to Sam Inspector later. The ticket seller and one of our police colleagues have arrived."
[138] The officer quickly squeezed his way to the rear door, where the ticket seller was pounding on the door and water was pouring down from his hat brim like a waterfall. A policeman stood beside him. The officer opened the door himself, and as soon as the two got on, he shut the door.
[139] The police officer saluted: "Ten Avenue Patrolman Moore reporting."
[140] "Good, I'm Inspector Duffy from the Eighteenth District." The officer spoke sternly. "Did you notify the headquarters?"
[141] "Yes, we contacted them, and Inspector Sam and the district's police officers should be here any moment. The inspector said to drive straight to the Green Line garage at Forty-second Street and Twelfth Avenue. He'll meet us there. The inspector also said that no one is to touch the body. I also called an ambulance."
[142] "He won't need an ambulance. Officer Moor, stay at the back door and make sure no one leaves the car."
[143] Inspector Duffy turned to the tall, ugly man who had been acting as a lookout. "Fellow, did anyone try to leave? Was the door opened?"
[144] "No," several passengers answered in unison.
[145] Inspector Duffy then walked over to the driver's seat. "Hey, driver! Get this car to the terminal and park it in the Green Line garage. Now!"
[146] The red-faced Irish young driver whispered, "Officer, but that's not our garage. This is the third avenue line, we don't—"
[147] "Shut up! Drive when I tell you to. And you, officer on Ninth Avenue, sound your siren and make the car pull over. What's your name?"
[148] "Westfield, eight-six-three-eight."
[149] "Alright, Westfield, you also keep an eye on the front door. Has anyone tried to get off?"
[150] "No, sir."
[151] "Driver, did anyone try to get off before Westfield came?"
[152] "No."
[153] "Good, let's go!"
[154] The trolley moved slowly, Duffy returning to the body. Theresa was crying, and Bollocks gently patted her hand to comfort her. DeWitt stood stiffly in front of Longstreet, like a sentry guarding the corpse.
[155] The trolley entered the empty New York Green Line garage, echoing through the space. A group of plainclothes police officers stood silently, watching the car pull in, as the rain continued to pour outside.
[156] With his gray hair, square jaw, and sharp gray eyes set in a face that was ugly yet almost comical, this giant of a man slapped the back of the car. Guard Morro shouted for Duffy, who quickly recognized the unmistakable figure of Inspector Sam, and hastily pulled the interior lever, opening both doors. Inspector Sam got in, motioning for Duffy to close the doors, and gestured to the police officers waiting outside, before walking down the aisle.
[157] "Well done," Sam seemed to casually glance at the corpse. "Duffy, what happened?"
[158] Duffy whispered something in Inspector Sam's ear, who still looked indifferent. "Longstreet, huh? The stockbroker... uh, who called Emily Jevitt?"
[159] The young woman, accompanied by a middle-aged escort, approached, with the escort glaring at Sam.
[160] "Miss, did you notice anything unusual about this person before he fell?"
[161] "Yes," the young lady said excitedly, "I saw his hand reach into his pocket for his glasses. His hand must have been hurt by something, bleeding when he pulled it out."
[162] "Which pocket?"
[163] "His left pocket of his coat."
[164] "When did this happen?"
[165] "Well, just a moment after the car stopped in front of the ninth avenue."
[166] "How long ago was that?"
[167] "Uh, the car took off again and got here in about five minutes, and he fell down about five minutes after the car started, uh, should have been only a few minutes - two to three minutes - from when he hurt his hand to when he fell down."
[168] "Less than fifteen minutes ago, right? Left pocket, is it? Sam crouched down, fished out a flashlight from his hip pocket, and ripped open the left pocket of the dead man's coat with it, shining the flashlight inside. He grunted in satisfaction, put down the flashlight, and used a large letter opener to carefully cut the stitching along the side of the pocket. With the flashlight illuminating, he could clearly see two items shining.
[169] Sam did not rush to take the items out, but kept them as they were to examine further. One was a silver eyeglass case, which Sam studied for a while, and the glasses that were inside, now askew on the dead man's purplish-black nose.
[170] Sam refocused on the pocket. The other item was a peculiar little thing, a small, round cork stopper, about an inch in diameter, with at least fifty ordinary sewing needles inserted into it, each needle protruding about a quarter of an inch from the cork, forming a delicate, one-and-a-half-inch murder weapon, with each needle tip having a reddish-brown substance. Sam examined the cork stopper with a knife and fork, noting that the needle tips on the other side of the cork were also coated with the reddish-brown substance - a thick, tar-like substance - which he took a whiff of. "It smells like a moldy cigarette," he said, turning to Duffy.
[171] Duffy stood behind him, peering over his shoulder. "I'd rather go a whole year without pay than touch that thing."
[172] Sam stood up, feeling his pocket, taking out a pair of tweezers and a pack of cigarettes, emptying the cigarettes back into his pocket. He skillfully used the tweezers to pick up the needles on the cork, carefully taking the cork out of Langstroth's pocket and placing it into the now-empty cigarette box. Then, he whispered something to Duffy, who immediately walked away and returned with what Sam wanted - a newspaper. Sam wrapped the cigarette box in six sheets of newspaper and handed it over to Duffy.
[173] "Detective, this is just like dynamite," Sam grinned, standing up, "treat it like dynamite, and you'll be fine with it."
[174] Duffy stiffened at the mention of dynamite, holding the object at arm's length, as if that would make it safer.
[175] Sam paid no attention to the eager glances of Langstroth's companions, walking to the front door and asking the driver and passengers there, then turning around to the back door and asking the ticket seller and passengers there, finally returning to Langstroth's body. He told Duffy, "Fortunately, no one has gotten off the bus since that guy got on at Eighth Avenue... Okay, let Moore and Westfeldt go back, we have enough people here. And have the outside people set up a perimeter and evacuate all the passengers."
[176] Daffy still held the deadly package as if it were a sacred object, exiting the bus through the back door. The bus driver promptly closed the door as soon as Daffy left.
[177] Five minutes later, the back door opened again. From the iron steps outside the back door to the stairwell leading to the garage, police and detectives stood in two rows. Sam instructed the group of people traveling with Langstritte to disembark first. They formed a single-file line and silently exited the bus, directly led to the garage's second-floor reception room, where the door was promptly closed. A police officer stood guard outside, and two detectives were stationed inside to monitor the group.
[178] After the group of Langstritte's companions disembarked, Sam ordered all other passengers to leave the bus. They too formed a single-file line, looking like a defeated army, passing through the same channel formed by two rows of police officers, and arriving at another spacious restroom on the second floor. Six detectives were stationed inside to supervise them.
[179] Now, the bus was empty except for Sam – alone with the deceased lying flat on the seat. He quietly observed the distorted face under the harsh interior lighting. The dead man's eyes remained open, with his pupils abnormally dilated. The sound of an ambulance siren outside caught Sam's attention. Two young men in white clothes rushed out, followed by a short, plump man wearing an outdated gold-framed pair of glasses and a gray, outdated small cap – the brim of which was flipped up, and the front one hung loosely.
[180] Sam pulled the back door handle and leaned out. "Here, Dr. Schilling."
[181] The short, stout man was the New York area's forensic expert, who breathlessly climbed onto the trolley, followed by his two assistants. Dr. Shilling bent over to examine the corpse while Sam reached into the left pocket of the body and retrieved the silver case for glasses.
[182] Dr. Shilling stood up. "Inspector, where do you want me to handle this stiff?"
[183] "The second floor," Sam winked, "take him up to the second-floor reception room and let him continue his party with his friends," he added coldly, "isn't that fun?"
[184] As Dr. Shilling instructed his assistants to carry the body, Sam exited the car and called over an officer: "Lieutenant, I want you to do something for me. I want you to thoroughly search this car from top to bottom, leaving no scrap behind. Collect everything. Then, follow the path that Langstroth's group and the other passengers took through the security zone and conduct a thorough search as well. I want to be absolutely sure that no one has thrown anything away. Chief Pibody, is that clear? A great task, isn't it?"
[185] Chief Pibody smiled and went to carry out the task. Sam then called out, "Officer, come with me." Daffy still held the package wrapped in newspaper, smiling weakly and silently following Sam up the stairs to the second floor.
[186] Act Four
[187] Garage Reception Room
[188] September 4, Friday, 6:40 PM
[189] The reception room on the second floor of the garage is a large, empty, and gloomy space with long chairs lining the walls. The group of people who attended Lengstrasse's party sit scattered around the room, with a variety of sad and tense expressions, but none of them speak.
[190] Sergeant Sam and Detective Duffy enter first, followed by Dr. Sillinger, who directs two assistants to carry the body in on a stretcher. Dr. Sillinger sets up a screen, and the three doctors begin their examination behind it. Everyone else remains silent, and, as if following an unspoken command, no one looks at the screen. After a long moment, Theresa leans on Detective Pollock's trembling shoulder and quietly begins to cry.
[191] Inspector Sam crosses his powerful hands behind his back and looks at the crowd with a calm, almost indifferent gaze.
[192] "Now, there are no outsiders in this room," the inspector says casually. "We can all speak freely. I understand that everyone is quite upset, but not so much that you can't answer a few simple questions." - everyone looks at Inspector Sam like a schoolchild - "Detective, " the inspector turns to Detective Duffy, "who can confirm that the deceased is Harry Lestrade?"

[195] "Please state your name first."

[198] DeWitt shook his head. "Has anyone else seen or heard of it?" The others shook their heads, indicating they did not know.
[199] "Good, Mr. DeWitt, now listen carefully. What I am about to say is all true. When you, Lunsdorf, and the others were standing under the canopy at the intersection of 42nd Street and 8th Avenue, you handed Lunsdorf a letter. He reached into his left pocket with his left hand, took out his glasses case, took out his glasses, and put the glasses case back in his pocket. Did you notice anything unusual about his left hand at that time? Did he scream or quickly withdraw his hand?"

Inspector Sam noticed the surprised glances from several people in the room, DeWitt's behavior seemed to pique their interest. "The deceased is my partner, our company is DeWitt-Lunsdorf Securities, located on Wall Street."
[201] DeWitt shook his head. "No, I haven't seen it before, or heard of it."
[202] Sam turned to the others: "Has anyone noticed anything unusual at the time?"
[203] Theresa said with a sobbing voice, "Nothing. I was standing right next to him. If he was pricked by the needles, I would have felt it."
[204] "Good, Mr. DeWitt, when Langstroth finished reading the letter, he reached into his pocket for the fourth time and put the glasses back in the case, and then—this is the fourth time—reached into his pocket again, put the glasses back in the case, and in this last time, did he call out or show any signs of being pricked by the needles?"
[205] "I swear there was none," DeWitt answered. "No cry or possible signs."
[206] Everyone nodded in agreement.
[207] Sam leaned forward slightly. "Miss Brown," he said to the actress, "Mr. Dewitt says that after Mr. Longstreet gave you the letter, you and Mr. Longstreet ran together to the tram, and you held onto his left arm until you got on the tram, is that true?"
[208] "Yes," she shivered slightly, "I was pushed around, holding onto his left arm, and he—his left hand was still in his pocket. We kept like that until we squeezed onto the tram and came up to the ticket collector."
[209] "Then, before the ticket collector, did you see his hand come out—his left hand?"
[210] "Yes. He reached out to find change in his waistcoat pocket, but he didn't find any, that was when we first squeezed onto the tram."
[211] "Was his hand okay—no punctures, no bleeding?"
[212] "Yes."
[213] "Mr. DeWitt, can I see the letter you gave to Lestrade?"
[214] DeWitt took the muddy envelope from his chest pocket and handed it to Sam. Sam began to read the letter—it was a complaint letter written by a client named Webber. Webber complained that they were supposed to sell his stocks at a certain time and price, but DeWitt-Lestrade did not execute it, resulting in significant losses for him. The letter emphasized that this was clearly due to negligence on the part of the brokerage firm and that they should be responsible for compensation. Sam silently handed the letter back to DeWitt.
[215] "So it seems quite clear," Sam concluded, "in other words—"
[216] "The cork," DeWitt continued calmly, "must have been put in his pocket after he got on the car."
[217] Sam said sarcastically, "Yes, during the time he was waiting for the car, he put his hand in his pocket four times; when everyone ran towards the tram, you saw Miss Brown standing close to his left side, and Langtree's left hand remained in the problematic left pocket. If there was anything wrong, you and Miss Brown would have noticed. Moreover, after getting on the tram, Miss Brown saw that his left hand was perfectly fine. In short, the cork with the needle was not in his pocket before Langtree got on the tram."
[218] Sam stroked his chin, pondered for a moment, and then shook his head, pacing back and forth in front of everyone, asking everyone about their distance from Langtree and their positions on the tram. He found that due to the swaying of the tram and the constant movement of other passengers, everyone was scattered. Sam did not show any disappointment on his face.
[219] "Miss Brown, why did Langtree take out his glasses on the tram?"
[220] "I think he wanted to read the newspaper." Cherise's voice was weak.

[224] "But he put on his glasses and read the stock market index, right?"

[233] DeWitt turned to look at Collins, looking incredulous. Sam immediately asked him, "Do you know about this transaction, Mr. DeWitt?"

[238] "Yes." The voice was angry.

[240] "At the branch office in Times Square. It was not long after noon."

[243] "You didn't answer my question."
[244] "Alright—no, no arguing."

[246] "Give me that thing – the cork, or whatever it is, that they told me downstairs, please, officer," Dr. Slinn said.
[247] Sam nodded at Detective Duffy, who handed over a package to Dr. Slinn, who took it, humming a tune, and disappeared behind the screen again.
[248] Cheryl stood up now, her eyes blazing with anger, her face twisted, looking like the Medusa of nightmares. She rushed to DeWitt, grabbed his collar, and shouted at his pale face: "You killed him, you did it. You hated him, you killed him!"
[249] The men in the room stood up nervously, and Sam and Duffy quickly pulled the screaming Cheryl away. I just know he hated Harry, hated him...Harry always said that -" Sam snorted, straightened up, and gave Detective Duffy a nod. Duffy gestured to the recorder, and the other detectives waiting outside entered the room. Bollux continued to chant his mind-reading spell, trying to calm Cherise down. Sam announced, "Everyone wait a moment, I'll be back soon." He then led the recorder, Jonathan, out of the room.
[252] Act 5
[253] Garage Restroom
[254] September 4th, Friday, 7:30 PM
[255] Detective Sam walked directly into the garage's restroom. He faced a ridiculous scene - the men and women inside were standing, sitting, sitting uneasily, and arguing. The room was filled with impatience, anxiety, and dissatisfaction. Sam looked at the on-duty detective, stomped his foot, and tried to get everyone's attention. Indeed, everyone rushed over, and the sounds of panting, complaining, protesting, questioning, cursing, and shouting filled the air.
[256] "Everyone back up!" Sam bellowed in his most commanding voice. "Now, please cooperate, don't complain, don't question, don't protest. The better you cooperate, the sooner you'll be home."
[257] "Miss Jevitt, you first. Did you see anyone put something in the victim's pocket – I mean when he was standing in front of you?"
[258] "I was chatting with my companions," the young woman said, licking her lips, "and it was so hot inside the car..."
[259] Sam couldn't help but roar, "Answer my question, yes or no?"
[260] "No, I didn't see anything."
[261] "Would you have noticed if someone put something into his pocket?"
[262] "I don't think so, I was talking to my friend..."
[263] Sam immediately turned to the burly man with gray hair and a cold, almost menacing face - Robert Clarke, an accountant. He answered that he had just been standing next to Langstroth when he fell, and didn't feel anything unusual. Clarke's expression of grimness suddenly disappeared, and he looked pale with anxiety, his mouth not quite under control, making him seem a bit comical.
[264] The middle-aged Italian man, Antonio Fontana - a burly, bearded barber - said he had just come home from the barbershop and had been buried in an Italian newspaper the whole time, unable to provide any clues.
[265] The next person questioned was the ticket collector, Charles Wood, badge number 2101, who had been serving on the Third Avenue subway for five years. He was tall, with red hair, and around fifty years old. Wood remembered the victim's appearance and recalled that he had boarded with a group of people at Eighth Avenue. He also remembered that the victim had handed him a one-dollar bill and bought ten tickets.
[266] "Wood, was there anything unusual about the group when they got on the bus?"
[267] "No, the bus was packed, and I was busy closing the door and collecting money."
[268] "Have you ever seen the victim before?"
[269] "Yes, he often took this bus at this time, for years."
[270] "Do you know his name?"
[271] "No."
[272] "So, do you know any of the people he was with?"
[273] "Yes, there was a short, gray-haired man. I've seen him with the victim on the bus."
[274] "Do you know his name?"
[275] "No."

[277] "No, sir. The car was full, so I didn't open the door at the next stop, Eighth Avenue corner. No one got on, and no one got off from the back. But I don't know if anyone got off from the front door. You'd have to ask my partner, Guinness. He's the driver, and he should know."
[278] Sam found the driver, Guinness, a broad-shouldered Irishman with the badge number 409. Guinness said he'd been driving this route for eight years and had never seen the victim. He thought for a moment and then added, "I suppose, I don't face the passengers like Charles does."
[279] "Are you sure you've never seen him?"
[280] "Well, uh, it's like, I think, I think I might have seen him once."
[281] "Did anyone get off from the front door after the Eighth Avenue stop?"
[282] "I didn't even open the door, sir. You should know, most passengers on this route go from the starting station to the end station and then take the ferry to New Jersey, where there are a lot of companies. And Detective Duffy can vouch for what I said, he was standing right next to me - heading home. He's a detective, after all! He was on the car, which is great."
[283] Sam frowned, furrowing his brow as he listened, but it was a thoughtful frown with a hint of excitement. "Okay, no problem. After the Eighth Avenue stop, the doors haven't been opened again, either the front or the back, right?"
[284] "That's correct."
[285] "Good, thank you. " The sergeant asked other passengers, but it seemed that no one noticed anything running into Longstreet's pocket, and there were no suspicious signs. Two passengers mumbled something, but it was clear that they were just being overly enthusiastic and making idle guesses. Experienced Sam ignored them and instructed Jonas to record the names and addresses of these people.
[286] At this point, Lieutenant Pibody came in, panting, carrying a sack full of miscellaneous items.
[287] "Did you find anything?" Sam asked.
[288] "Just a bunch of junk, take a look!" Pibody dropped the sack on the floor, containing scraps of paper, torn newspapers, empty cigarette packs, broken pencil tips, lit matchsticks, flattened chocolate bars, and two old timetables – all garbage with no trace of cork or needles, or anything related to cork or needles.
[289] "We searched the whole car, from top to bottom, almost using a microscope. So, if anyone brought something on board, I think it's still on them."
[290] Sam's gray eyes lit up. He was the most efficient inspector in the New York Police Department, relying on his extraordinary energy, quick reflexes, wealth of common sense, and authoritative voice to climb the ranks from the bottom to his current position. He was meticulous in his investigations, never compromising, and made decisions promptly and without hesitation...
[291] "Then there's only one thing left to do," he said. "Let's start patting down everyone in the house. Look for corks, needles, and anything else that looks out of place or suspicious. If anyone resists, give them a good beating. Let's get started!"
[292] "What are we patting down for?"
[293] "Corks, needles, and anything else that doesn't belong. If anyone objects, hit them. Let's go!"
[294] Pibody smiled and went out, returning with six male and two female police officers. He jumped on the chair and shouted, "Everyone, listen up! Line up, ladies on one side, gentlemen on the other, and keep your mouths shut. Anyone who wants to go home early, do it quickly."
[295] For the next fifteen minutes, Sam the detective stood with his back against the wall, cigarette hanging from his lips, coldly watching the farce unfold. The female officers searched the guests with firm hands, patting down pockets, checking bags, hats, and shoes, while the offended ladies let out shrill shrieks. The men generally maintained their composure, acting like a quiet herd of sheep. After the pat-downs, Jonathan noted down the names, companies, and addresses of everyone. Sam didn't participate in the pat-downs, but his eagle-eyed gaze scrutinized every single person. He stopped a man, a small, pale office worker dressed in an old coat. Sam asked him to step aside and remove the coat - it was a waterproof field jacket. The man turned pale with fear. Sam examined it thoroughly, said nothing, and returned it to him. The man left, almost running away.
[296] Soon, everyone had left.
[297] "Nothing," Pibody said, disappointed.
[298] "Search the room again."
[299] Pibody led his team in another thorough search, including under the chairs and in the corners. All the clutter was quickly gathered together. Sam squatted on top of the garbage pile that had been dumped from the burlap sack, carefully sifting through it with his fingers.
[300] Finally, he looked at Pibody, shrugged, and quickly left the break room.
[301] Act Six
[302] Hamlet's Manor
[303] September 8, Tuesday, 11:20 AM
[304] "Mr. Reen, I'll just add a few more details," said the prosecutor Bruno, interrupting, "most of which have been confirmed by our investigation. But to be honest, most of the information seems unimportant and not worth much consideration..."
[305] "My dear Mr. Bruno," said Reen, "nothing is unimportant, it's such a boring and yet so true statement! Anyway, Sam's narration was quite good. He sat back in his large chair, stretching his long legs towards the fireplace. "Let's take a break, Sergeant, and continue in a moment."
[306] The firelight flickered. Despite being shrouded in shadows, the two men clearly saw Raine close his eyes peacefully, his hands gently clasped on his knees. His fair, pleasant face was very serene. For a moment, they seemed to be in a peaceful room from another era, with only the silent, looming walls around them.
[307] Suddenly, a soft rustling sound came from the dark corner, like the sound of dried parchment, startling Bruno and Sam - it was Quasi, the hunchbacked old man, who had inexplicably chuckled softly.
[308] Bruno and Sam exchanged glances. At this point, Raine's calm, gentle, and trained voice spoke again.
[309] "Sergeant Sam," he said, "There's one thing I'm not quite clear about."
[310] "Please, Mr. Raine."
[311] "Based on what you just said, it was raining when the car was between Seventh and Eighth Avenues, so when the Longstreet party boarded at Eighth Avenue, I remember you saying that the windows were closed. Do you mean every window was closed?"
[312] Inspector Sam's rugged face showed a hint of suspicion. "Oh, of course, without a doubt, every window, Detective Duffy was certain."
[313] "That's good," the soft voice continued. "So, has any window been opened since then?"
[314] "Absolutely not. In fact, as the rain started falling, the windows were tightly closed the whole time the car was driving, so they were shut every second."
[315] "Excellent, excellent," the deep-set eyes under the gray eyebrows gleamed. "Please continue!"
[316] Scene 7
[317] Garage Reception Room
[318] September 4th, Friday, 8:05 PM
[319] Detective Sam said that the case took a dramatic turn after all other passengers left the car.
[320] Detective Sam returned to the upstairs reception room, where the Langstroth group was quietly waiting. Mr. Firpo Leary, the gentlemanly gentleman, stood up, snapped his heels together, and bowed to Detective Sam in standard military etiquette.
[321] "Dear Sergeant," he said with utmost sincerity, "I'm afraid it's quite presumptuous of me, but I think everyone might benefit from some sustenance, whether they have an appetite or not. Would you mind preparing some food, at least for the ladies in the room?"
[322] Sam glanced around the group. Mrs. DeWitt sat motionless, half-closed eyes; Jenny leaned on her boyfriend, Lord, both with pale faces; DeWitt and Ehrn whispered to each other; Pollock folded his hands on his knees and leaned in to whisper in Theresa's ear; Theresa frowned and gritted her teeth, losing all her elegance; Collins covered his face with his hands.
[323] "Sure, Mr. Verrier. Dick, go downstairs and get something to eat for everyone."
[324] A detective took the money from Mr. Verrier's hand and left the room. The Swissman completed his task to everyone's satisfaction and sat back down contentedly.
[325] "Doctor, how did it go?"
[326] Dr. Schilling appeared from behind the screen, putting on his coat and placing the tattered cap on his bald head. He motioned for Sam to come over. The two walked around the screen to the corpse, where a young assistant sat next to the body, filling out a report while another whistled and trimmed his nails.
[327] "This one," Dr. Schilling said cheerfully, "is a very nice touch, very nice indeed. The cause of death is respiratory paralysis, but that's not the interesting part." He counted off on his short, stubby fingers, "First, let's talk about the poison."
[328] "That's why I've been smelling such a strong odor of tobacco," Sam murmured.
[329] "Yes, nicotine is a colorless, odorless oily liquid, but when it's diluted in water or left in the air for too long, it turns dark brown and has a strong tobacco smell. I can guarantee that this is the cause of death. Of course, we'll still have to perform an autopsy to check for any other fatal causes. The poison entered the body directly - there are twenty-one puncture wounds on the fingers, and nicotine flowed directly into the bloodstream from there. I estimate that the victim died within a few minutes, thanks to their long-term smoking and strong resistance to nicotine.
[330] "Second, let's talk about the weapon." He counted off on his second short, stubby finger, "It should be added to your police museum. Inspector, look, so ordinary, so simple, so peculiar, and most importantly, so deadly - only a genius could have thought of it."
[331] "Third, about the possible source of the poison." The third finger was counted off, "Unless these nicotine are obtained through proper channels, you'll have a hard time tracing them, my friend. Of course, pure nicotine is not easy to buy, and if I were the killer, I wouldn't be so foolish as to go to the pharmacy. The content of nicotine in ordinary cigarettes is about 4%, of course, you can distill these nicotine from a large amount of tobacco. But how do you track down this amateur nicotine manufacturer? Another more convenient way is to buy a can—" Dr. Shilling mentioned the name of a commonly used insecticide, "Things become easier, this insecticide contains 35% nicotine, simply heating it and you have the same concentration of nicotine as on the needle."
[332] "We still need to check the normal channels," Sam's face grew serious, "How long does it take for the toxicity to set in?"
[333] Dr. Shilling pursed his lips. "Generally, it takes a few seconds, but if the concentration of nicotine is not enough, and Lestrade has been smoking for years, it may take about three minutes, and that's the actual situation."
[334] "Okay, I think the poison is nicotine. Are there any other discoveries?"
[335] "Inspector, I'm not a particularly demanding person, but this person's health condition is just too poor," Dr. Shilling replied, "As for the details, I'll tell you after the autopsy—I'll start tomorrow. There's nothing else here, I'll have someone take this lying gentleman away, the car is waiting outside."
[336] Inspector Sam put the weapon back in the cigarette box, wrapped it in newspaper, and walked back to the group of people who couldn't have their party.
[337] He handed the weapon over to Detective Duffy. Two young forensic assistants carried the body covered with a blanket, and Dr. Sheldon followed behind, walking briskly.
[338] The room fell silent again as the body was being taken away.
[339] The detective in charge of finding food successfully completed his task. A group of people mechanically chewed on sandwiches and sipped coffee.
[340] Inspector Sam gestured to DeWitt. "You are Langstroth's partner, and you may be the best person to talk about his habits. Mr. DeWitt, the ticket agent said he often saw Langstroth taking that train, what do you think?"
[341] "Langtry's daily routine is almost set in stone," DeWitt grimaced. "Especially his working hours. To be honest, he gets impatient with tasks that take time and effort and usually leaves them to me. Our headquarters are on Wall Street, but after the stock market closes, we usually go back to our branch office in Times Square and then back to West Englewood. Langtry always leaves by 6 pm and takes the same train to New Jersey. That's probably why we ended the banquet early today, to catch that train. That's why we took that train."
[342] "I understand you also take that train often?"
[343] "Yes, if I don't stay late at the office, I usually take the same train back to West Englewood with Langtry."
[344] Chief Sam sighed. "Why don't you two just drive yourselves to work?"
[345] DeWitt chuckled bitterly. "The traffic in New York is terrible, and we leave our cars at the West Englewood station."
[346] "Is Lestrade like that in other aspects too - doing the same thing at the same time?"
[347] "Very much so, Inspector. Especially in small things. Despite his carefree private life, he reads the same newspaper every day, reads the stock market closing reports on the same train to the ferry, just like I told you. And he wears the same style of clothes to work, smokes the same brand of cigarettes and cigars - he's a heavy smoker - yes, he follows a fixed pattern in most aspects of his life, " Dewey said, his eyes turning cold, "even the time he returns to the office after lunch is fixed."
[348] Inspector Sam glanced at Dewey and lit a cigarette. "Does Lestrade wear glasses when he reads?"
[349] "He does, especially when doing fine work. Basically, he's a vain person who thinks glasses detract from his appearance, so he doesn't wear them in public or social situations unless necessary. However, he still needs them for reading, both indoors and outdoors."
[350] Inspector Sam put a friendly hand on Dewey's thin shoulder. "Mr. Dewey, let's be honest about this. You just heard Miss Brown accuse you of killing Lestrade, of course, that's just nonsense, but she kept emphasizing your hatred for Lestrade. Is that true?"
[351] DeWitt moved, and Sam's large hand slipped off his shoulder. DeWitt said coldly, "If you want to be honest, I can tell you that I absolutely did not harm my partner."
[352] Sam looked straight into DeWitt's clear eyes for a long time before shrugging his shoulders and turning to the others, "Everyone here, please come to the DeWitt-Lundstrum office at Times Square at 9:00 am tomorrow. We have some further questions we would like to ask everyone. All gentlemen and ladies, everyone must be present."
[353] The crowd's faces were all dejected, and DeWitt was especially angry, cursing. Sam smiled and said, "How can we be sure that everyone here is clean and doesn't have anything they shouldn't have?"
[354] So, the body search that had been performed in the lounge was repeated under Sam's watchful eye. The men looked very uncomfortable, and the women turned red with anger. DeWitt's wife, who had been silent for hours, finally broke her silence, spewing a string of Spanish from her broad chest. Sam raised his eyebrows and waved his hand to the female officer conducting the search, telling her to continue.
[355] After the search, everyone lined up and headed for the door. Jonas stood at the door, calling out, "Please leave your names and addresses."
[356] Duffy was somewhat disappointed: "Nothing, sir, no corkscrews or needles, not even a whiff of fish.”
[357] Sam stood rigidly in the center of the room, frowning and biting his lips. "Search the room!" he ordered gruffly.
[358] The detectives began searching the entire room.
[359] Sam left the garage with his men, still frowning.
[360] Act Eight
[361] DeWitt-Lestrade Securities
[362] September 5th, Saturday, 9:00 AM
[363] Saturday morning, despite the turmoil within, appeared calm on the surface. As Sam Inspector strode into the DeWitt-Lestrade Securities office, the employees and customers were startled by his sudden appearance, but quickly resumed their work. Sam's men arrived on the scene, being careful not to interfere with the company's normal operations, quietly walking around and observing.
[364] In the office marked "John O. DeWitt," the group of people from last night were gathered, under the watchful eye of the alert Pibody sub-chief. The next office, indicated by a glass sign reading "Harry Lestrade," had Duffy's massive deep blue silhouette behind it.
[365] Sam looked at the crowd without emotion, giving a curt nod, and led Jonas to Lestrade's office. Inside, he saw a young woman sitting nervously at the front of the chair - a tall, well-dressed, slightly tanned woman, very pretty but a bit tacky.

[367] "I suppose you're Lestrade's personal secretary?"
[368] "Yes, my name is Pratt, Anna Pratt. I've been Lestrade's personal secretary for four years now." Anna's nose tip was slightly red and a bit comical, her eyes were damp. She dabbed at her eyes with a soft handkerchief. "It's terrible!"
[369] "Of course, of course," the inspector said with a dull smile. "Don't cry yet, Miss. We'll take care of business first, then you can cry all you want. I understand you're the type of girl who knows everything about your boss's professional and personal life. Tell me – how do Lestrade and DeWitt get along?"

I can tell you one more thing," she said, her tone becoming like a weather forecaster, "I think you'd be interested to know, wouldn't you? It turned into a brawl when her boyfriend Lord heard the commotion and walked in to see it all. He punched Mr. Longstreet to the ground. Mr. DeWitt also came quickly. They sent me away, and I don't know what happened after that. It happened about two months ago."
[379] The Chief looked calmly at the secretary, with his own plans in mind. "Very good, Miss Pratt. You're very good. Do you think DeWitt has anything on Longstreet?"

'Personal loans,' Mr. Longstreet would say maliciously, and he always got the money. In fact, just a week ago, he borrowed twenty-five thousand dollars from Mr. DeWitt, and Mr. DeWitt was furious, I was afraid he would have a stroke on the spot..."
[381] "I believe so," Sam murmured.
[382] "They argued in this room, but it was Mr. DeWitt who gave in, as usual."
[383] "Were there any harsh words?"
[384] "Yes, Mr. DeWitt said, 'Things can't go on like this,' and he said they both needed to clean up their act, or they'd all be in trouble."

[386] Anna blinked her brown eyes. "You won't find anyone who spends money like Mr. Lunsford does," she said with a malicious tone. "Luxurious living, gambling, horse racing, speculating – and always losing, blowing through his regular income from the company in a few rounds. When he's broke, he comes to Mr. DeWitt for 'personal loans.' 'Private loans,' for heaven's sake, that's what they are – he hasn't paid a penny back. I know for a fact, I've been calling the bank on his behalf, asking them to extend his credit, to allow him to borrow more, and he's already liquidated all his bonds and properties for cash. I'd bet he doesn't have a penny left."
[387] Sam looked thoughtfully at the glass-topped desk. "You say Mr. DeWitt's money always goes unrepaid, and Mr. Lunsford keeps asking for more like he has a rich father, very good, very good!” He suddenly fixed his gaze on Anna, who lowered her eyes uneasily. "Miss Pratt,” he continued lightly, "we're all grown-ups now, and we don't believe in the fairy tale of the stork bringing the baby anymore. What's the relationship between you and Mr. Lunsford? [389] "Sit down, sit down, Miss," Sam smiled. Anna sat back in her chair. "I'm sure of it, now tell me, how long have you been living together?"

[391] "Of course, of course," the chief of police soothed. "Do you live with your parents?"
[392] "My parents live in the suburbs."
[393] "I guess so. Did Mr. Longstreet promise to marry you too? Of course, it's just another unfortunate girl meeting the wrong guy. Then, Mr. Longstreet hooked up with Mrs. DeWitt and left you, right?"
[394] "This…" Anna stammered, suddenly staring at the floor tiles. "Yes, that's true."

[396] This time Anna chose to remain silent. She wanted the relentless Sam to know that she was smart enough to not fall for his bait. Sam hummed a tune, gazing intently at her neatly combed hair without speaking. After a while, Sam spoke again, this time with a completely different serious tone and asked a completely different set of questions. From her, he learned that on Friday afternoon, just as Longstreet was preparing to go to the Grant Hotel to see Cheryl Brown, Mike Collins burst into the office, his face pale with anger, pointing his finger at Longstreet's nose and calling him a fraud. DeWitt was not present at the time. [405] "You're too much!"

[411] "Twelve years."

[413] "Before the war, we made money mining in South America, and then we came back to the United States and started a brokerage firm together."

[417] DeWitt sat calmly. "Who told you that?"
[418] "DeWitt, I'm asking you."
[419] "That's a boring question," DeWitt biting on his own beard, "I occasionally lend him some money, that's just friends helping each other out - small amounts..."
[420] "Two and a half thousand dollars is a small amount, right?"
[421] DeWitt, looking frail, sat as if on pins and needles. "That - that wasn't a loan, it was a personal matter."

[423] DeWitt couldn't sit still anymore, jumping up from his chair with a twisted face and a greenish tint. "That's abuse of police power! I'll tell you, it has nothing to do with Longstreet's murder -"
[424] "Alright, stop acting. Go wait outside."
[425] DeWitt stood there with his mouth open, panting. He gradually calmed down, the violent emotions dissipating, but still stood upright and walked out, a bit unsteadily. Sam watched him leave, a bit perplexed. DeWitt's behavior was odd and hard to understand...
[426] Sam called the next person, DeWitt's wife Fawn.
[427] The conversation soon ended, and Sam didn't get much out of it. This old, grumpy, and often explosive woman was no less strange than her husband. She seemed to hide deep, twisted emotions and secrets, but she said she didn't know anything, nothing at all. When asked about her relationship with Lister, she calmly denied everything except that they knew each other and had a superficial friendship; as for Lister's attempt to seduce her daughter Jennie, she scoffed: "As far as I know, he's interested in mature women." Her tone was like ice; as for Theresa Brown, DeWitt's wife only said she was "a scheming actress" who bewitched Lister with her pretty face, and that was all she knew; finally, when asked if DeWitt was being extorted, DeWitt's wife reacted with disbelief: "What nonsense! There's no such thing..."
[428] Sam didn't say anything, but he was already thinking: This is a typical shrew, full of vinegar. Sam further threatened and coaxed, but besides the fact that he and DeWitt had been married for six years and that Jennie was DeWitt's daughter from his first wife, he couldn't get anything out of her, and had to give up.
[429] DeWitt's wife stood up, took out a powder compact from her handbag, and continued to powder her already powdered face.
[430] Her hand trembled, and the powder compact fell to the ground, shattering the mirror. Her arrogant posture disappeared, and the blood drained from her face under the makeup. She quickly made the sign of the cross, her eyes full of fear, and muttered in Spanish, "God save me!" But in that instant, she regained her composure, glared angrily at Sam, glanced coquettishly at the broken mirror fragments, and then left briskly. Sam laughed, picked up the mirror fragments, and put them on the table.
[431] Ehring was a big guy who looked younger than his actual age. He walked with a confident stride, a smile on his lips, and a very gentle and open gaze.
[432] "Please sit down, Mr. Ehring. How long have you known DeWitt?"
