[990] Sam and Bruno stood up. Bruno smiled sincerely: "Welcome, Mr. Raine. It's good to see you again. I thought you had disappeared into thin air last night. Where did you go?"
[991] Raine sat down, placing his cane between his knees. "You must consider an actor's dramatic behavior as a given, Mr. Bruno. The most effective way to attract an audience is to learn how to dramatically exit the stage. But I'm afraid my disappearance has no mysterious meaning. I have seen everything that needs to be seen, and there is no longer anything I can do on the scene, so I returned to my refuge at Hamlet's Manor... Oh, Chief Inspector, how are you on this gloomy day?"
[992] "Not bad," Sam replied with little enthusiasm. "For an old actor, you get up early, don't you? I thought actors slept until noon."
[993] "Not necessarily, Chief Inspector," Raine blinked his clear and bright eyes. "Since people stopped looking for the Holy Grail, my profession has been the most vibrant on Earth. This morning, I woke up at 6:30, swam two miles before breakfast to satisfy my appetite, then tried on the new wig made by Quixi, which was completed yesterday and considered a masterpiece by Quixi; then, I discussed issues with my director Kropotkin and my stage designer Fritz Hoff; then, I read through all the letters I received; and finally, I entered Shakespeare's era, wandering in that wondrous and glorious ancient time – it's 10:30 now, and I'm here. How is that for an ordinary day? Aren't you pleased with the beginning?"
[994] "Of course, of course," Sam answered, trying to match Raine's cheerfulness. "But for retired people like you, there are no troubles like the ones we face under work pressure, for example – who killed Wood? I won't ask you about the mysterious murderer X anymore – you already know who killed Langstroth."
[995] "Chief Inspector Sam!" Raine's tone remained gentle. "Are you forcing me to quote Brutus's words? 'I will patiently listen and seek to hear and respond to such noble things, my noble friend, until the moment comes when I can do so. Think deeply on what I say.'" Raine chuckled. "Did you get the autopsy report on Wood?"
[996] Sam looked at Bruno, who also looked at Sam, and the two burst out laughing, filling the office with a pleasant atmosphere. Sam picked up Dr. Schiller's report and handed it to Rayn without comment.
[997] Rayn held the report up in front of him, reading it with full concentration. The report was concise, written in meticulous German calligraphy. Occasionally, Rayn closed his eyes and focused his mind.
[998] The report stated that Wood was unconscious when he fell into the water, but he was not dead. The cause of unconsciousness was a severe blow to the head, but the skull did not fracture. The inference that Wood was unconscious when he fell into the water, Dr. Schiller wrote, could be proven by the small amount of fluid in Wood's lungs, indicating that the deceased had some life signs shortly after falling into the water. The report concluded that the reasonable inference was that Wood had been struck on the head with a blunt object, rendered unconscious, and then thrown into the water from the ship, where he repeatedly hit the hull of the Möhawk and the wooden stakes of the pier, resulting in his death.
[999] The report continued, stating that there was nicotine in the victim's lungs, but in a minor amount, indicating that the deceased had been trying to reduce his smoking; the scar on the left leg, at least twenty years old, was determined by the distorted scar after healing, suggesting that the person who treated it was not a professional medical personnel; the blood sugar level was high, but not high enough to constitute diabetes; there were clear signs of alcohol poisoning, possibly indicating that the deceased was fond of diluted spirits. Based on physical condition, the deceased was a sturdy middle-aged man with red hair, distorted fingers, and deformed nails, indicating that he was or had been a physical laborer; there were signs of a fracture in the right wrist, but it had already healed; there was a small black birthmark on the left arm; and there was a scar from an appendectomy two years ago; the ribs had also been broken, with the fracture time estimated to be about eleven years ago, and it had also healed; the weight was 220 pounds, and the height was 6 feet 6 inches.
[1000] After reading the report, Rayn smiled and handed it back to Sam.
[1001] "Reinhold, have you noticed anything unusual?" Bruno asked.
[1002] "Dr. Schilling is a very meticulous worker," Reinhold replied. "This is a very thorough report. It's impressive how much detail can be gleaned from such a severely damaged body. As of this morning, what do you two think of DeWitt's involvement?"
[1003] "You're so interested in this guy?" Sam asked, deflecting the question.
[1004] "Very interested, Inspector," Reinhold answered.
[1005] "Yesterday, we—" Bruno rushed to answer, as if that was an answer to the question. "We had someone follow him all day."
[1006] "Bruno, are you trying to withhold something from me?" Rain asked softly, standing up and adjusting his cape. "But I don't believe you would... Inspector, thank you for giving me the clear photo of Lestrade. This photo could prove very useful before the end."
[1007] "Oh, that's no problem, don't mention it," Sam answered, his tone suddenly friendly. "Honestly, Rain, both Bruno and I believe DeWitt is the most suspicious."
[1008] "Really?" Rain's green eyes wandered from Sam to Bruno, becoming distant. He gripped his cane tighter. "I won't bother you two with my work anymore. I have a full schedule today, personally speaking."
[1009] He strode towards the door, turning back at the threshold. "Please allow me to sincerely advise you two, for now, don't take any direct action against DeWitt. We are facing the most difficult times, you two, I mean 'we.'"
[1010] They nodded politely to Rain, who quietly closed the door and left.
[1011] Act V
[1012] Hamlet Manor
[1013] September 10, Thursday, 12:30 PM
[1014] If Sam Inspector and Bruno Prosecutor appeared at Hamlet Manor on Thursday at 12:30 PM, they would question whether what they saw was real.
[1015] They would see a different Jury Leen - only half of Leen: his eyes and voice remained the same, but his attire differed greatly from before, and his appearance, under the skillful hands of Old Quixy, underwent a surprising transformation every moment.
[1016]In the dazzling light, the two of them seem like characters straight out of a medieval play, and the room itself is like the laboratory of the Swiss alchemist Paracelsus. The room is large, with several worktables and miscellaneous items. The doors of several old-fashioned cabinets are wide open, revealing all sorts of strange and bizarre objects inside. There are small piles of hair and various colored powders scattered on the floor, deeply embedded in the wooden cracks. In the corner is an interesting modern machine – an electric sewing machine. As for the walls, one has a thick wire hanging from it, on which at least fifty wigs of different sizes, styles, and colors are hanging; and the innermost wall has been designed into a series of compartments, with a total of ten or so plaster busts of life size – black, Mongolian, and Caucasian – some with hair, some bald, some expressionless, and some with various emotions: fear, joy, surprise, sadness, pain, sarcasm, anger, determination, admiration, depression, ferocity, and so on.
[1017]Besides the large, bright chandelier hanging above Vance's head, there is no other source of light in the room – various-sized lamps are scattered about, but they are all shrouded in darkness. The giant shadow cast by the chandelier is like a tragic horror story unfolding. Vance sits motionless, his shadow is exaggeratedly magnified and motionless, projected onto the wall, while Quincannon's tiny, hunched-over figure is like a giant flea, circling around Vance's shadow from time to time, like a wave of ink splashed.
[1018]Everything is so bizarre, terrifying, yet also dramatic; even the boiling cauldron in the corner does not seem like something from the real world, but rather a scene from Macbeth where the smoke rises up the walls, like three witches brewing a potion.
[1019]In this terrifying shadow story, Vance, who remains motionless, plays the role of the enchanted person, while Quincannon's distorted figure, shrunk and hunched, is Mesmer, and the diminutive Merlin without his robes.
[1020]However, the truth is that Quincannon's work is merely routine makeup work – with his nimble fingers, he uses various pigments and powders to change his master's appearance.
[1021]Rayne looked at himself in the three-way mirror – now, he wore a well-tailored casual outfit with barely any stitching marks.
[1022]Quincy stepped back, wiping his hands on his smock, and examined his work with narrowed eyes. "The eyebrows are a bit too thick – a little unnatural," Rayne said, pointing at his eyebrows with a long index finger.
[1023]Quincy raised his brown face, craned his neck, and closed one eye, much like a portrait painter stepping back to reevaluate the model's proportions. "Probably a bit of a problem, probably a bit of a problem," he squeaked. "The curve of the left eyebrow – too – shouldn't be going down like that." He picked up a small pair of scissors attached to his belt and carefully trimmed Rayne's eyebrows. "Now, I think it's much better."
[1024]Rayne nodded. Quincy once again got a hand full of pigments that matched his skin tone and gently applied them to Rayne's chin...
[1025]Five minutes later, he stepped back half a step, put down the small scissors, and placed his hands on his hips. "This time, it looks good, doesn't it, Mr. Rayne?"
[1026] The veteran actor looked at his new appearance again. "This makeup job for this investigation can't have any flaws, you know, you ugly clown," Quixi grinned like a mischievous fairy; there was no doubt that Rene was very satisfied - this was the tacit understanding between master and servant, Rene only used the name of the grotesque monster Caliban from the play "The Tempest" to call Quixi when he was extremely satisfied with Quixi's work, "However - now it won't be like that. It's time for the hair part."
[1027] Quixi hopped over to the other side of the room, turned on the light, and stared unblinkingly at the wig hanging on the wire. Rene leaned back in his chair to rest.
[1028] "Caliban," Rene's voice was not loud, but had a challenging tone, "I think there are some differences in our ideas."
[1029] "Oh?" Quixi responded without turning around.
[1030] "It's about the basic understanding of makeup. If there's any flaw in your amazing makeup skills, it's that you do it too perfectly."
[1031] Quince picked a thick gray wig, turned off the light, and walked back to Rene's side, squatting on the bench, and took out a strange comb to deal with the wig seriously.
[1032] "Mr. Rene, there is no such thing as doing makeup too perfectly," Quince said, "one can only say that the world is full of mediocre makeup artists."
[1033] "Oh, no, I don't doubt your genius in this area, Quince," Rene looked at Quince's delicate combing action with his claw-like hands, "However, I'll say it again - in fact, in terms of appearance, it's not the most important thing whether it's exactly like it is. In a sense, it's just the supporting part." - Quince snorted - "Well, I know you disagree, but have you really thought about it? Humans naturally tend to get an overall impression when they look at things, which means that people pay attention to the overall image rather than every detail."
[1034] "But," Quince countered seriously, "that's the problem! If a detail is wrong - I don't know how to say it - distorted, it will interfere with the overall image in people's eyes, and it will inevitably force people to find out where the detail that destroys the overall image is, so I say - every detail must be flawless."
[1035] "Good job, Caliban, good job," Rene's voice was extremely gentle and friendly, "you've argued well for yourself, but you still haven't really grasped the subtlety I mentioned. I didn't say that the details of makeup could be treated carelessly, carelessness would inevitably attract people's attention, and you're absolutely right - the details must be flawless. But we don't need all the perfect details! Do you understand what I mean? For an outstanding makeup artist, it's very painful to accept this point, but it's irrefutable ... It's like saying, when painting a seaside scene, you meticulously paint every wave, and when painting a tree, you meticulously paint every leaf. Every wave, every leaf, every wrinkle on a person's face, it's true, but it's a bad work of art."
[1036] "Well, maybe," Quirky said reluctantly. He raised the wig and carefully examined it under strong light, shook his head, and then started combing it rhythmically with his hand holding the comb.
[1037] "So far, we can conclude that makeup, powder, and other makeup accessories are what we use to create the appearance part of the makeup, but they are not the makeup itself. You also know that sometimes we have to pay special attention to a certain part of the appearance in makeup, for example, if you want to make me look like Abraham Lincoln, you have to emphasize the mole, mustache, and lips, and other parts can be simplified. No, not just appearance, but you have to combine posture, manner, temperament, and character, etc., to truly imitate it perfectly. Let me give you another example, wax statues are made to imitate real people, and every detail from shape to skin color is very lifelike, but what we see is still an inanimate object, and if a wax statue can naturally move its arms, speak vividly from its wax lips, and turn its glass eyes - you know what I mean."
[1038] "That's good enough," Quirky said calmly, holding the wig up to the light again.
[1039] Ren closed his eyes: "This is what has always fascinated me about the art of drama - creating the appearance of real life and the image of lively characters through action, voice, and posture... In the face of this art of recreating life, Belasco is the genius who best understands the essence of it. He can create a cozy and leisurely atmosphere on an empty stage without any trouble, without relying on a burning fireplace to bring a visible peaceful and quiet atmosphere, and without the stage designer using various props and scenery to accompany it. He only ties a cat so that it cannot move before the performance, and unties it at the moment when the curtain rises, so that when the curtain rises, the first thing the audience sees is a cat standing on the stage, as if there is a fireplace in front of it, comfortably yawning and stretching. Without hearing any lines, just watching a simple and familiar action of daily life, all the audience can feel as if they are in a warm and comfortable room. This is the most exquisite and accurate performance design I have ever seen by Belasco."
[1040] "Mr. Ren, that's an interesting story," Quirky walked up and carefully put the wig on Ren's well-proportioned head.
[1041] "Quincy, this is a remarkable person," Rayne whispered, "bringing real life into fictional drama - in fact, in the Elizabethan era, drama relied only on the actors' lines and bodily movements to recreate real life. Actors had to perform on an empty stage - an extra carrying a tree crawling across the stage represented a forest from Bermuda to Dunsinane, and this was performed for decades, and no audience member did not know the meaning. I often wonder if modern stage design is too excessive and overbearing, causing harm to the drama itself..."
[1042] "All right, Mr. Rayne," Quincy lightly tapped Rayne's leg professionally, and Rayne finally opened his eyes, "finished."
[1043] "Oh, is that so? Then please step aside from the mirror, you little brat."
[1044] Five minutes later, Rayne stood up. From the perspective of clothing, appearance, manner, and temperament, the original Jules Renard had completely disappeared and had become a completely different person. He strode through the room, turned on the main light. Under the light, he wore a thin coat, had a gray hairdo with a yellow soft felt hat, and his lower lip protruded.
[1045] Quincy laughed, standing happily next to Rayne.
[1046] "Tell Dromio that I'm ready to leave. And get ready yourself."
[1047] Act Six
[1048] Wycherly
[1049] September 10, Thursday, 2:00 p.m.
[1050] Sam disembarked at Wycherly and looked around. A New Jersey police officer patrolled the gangplank, keeping watch over the empty Mohegan ferry. Seeing Sam, he snapped to attention and saluted with a standard salute. Sam nodded briefly in return, passed through the waiting room, and left the dock.
[1051] He walked along the cobblestone path leading to the ferry terminal, climbing up a steep hill. The hill stretched from the dock to the top, where it faced the other side of the river, with sheer cliffs below. Sam made his way up, step by step, as cars drove past him, slowing down as they descended. He stopped and looked down, taking in the majestic view of the Hudson River and the city beyond. Soon, Sam continued on his journey.
[1052] At the top of the hill, Sam saw a traffic cop and asked for directions to Bovard's Way in his deep voice. He crossed a wide road and walked down a quiet, somewhat cluttered street lined with trees, arriving at a busy intersection. Sam knew he had reached the Bovard's Way he had been looking for, so he turned north and continued walking.
[1053] Finally, he reached his destination—Number 2075, a wooden house sandwiched between a milk shop and an auto parts store, with peeling paint and in a state of disrepair. The front yard was cluttered with three old rocking chairs and a rickety long bench, with faded "Welcome" lettering on the mat. A yellow sign on one of the posts read "Men's Room for Rent."
[1054] Sam looked around the street, straightened his shirt, and tightened his hat before stepping up the creaky steps and pressing the buzzer marked "Manager." From the depths of the dilapidated house, the sound of the buzzer echoed, followed by the sound of clattering sandals. The door opened a crack, revealing a red nose. "What do you want?" a woman's gruff voice asked, then turned into a gasp of recognition, followed by giggles, and finally the door swung open, revealing a beer barrel-shaped woman in worn-out house clothes—matching her house perfectly. "Oh, it's the police officer! Come in, come in! Sorry, Sam Inspector, I didn't know it was…" She rambled on, trying to smile but only managing to show her yellow teeth. She stepped aside, muttering and shaking, opening the door for Sam to enter.
[1055] "Oh, it's been hell lately," she continued without stopping her mouth, "This morning, we've had a whole bunch of reporters and people with big cameras! We—"
[1056] "Is someone upstairs, ma'am?" Sam asked.
[1057] "Of course, Detective, that man has been upstairs all day, dropping ashtrays all over the carpet. I've been photographed four times this morning… Chief, would you like to see the poor man's room again?"
[1058] "Take me upstairs," Sam gruffly said.
[1059] "Yes, sir," the woman smiled obsequiously, using her two thick fingers to pretend to hold her dirty skirt, and wiggling up the stairs carpeted with thin carpet. Sam cursed under his breath as he followed behind, and when they reached the second-floor landing, a bulldog-like man stood in their way.
[1060] "Who is it, Mrs. Murphy?" the bulldog detective asked, emerging from the dim light.
[1061] "It's all right, calm down, it's me," Sam replied loudly.
[1062] The detective relaxed, flashing his white teeth in a smile. "Didn't recognize you at first, officer. Glad to see you. It's been quite boring standing guard here."
[1063] "Anything happen since last night?"
[1064] "Nothing at all."
[1065] The detective led the way down the hallway to a room at the back, with Mrs. Murphy trailing behind, swaying her hips. Sam stopped at the open door.
[1066] The room was small and empty, with a cracked ceiling, stained walls, and worn-out carpet. The furniture was old, and the sink had an early model lead pipe. The only window had a faded floral curtain, but the room had a clean smell, and it was clear that the occupant took care to keep it tidy. There was an old iron bed, a cabinet with drawers against the wall, a small table with a marble top, a chair with a wire frame that still worked, and a wardrobe - that was all the furniture.
[1067] Sam walked in and stood in front of the wardrobe, opening both doors. Inside were three old men's suits neatly hung, with two pairs of shoes underneath, one relatively new and the other with a hole in the big toe. On top of the wardrobe was a straw hat in a paper bag, and another hat with a sweat stain on the band. Sam searched the pockets of the suits, checked the shoes and hats, but found nothing of interest. He frowned, disappointed with his search, and closed the wardrobe door.
[1068] "Are you absolutely sure," Sam turned to the stiff detective standing by the door, "that no one has touched anything here since last night?"
[1069] The bloodhound detective shook his head: "Inspector, I am very careful and attentive when I am on duty. Since you left last time, nothing has been touched here."
[1070] Next to the wardrobe was a cheap handbag with a broken handle, still attached to the bag. Sam opened it and found it empty.
[1071] Sam walked to the cabinet and pulled out the heavy drawer, which contained several sets of clean, old underwear, a stack of handkerchiefs neatly folded, half a dozen striped shirts, a few wrinkled ties, and a ball of clean socks.
[1072] Sam moved away from the cabinet. Despite the damp and cold outside, the small, sealed room was stuffy, so he used a silk handkerchief to wipe the sweat from his face and stood with his legs apart in the center of the room, looking around and then walking to the marble table. There was a bottle of ink, a pen with dried ink, and a stack of cheap grid-lined paper on the table. Sam skipped over these items and picked up a Royal Bengal brand cigar box out of curiosity, only to find that it contained only one cigar. He touched it with his finger, and the cigar broke into pieces. Sam put the cigar box back and frowned even deeper, but he continued to search the room.
[1073] There was a shelf on the edge of the sink, with some items on it. Sam walked over and took everything off the shelf, including a broken alarm clock, a quarter-full bottle of black malt whiskey – he pulled out the cork and sniffed deeply – a glass, a toothbrush, a rusty metal shaving box, a small bottle of aspirin, and an old copper ashtray … Sam took a stub of a cigar butt from the ashtray, checked the brand label buried in the ash – it was a Crem brand – and thoughtfully walked back to the door.
[1074] Mrs. Murphy watched Sam's every move with her malicious little eyes, and she spoke in a nasal voice: "I must say, Inspector, you have to put up with this room being so messy. This tenant said he wouldn't let me help him clean it up."
[1075] "Oh, it's fine," Sam said dismissively, suddenly stopping and looking at Mrs. Murphy with bright eyes. "By the way, Mrs. Murphy – has any woman come to see Mr. Wood?"
[1076] Mrs. Murphy snorted and lifted her chin, which was covered in pimples. "Inspector, if you weren't a police officer, I'd knock your head off for saying that. I can tell you, no, no woman has come to see him. This is a respectable place, and everyone knows it; I've always told my tenants that the most important rule here is, 'No women allowed,' and I've made that very clear to them. There's no monkey business in Mrs. Murphy's house."
[1077] "Hmm," Sam sat down on the only chair in the room, "no women have come to see him... relatives? Has his sister or sister come to see him?"
[1078] "As for that," Mrs. Murphy replied quickly, "I can't forbid someone from having sisters, so of course, my tenants have sisters who come to see them, and they have aunts, nieces, and cousins, but Wood never had any. You know, I've always considered Mr. Wood to be my best tenant, and he's been here for five years, never causing any trouble, so quiet and polite, a true gentleman. As far as I know, no one has ever come to see him. But we don't see him often either, he works on the New York subway from noon to night; and we don't provide meals here – tenants have to go out to eat – so I don't know how Wood eats. But this poor guy, I can say – he pays his rent on time, doesn't cause any trouble, and hasn't gotten drunk – so quiet that it's as if he doesn't exist, I –"
[1079] But Sam didn't listen any further, standing up and turning his back to Mrs. Murphy. Mrs. Murphy stopped talking, blinking her frog eyes at Sam's back, and then huffed, pouting, and leaving the room.
[1080] "An old hag," the agent cursed beside the doorpost, "of course, it's only sisters, aunts, and aunts who can come, that's all I've seen. He sneered lewdly.
[1081] But Sam paid no attention to these things; he was walking slowly, trying to feel the situation under the carpet with his feet. Suddenly, a small bulge caught his eye near the edge of the carpet. He lifted the carpet and found that it was caused by a raised board. Then he walked to the bed, hesitated for a moment, and then knelt down and crawled under the bed like a blind man. The detective exclaimed, "Hey! Sir - I'll come."
[1082] But Sam didn't pay any attention to him; he carefully felt around the carpet under the bed. The detective followed him, crawling on his belly and shining a small flashlight into the dark space under the bed. Sam exclaimed triumphantly, "It's here!" The detective lifted a corner of the carpet, and Sam grabbed a small yellow book from under the bed, and then they both emerged from under the bed, brushing off the dust from their clothes.
[1083] "Sir, it's a bank passbook, isn't it?"
[1084] Sam didn't answer - he flipped through the small book, which listed the amount deposited into the savings account each year, with no record of withdrawals, and each deposit was less than ten dollars, mostly five dollars. The balance in the account was $945.63. There was also a folded five-dollar bill in the account, obviously Wood was planning to deposit but was killed before he could do so.
[1085] Sam put the passbook in his pocket and turned to the detective, "What time do you work until?"
[1086] "Eight o'clock, someone will be relieving me."
[1087] "Listen," Sam said grimly, "call me at headquarters tomorrow at 2:30 p.m. There's a special task I want you to handle, understand?"
[1088] "Yes, I'll call you at headquarters tomorrow at 2:30 p.m., and I'll make sure to do it."
[1089] Sam left the room, went down the stairs – each step seemed to elicit a pig's squeal – and exited the house through the front door. Mrs. Murphy was vigorously sweeping the porch; with a pus-filled red nose, she grunted and made way for Sam to pass.
[1090] Sam walked onto the sidewalk, looked at the information on the cover of the passbook, and then roughly judged the direction before crossing Pova Road and heading south. After walking three blocks, he saw the building – a small bank with a marble entrance. Sam walked in and picked the window marked "S-Z", which was attended by an elderly gentleman who greeted him with a glance.
[1091] "Are you the person responsible for this window?" Sam asked.
[1092] "Yes, sir, what can I do for you?"
[1093] "You may have read in the paper that a streetcar conductor named Charles Wood was murdered. He lived around here," - the old gentleman nodded - "I'm Detective Sam from the opposite bank's homicide department, investigating this case."
[1094] "Oh!" the old gentleman reacted quickly, "Wood was one of our customers, Detective. I saw his photo in the paper this morning."
[1095] Sam took out Wood's checkbook from his pocket. "So, uh -" he looked at the name of the service personnel listed on the window, "Mr. Aylie, how long have you been responsible for this window?"
[1096] "Eight years."
[1097] "Does Wood usually deposit his money through you?"
[1098] "Yes, sir."
[1099] "From the records on the passbook, he comes to deposit money every week - not necessarily on the same day. Can you describe how he comes here to deposit his money?"
[1100] "Detective, there's nothing special about it, as you said, in my memory, Mr. Wood comes here once a week, and almost at the same time - between 1:30 PM and 2:00 PM - I only found out from the news report that he comes before work."
[1101] Sam frowned. "In your memory, does he come here alone to deposit the money? That's what I want to know. Does he come alone?"
[1102] "I don't have any impression of anyone else accompanying him."
[1103] "Thank you for your time."
[1104] Sam left the bank and walked back to the apartment near Mrs. Murphy's on Bowditch Street. Across the street from the milk shop was a stationery store, and Sam went inside.
[1105] The sleepy owner yawned and greeted Sam.
[1106] "Do you know Charles Wood, who lives on this street? The Charles Wood who was murdered on the ferry last night?"
[1107] The owner perked up. "Oh, of course! He's my regular customer. He often comes here to buy cigars and paper."
[1108] "What kind of cigars does he buy?"
[1109] "Cream or Bengal Royal, usually those two."
[1110] "How often does he come?"
[1111] "Almost every day after lunch – before work."
[1112] "Almost every day, huh? Have you ever seen anyone with him?"
[1113] "Oh, no, he's always alone."
[1114] "And he buys his stationery from you too?"
[1115] "Yes, he does. He only comes to buy once in a while – ink, and some paper."
[1116] Sam begins buttoning up his shirt. "How long has he been coming here?"
[1117] The boss tugs at his disheveled hair. "I guess about four or five years. Say, are you a journalist, right?"
[1118] Sam walks out in silence, stopping on the sidewalk. He sees a tailor's shop nearby and goes to inquire, discovering that Wood has only visited a few times over a long period, always alone.
[1119] Sam's eyebrows furrow more and more as he asks around nearby laundry shops, shoe repair shops, shoe stores, restaurants, and pharmacies. All of them only remember Wood occasionally visiting, always alone - even when going to the restaurant.
[1120] Sam asks more questions at the pharmacy, but the pharmacist doesn't remember Wood coming with a doctor's prescription. The pharmacist mentions that if Wood was sick and had a doctor's prescription, he would likely have bought the medicine from a pharmacy nearby in New York. At Sam's request, the pharmacist prepares a list of the names and clinics of eleven doctors and three dentists within a five-block radius.
[1121] Sam knocked on every door, repeating the same words and questions at each clinic: "You may have seen in the paper that Charles Wood, a conductor on the Forty-second Street subway, was murdered last night on the Wyckoff ferry. He lived around here. I'm Inspector Sam from the police department, looking into some background information on him to see if anyone knows anything about his personal life, his friends, or his relationships. Did he ever come to your office for a visit, or did you ever visit him when he was sick?"
[1122] Four doctors had not seen this murder report and did not know this person, even not heard of him; another seven doctors had seen the report but had not treated him, so they knew nothing about him.
[1123] Sam gritted his teeth and relentlessly visited the three dentists listed on the slip of paper. At the first dental clinic, he was even more infuriated when he had to wait for thirty-five minutes to see the dentist; after finally being ushered into the examination room, the dentist turned out to be a standard hardliner who refused to speak without seeing Sam's identification. This situation lit a fire of hope in Sam, and he quickly put on an air of authority as a police officer, using threats, intimidation, and shouting to scare the dentist into submission. However, the dentist's response left Sam deflated. The dentist reluctantly admitted that he did not recognize Charles Wood.
[1124] Other two dentists had never heard of Wood either.
[1125] Sam sighed, trudging slowly along the main road back up to the crest of the hill, then winding down to the ferry landing and taking the boat back to New York.
[1126] New York.
[1127] Sam returned to the city and immediately headed to the headquarters of the Third Avenue Trolley System. The congested traffic made his already unattractive face even more contorted.
[1128] At the office building of the personnel department, Sam immediately asked to see the personnel manager, and the staff immediately led him to a large office. The personnel manager had a rather worn-out appearance, with deep and dense wrinkles on his face. He rushed up to greet Sam. "Inspector Sam, right?" He greeted Sam warmly, and Sam politely responded, "Please sit, Inspector." The manager pulled a rather dirty chair over and sat Sam down without a word. "I think you're here to investigate Charles Wood, right? It's terrible, really terrible," he said, sitting back at the table and biting off the end of his cigar.
[1129] Sam coldly surveyed the other man. "Yes, I'm here about Charles Wood," he grunted.
[1130] "Yes, yes, it's terrible, I don't know how this could happen - Charles Wood was one of our best employees, quiet, diligent, and reliable - the most standard staff."
[1131] "Mr. Cropp, you mean he hasn't caused any trouble, right?"
[1132] Cropp leaned forward eagerly: "Sergeant, I tell you, this guy is a gem to our company, never drinks on duty, everyone in the office likes him. His work record is spotless, the most trustworthy person we have. In fact, I was about to promote him. After five years of service like this, I was going to promote him to an investigator, yes, that's right."
[1133] "Oh, you mean he's civic-minded and helpful?"
[1134] "I didn't say that, I didn't say that, Sergeant Sam, " Cropp hastily denied, "I just mean — he's very trustworthy. You came to investigate Charles Wood's character, right? This poor guy, he's been working hard since he joined the company. He wanted to do a good job, and we gave him the chance, Sergeant. That's our company's policy: if you work hard and want to stand out, we'll support and promote you from behind."
[1135] Sam only murmured a few words and didn't respond.
[1136] "Inspector, I tell you, Mr. Wood never comes late, never leaves early, and never takes a day off. He works on holidays too, and gets double pay for overtime. Some of our drivers and conductors often have to advance their salaries, but Mr. Wood, no, he won't, Inspector, he absolutely won't! He saves all his earnings - you can check his savings book if you don't believe me."
[1137] "How long has he been with the company?"
[1138] "Five years. Wait, let me check." Mr. Kropf got up and ran to the door, calling out, "Hey, John, bring me Charles Wood's file."
[1139] Soon, Mr. Kropf returned to the desk, handing a long piece of paper to Inspector Sam. Inspector Sam leaned over the desk, looking at Mr. Wood's file. "Look here," Mr. Kropf pointed to the record on the paper, "He's been with the company for five years and a few months. He started on the East Third Avenue line; three and a half years ago, we transferred him and his partner driver Patrick Ginnis to the Westville trolley car - he lives in Weehawken, which is convenient for him to commute to work on this line. You see, there's no bad record, right?"
[1140] Inspector Sam pondered, "So, Mr. Kropf, what about his personal life? Do you know anything about his friends, relatives, or people he often hangs out with?"
[1141] Croppenstedt shakes his head. "I don't know much about that, there are always rumors, but I don't think they're reliable. I know he gets along well with his colleagues, but he never goes out with them to have fun. I guess the closest thing to a friend he has is Patrick Guinness. Look here," Croppenstedt flips the file over, "this is what he filled out himself - relatives - none. Inspector, just the kind of answer."
[1142] "I hope to confirm it." Sam says quietly.
[1143] "Maybe Guinness -"
[1144] "It's fine, I'll get in touch with him if I need to." Sam puts on his hat, "For now, that's all. Thanks, old buddy."
[1145] Croppenstedt warmly grabs Sam's arm, escorting him out of the office and out of the company's main entrance, repeatedly assuring him of full cooperation with the police. Sam cuts him off, nodding and saying goodbye, turning and walking away.
[1146] He stopped at the corner, glancing at his watch, as if waiting for someone. A few minutes later, a black large Lincoln car with tightly drawn curtains pulled up, screeched to a stop in front of him. A slim, uniformed young man jumped out of the front seat, opened the back door for him, and waited for him to get in. Sam looked around, then got in the car. Hiding in the corner of the car was old Quix, looking more like a forest fairy tale than usual, dozing off.
[1147] The young driver closed the door, returned to the driver's seat, and continued on the road. Quix was awakened by the car's vibrations, opened his eyes, and saw Sam sitting beside him, lost in thought. His grotesque face immediately broke into a smile. He bent down to open a small dark compartment embedded in the car's undercarriage, then sat up straight, his face slightly flushed, but a metal box in his hand. The inner layer of the box lid was a mirror.
[1148] Sam moved his broad shoulders. "A whole day's trouble, Quix, but not in vain," he said.
[1149] Sam took off his hat, reached into the box to feel around, and then smeared a thick layer of oily liquid on his face. Quix held the mirror and handed him a soft towel, which Sam used to wipe his oily face vigorously. Then, ah, when the towel was removed, Sam disappeared like magic, or rather, he didn't completely disappear, but there was still a little oil left on his face, but the original makeup was gone, revealing a clean, sharp, and always smiling Philo Vance.
[1150] Scene 7
[1151] West Endgate Dewart Manor
[1152] September 11, Friday, 10:00 am
[1153] On Friday morning, the sun finally emerged, and the luxurious black Lincoln limousine glided down the quiet residential streets. The tall poplars stretched out their leaves to greet the long-lost warmth of the sun.
[1154] Rayne looked out the window, chatting with Quix, who said that at least the high-end residential area of West Endgate had not been designed in a uniform style, with each house occupying a large plot of land and clearly separated from its neighbors. Quix replied indifferently that he still liked Hamlet Manor.
[1155] The car stopped in front of a small villa. The green lawn framed a colonial-style white house with tall birch trees and poplars on either side, impeccably kept. Rayne got out of the car, waving at Quix. He was still dressed as usual, with a black hat, a shawl, and a walking stick in his hand.
[1156] "Am I going too?" Quix was surprised and even a little flustered. He didn't have his calming apron on, and his mood was even more unsettled. He wore a plain top hat, a black velvet short coat, and shiny new shoes. But the new shoes seemed a bit tight, causing him to wince when he stepped onto the sidewalk. He limped after Reine towards the porch.
[1157] An elderly man in uniform greeted them and led them through the bright hall to a colonial-style large living room.
[1158] Reine sat down and admired the room, while Quix stood uneasily behind him.
[1159] "I am Jury Reine," Reine said to the old man, "Is the host at home?"
[1160] "No, sir, they are all out. Mr. de Witt is in town, the Miss went shopping, and the Madame –" He coughed, "went for a mud mask, I think that's what it's called, sir, so –"
[1161] "How interesting," Rine said with a smile. "You are—"
[1162] "I'm Jurgens, the oldest servant of Mr. DeWitt."
[1163] Rine comfortably leaned back in a caped chair: "Excellent, Jurgens, you're just the person I need. I'll explain my identity and purpose."
[1164] "You mean me, sir?"
[1165] "You should know, the murder of Lancelot Lestrade is being investigated by Bruno prosecutor, and through his kindness and generosity, I've been allowed to participate in the investigation. I—"
[1166] Johannes' expressionless face suddenly cleared. "Sir, I apologize for interrupting you. If I may say so, Mr. Raine came today——"
[1167] "Well, well," Raine waved impatiently to interrupt him. "Johannes, I have a few questions for you, and I hope you will answer honestly. Mr. DeWitt——"
[1168] Johannes suddenly became alert, which could be seen from the change in his expression. "If I show any disloyalty to Mr. DeWitt, sir——"
[1169] "Remarkable, Johannes, remarkable," Raine's sharp eyes fixed on Johannes. "I'll say it again—you are remarkable, so loyal. I can assure you that I came here today to help Mr. DeWitt."
[1170] Johannes' pale lips finally relaxed, and a smile appeared.
[1171] "We continue, Mr. DeWitt was involved in this tragic murder case because of his close relationship with Mr. Longstreth. I believe that we can find helpful information from their relationship. I ask you, does Mr. Longstreth often come here?"
[1172] "No, sir, he rarely does."
[1173] "Why doesn't he come often, Jorgens?"
[1174] "I'm not quite sure of the reason, sir, but I know that Miss DeWitt doesn't like Mr. Longstreth, and Mr. DeWitt—well, sir, to be more explicit, whenever Mr. Longstreth is present, Mr. DeWitt seems preoccupied…"
[1175] "Oh, I see. And what about Mrs. DeWitt?"
[1176] Mr. Jorgens hesitates: "Well, sir... "
[1177] "You think it's best not to discuss this, is that it?"
[1178] "Yes, sir, it's best not to."
[1179] "For the fourth time, I must commend you—remarkable... Quincy, sit down, old friend, you must be tired."
[1180] "Sir, over eight years."
[1181] "Do you think Mr. DeWitt is a person who makes a lot of friends - has many friends?"
[1182] "This... sir, I don't think so. I believe his only true friend is Mr. Ehren, who lives nearby. But I don't want you to misunderstand, Mr. DeWitt is a very pleasant man if you get to know him."
[1183] "So you mean there aren't many visitors here, right?"
[1184] "Not many visitors, sir. Oh, of course, Mr. Inperiali is staying here now, but he's a special friend in a way, and he's probably come here three or four times over the years, besides that, Mr. DeWitt rarely invites guests."
[1185] "You said there aren't many visitors, so do any of the occasional visitors here happen to be customers - I mean those with business dealings?"
[1186] "Yes, sir, but they are few and far between. For example, recently there was a guest from South America who stayed in the house."
[1187] Rayne thought for a moment. "You said recently, around when?"
[1188] "The gentleman stayed here for about a month and left about a month ago."
[1189] "Has he been here before?"
[1190] "I don't recall him."
[1191] "You said South America, which part of South America are you referring to?"
[1192] "Sir, I don't know."
[1193] "Do you remember the exact time he left?"
[1194] "I believe it was August 14th."
[1195] Rean remained silent for a while and then asked in a slow, highly interested voice, "Do you recall if Lestrade came to visit while the South American guest was staying here?"
[1196] Jurgens replied without hesitation, "Yes, sir, and more frequently than usual. Mr. Machado, oh, sir, Felipe Machado is the South American guest - came the first night, Mr. Lestrade stayed here all night, he, Mr. DeWitt, and Mr. Machado were in the study until the early hours of the morning."
[1197] "Of course, you wouldn't know what they talked about, would you?"
[1198] "Oh, sir, of course not."
[1199] "Quite right, I asked a silly question," Raine said mildly. "Felipe Machado, sounds like a foreign name, what kind of person was he, Jurgens? Can you describe him for me?"
[1200] Jurgens cleared his throat and said, "He was a foreigner, sir, looked like a Spaniard, very dark, tall, with a military-style black beard. His skin was so dark, I should say - almost like a black or Indian. And he was a bit of an odd character, too, rarely stayed indoors, not talkative, didn't often have meals with the family, almost as if he didn't get along well with them. There were a few nights when he went out until the early hours of the morning or even didn't come back at all."
[1201] Reen smiled and said: "Such an unusual guest, such unusual behavior, Jorgens, how did Mr. DeWitt react to it?"
[1202] Jorgens became a little uneasy: "Well, Mr. DeWitt thought it was fine, letting Mr. Machado come and go as he pleased."
[1203] "Do you know anything else about this man?"
[1204] "Oh, he speaks English with a strong Spanish accent. He has very little luggage, just a large suitcase. He often has secret conversations with Mr. DeWitt in the evening, occasionally with Mr. Lunsford. Sometimes there are other guests, and Mr. DeWitt doesn't introduce Mr. Machado much, just - well, at the level of a casual introduction in a social setting. That's all I know, sir."
[1205] "Does Mr. Ehren know Mr. Machado?"
[1206] "Oh, he doesn't."
[1207] "What about Mr. Inpriale?"
[1208] "Mr. Inpriale hadn't come yet when Mr. Machiux left. Mr. Machiux left after a while, and Mr. Inpriale came later."
[1209] "Do you know where Mr. Machiux went when he left?"
[1210] "No, he left with his large suitcase. I believe that, aside from Mr. DeWitt himself, no one in the house knows more about Mr. Machiux than me, including Mrs. DeWitt and Miss DeWitt."
[1211] "And one more thing, Jorgens, how did you know that Mr. Machioco was from South America?"
[1212] Jorgens coughed twice, covering his mouth with his dry, parchment-like hand. "I was there when Mrs. DeWitt asked Mr. DeWitt, and it was Mr. DeWitt who said it."
[1213] Rine nodded, closed his eyes, and then opened them again, saying, "Can you recall if there were any other South American guests? In the past few years?"
[1214] "No, sir. Since I've been working here, Mr. Machioco is the only South American guest."
[1215] "Excellent, Jorgens, it was a pleasure talking to you. Now, can you please call Mr. DeWitt and tell him that Philip Rine has urgent matters to discuss with him, and I would like to meet him for lunch today."
[1216] "Yes, sir." Jurgens walked over to the low coffee table and calmly dialed the number, waiting a moment before speaking. "Mr. DeWitt? This is Jurgens... Yes, sir, there's a Philo Vance here who wishes to invite you to lunch today. He says it's a matter of great importance... Yes, sir, Philo Vance... He specifically asked me to tell you that it's urgent, sir..."
[1217] Jurgens turned to Reine. "Mr. Reine, Mr. DeWitt asks if it would be convenient to meet at the Stock Exchange Club at noon?"
[1218] Reine's eyes lit up. "Very convenient, Jurgens. We'll meet at the Stock Exchange Club at noon."
[1219] Reine and Quixley left the house and got into the car. Reine, turning to Quixley, who was busy loosening his collar, said, "By the way, Quixley, you haven't had much opportunity to put your observation skills to the test these days. Would you like to play detective for a while?"
[1220] The car started moving, and Quixley managed to loosen his collar. "Whatever you say, Mr. Reine, but this collar..." Reine's laugh came from deep in his throat. "This is just a warm-up for you—I must apologize for making you handle such a trivial matter, but you're still a novice in the detective game... This afternoon, I have a lot of things to take care of, so you can go to the consulate of every South American country in New York City and try to find out which consulate's staff have had contact with a man named Felipe Machado, the tall, dark, and mustachioed man with some African or Indian blood, essentially a true Othello. You should know how to proceed with caution, Quixley, I don't want Sam the Cop and Bruno the Prosecutor to suspect my investigative direction, so you understand, don't you?"
[1221] "Machado," Quincannon murmured the name, his dark fingers twisting his mustache into thin lines. "How do you spell this strange name?"
[1222] Vance continued his unfinished thought as he pondered, "If Sam Inspector and Bruno Prosecutor don't think to question DeWitt's butler, I don't need to tell them anything."
[1223] "The butler is too talkative," Quincannon said acerbically. His aversion to Jurgens was typical of a man who had spent his life speaking little and listening a lot.
[1224] "On the contrary, you dimwit," Vance said softly. "He's just not saying enough."
[1225] Scene 8
[1226] Securities Trading Club
[1227] September 11, Friday, noon
[1228] Despite no prior arrangement, Raine's entrance was nonetheless remarkable. To Raine, it was simply a matter of walking into a typical Wall Street securities trading club with its leather-hard atmosphere, but in fact, his appearance caused quite a stir. As he entered, three men discussing golf in the lounge first caught sight of him, immediately abandoning their game to start whispering. A black waiter's eyes widened upon seeing Raine's peculiar poncho. An employee sitting behind a desk was so startled that he dropped the pen he was holding. The news quickly spread throughout the club.
[1229] One person after another passed by Raine, pretending to be indifferent but actually curious about the strange figure from the distant past.
[1230] Raine sighed and took a seat in the lobby. An elderly man rushed over, bowing so low that his back almost touched the ground.
[1231] "Good afternoon, Mr. Rine, welcome," Rine smiled slightly, "It's a great honor to see you. I am the head waiter here, and I'm at your service. Or would you like to start with a cigar first?"
[1232] Rine politely held him off with a hand. "Oh, no, no, thank you very much for your kindness. You know, my throat doesn't allow it." These words seemed to have been said a thousand times by Rine, as they were spoken with such politeness that they sounded practiced, even mechanical, "I have an appointment with Mr. DeWitt here, has he arrived?"
[1233] "Mr. DeWitt, is it? I think he hasn't arrived yet, Mr. Rine. He should be here soon." The head waiter's voice subtly conveyed a sense of reprimand towards DeWitt, implying how could such a famous man like Rine wait for him, "Sir, if he hasn't arrived yet, please let me know if you need anything."
[1234] "Thank you." Rine leaned back in his chair, closed his eyes, and the head waiter, feeling quite proud, adjusted his tie and returned to his post.
[1235] At this point, the diminutive DeWitt quickly entered the hall. His face was pale and his expression was quite anxious. With new worries added to his old ones, he looked even more agitated. He responded to the head waiter's smile with a face that didn't change, then quickly crossed the lounge to Rine, drawing envious glances from the other club members.
[1236] The manager said to Rain, "Sir, Mr. DeWitt has arrived." Rain didn't react, which made him a little embarrassed. DeWitt asked him to leave and touched Rain's stiff shoulder, so Rain opened his eyes. "Oh, DeWitt!" Rain said happily, standing up.
[1237] "I'm sorry, Mr. Rain, for making you wait, " DeWitt's tone was a little unnatural, "I had another appointment - I had to cancel it - that's why I'm late..."
[1238] "Don't worry." Rain said, taking off his cape, a black uniformed servant quickly stepped forward, taking Rain's cape, hat, and cane, as well as DeWitt's coat and hat, and the manager led them both through the lounge to the dining room. The manager, with a professional look of fatigue, immediately smiled and came forward to seat them, and then led them to a less conspicuous corner according to DeWitt's request.
[1239] Throughout a simple lunch - DeWitt listlessly flipping through the meat, Rain relishing a thick slice of roast beef - Rain showed no intention of getting to the point. DeWitt tried several times to probe Rain's purpose for meeting him, but Rain simply said "Eating in peace won't cause indigestion" and left the topic aside, leaving DeWitt helplessly smiling. Rain continued to eat effortlessly and naturally, as if nothing else mattered more to him than savoring the British roast beef in front of him. Rain talked about some precious past stage days of his own as he ate, punctuating his stories with the names of stage stars - Otho Skinner, William Faversham, Booth, Mrs. Fiske, Ethel Barrymore, etc. With Rain's light and interesting conversation as an old stage actor, DeWitt's originally tense mood relaxed and he began to listen attentively.
[1240] After the meal, they drank coffee, Rain declined DeWitt's cigar, and DeWitt's mood had completely stabilized. Rain said, "Mr. DeWitt, I have noticed that you are not a naturally melancholic person." - DeWitt was startled unexpectedly but only exhaled smoke without answering - "From your facial features and your recent actions like a tragic story, this is not a remarkable discovery in psychiatry - I believe that mental exhaustion is the result of long-term accumulation, causing an alienation of your original character."
[1241] DeWitt murmured, "In a way, I've been living a very difficult life."
[1242] "That's right," Rene's voice sounded more convincing, and he placed his long fingers on the table, unmoving. DeWitt stared at the hands, as if focusing on a single point. "Mr. DeWitt, I spent an hour talking to you, and my intention was benevolent. I feel that I must get to know you better, and I believe, perhaps a bit arrogantly, that I should be able to help you. In fact, I think you need some special help in your current situation."
[1243] "Thank you so much," DeWitt's voice was gloomy, and his lowered eyelids never lifted, "I am well aware of my precarious situation. Whether it's Bruno the prosecutor or Sam the police officer, they are not just trying to intimidate me. I am being monitored all the time, and even my letters are being checked. Including you, Mr. Rene, you have also questioned my servants..."
[1244] "I only asked your butler, Mr. DeWitt, and it was purely to help you."
[1245] "Sam the police officer also said the same thing, so you can see - I know my situation. Of course, on the other hand, I can feel that you and the police are a bit different - you are more humane, right?" DeWitt shrugged, "You may find it surprising, but since Tuesday night, I've been thinking about you constantly. You've broken through my defenses several times..."
[1246] Reen's face grew serious. "Now, do you mind if I ask you a couple of questions? My approach to solving cases is different from the police, purely personal, and my only goal is to uncover the truth. To seek further truth, I must first know some things..."
[1247] DeWitt suddenly raised his head: "Further truth? Mr. Reen, you mean you have some truth in your hands?"
[1248] "Yes, Mr. DeWitt, two fundamental facts." Reen waved his hand, and a waiter quickly ran up. He ordered another cup of coffee. DeWitt's cigar went out, hanging loosely between his fingers, but he was so focused on staring at Reen that he didn't notice. Reen smiled gently and said again, "I must point out that a beautiful lady's words are inappropriate and wrong, that it was an incorrect prophecy. Mr. DeWitt, did you know that Mrs. Savini compared Shakespeare to an immortal cup of coffee, predicting that Shakespeare's verses would soon be forgotten by the world?"
[1249] DeWitt looked as if Reen had slapped him, his face drained of color, and his cigar snapped in two between his fingers. Under Reen's calm gaze, DeWitt blinked rapidly, trying to hide his shock. He forced himself to remain calm and said, "You know who killed Lestrade and Wood!"
[1250] Reen politely said, "Mr. DeWitt, I am taking appropriate action,"—DeWitt sat stiffly like a statue—"Unfortunately, we face the black and white justice of the law, which only recognizes specific and concrete evidence. Will you help me, Mr. DeWitt?"
[1251] DeWitt didn't answer for a long time. His face twisted, and his eyes scrutinized the unusual detective as if trying to discern from his mask-like, expressionless face how much he knew, or more accurately, what he knew. He finally spoke, his voice like a taut string: "I will do whatever I can, whatever I can..."
[1252] "Do you mean it, Mr. DeWitt?"
[1253] The situation had developed into a sentimental melodrama, and in a sense, it was quite mediocre. Ren felt inexplicably annoyed, as if a small insect were wriggling uneasily in some corner of his body.
[1254] DeWitt remained silent, still looking intently into Ren's eyes, as if the killer's name were written there. Finally, he struck a match and held the flame to the end of his cigar that had gone out. "I will tell you everything I know, but, Mr. Ren - how can I put it? - it seems my hands are tied... There's one thing you must not force me to say - the identity of the person I had an appointment with on Tuesday night."
[1255] Ren shook his head, not looking displeased. "You have put yourself in a difficult position, Mr. DeWitt, if you insist on keeping silent about the crucial part of this case. Anyway, let's put that aside for now..." Ren paused, "So far, Mr. DeWitt, I know that you and Lent had once explored for gold in South America and made a fortune. Then, you returned to America and set up a company that required a lot of money. I also know that you found precious metal mines in South America, and I believe that all this happened before the war, right?"
[1256] "Yes."
[1257] "Where was your mine located in South America?"
[1258] "Uruguay."
[1259] "Uruguay, I see," Rain half-closed his eyes, "So, Mr. Machado is a Uruguayan, then?"
[1260] DeWitt's jaw dropped, and his eyes were filled with confusion. "How do you know Machado?" he asked, "It must be that damned old bastard, Jorgens. I should have told him...".
[1261] Rain sharply interrupted him, "Mr. Dwyte, I must say, your attitude towards this matter is completely wrong. Jorgens is a respectable man, a loyal servant, and he only told me because what I asked was beneficial to you, and only in such a case would he speak up. I think you should learn from him—unless you doubt my intentions."
[1262] "No, no, I'm sorry. Yes, Machado is Uruguayan," said Dwyte, looking miserable and glancing around, his eyes becoming wild again, "But Mr. Rain, please stop asking me about Machado."
[1263] "Mr. Dwyte, I must insist. Who is Machado? What is his profession? How do you explain his strange behavior when he lived in your house? You must answer my questions."
[1264] Dwyte absent-mindedly drew his spoon across the tablecloth. "If you insist on asking... Well, there's nothing extraordinary about it, he was just a client of mine, Mr. Rain. Machado—he represented a South American state-owned listed company—wanted us to handle some funds for him... You know, it's a legitimate company, and I—"
[1265] "Mr. Dwyte, did you and Langstrith decide to accept the commission?" Rain asked expressionlessly.
[1266] "Well... we... we're still considering." DeWitt's spoon kept tracing the tablecloth, getting faster and faster, leaving various geometric shapes on it, including angles, curves, and rhombuses.
[1267] "You only agreed to consider it," Rene repeated sarcastically, "so why did he stay for so long?"
[1268] "Well, I'm not too sure... maybe he contacted some other financial institutions...?"
[1269] "Can you give me his address?"
[1270] "I'm not quite sure about his contact information... he's always on the move, not staying in one place for too long..."
[1271] Rene unexpectedly laughed: "Mr. DeWitt, you're terrible at lying. We both know that going on like this won't lead to any results. Before your rambling gets us even more confused, I think we should stop here. Goodbye, Mr. DeWitt. I must also tell you that my judgment and analysis of human nature have always been one of my proudest abilities, but your attitude today was a heavy blow to me."
[1272] Rene stood up - a waiter rushed to pull out his chair. Rene smiled at him and looked at DeWitt's lowered head, still speaking in a very friendly voice: "Anyway, you are always welcome to visit Hamlet Manor, on the Hudson River, I live there, if you ever change your mind. Goodbye, Mr. DeWitt."
[1273] Rene left, leaving DeWitt alone, who sat there dejectedly, as if he had just been sentenced to death.
[1274] The headwaiter led Rene through the other tables, and Rene suddenly stopped, laughing to himself, and then walked out of the restaurant. Not far from where DeWitt was sitting, a man was dining, with a flushed face, looking quite peculiar. Rene and DeWitt had been talking, and he had been leaning over, clearly eavesdropping.
[1275] In the lounge, Rene tapped the headwaiter on the shoulder: "The man with the flushed face, the one sitting not far from our table - is he a member here?"
[1276] The headwaiter's face fell. "No, sir, he's a detective. He just showed his badge and insisted on having dinner here."
[1277] Rayne laughed again, slipped a bill into the headwaiter's hand, and casually approached the service counter, where the responsible staff rushed to greet him.
[1278] "Could you please arrange for me to see Dr. Morris of your club and the secretary in charge of the affairs here?" Rayne said.
[1279] Scene 9
[1280] Prosecutor's Office
[1281] September 11, Friday, 2:15 p.m.
[1282] At 2:15 p.m. on Friday, Reen strode down Central Avenue, with the towering headquarters of the New York Police Department on one side and a row of foreign shops on the other. Number 137 was a ten-story building that housed the office of the Chief Prosecutor of New York City, a magnificent structure. Reen entered the building, walked through a corridor, and took the elevator up.
[1283] As usual, he controlled his emotions perfectly, with no expression on his face. Lifelong training on the stage allowed him to control every muscle in his face at will, but at this moment, when no one could see him, his eyes glowed with an uncontrollable light - an excited light, a light of anticipation that something was about to happen, like the fire in the eyes of a hunter waiting with a gun for his prey, a light of joy that came from vitality and sharp-mindedness. If someone saw these eyes now, they would never believe that their owner was deaf and elderly ... It was undoubtedly the fiery passion of his soul that had been touched, and the powerful vitality that gushed out, forming a torrent of confidence, vitality, and sharpness.
[1284] However, when Reen pushed open the door to the outer office of Bruno prosecutor, the light in his eyes suddenly disappeared, and he was just the strange man in old-fashioned clothing who looked quite young.

[1346] "At regular intervals, we always hear a producer publicly declare that a famous stage actor has been deeply moved by the grandeur of this immortal play and has decided to reprise the role of Hamlet. At this point, what is the first thing the well-intentioned but misguided producer usually does? The focus is entirely on Mr. Barrimore or Mr. Hapden, and all the promotional efforts revolve around them, resulting in the public viewing the performance solely through the lens of these actors' performances, completely overlooking the epic charm of the play itself."
[1347] "Mr. Geddes had noticed this, and in order to correct the overemphasis on the actors, he particularly recruited the talented young actor Mr. Mace as the protagonist. Mr. Geddes' ingenuity lay in the fact that Mr. Mace had never performed Hamlet before, which indeed revived some of the playwright's original intentions - but Mr. Geddes only showcased his own interests in Hamlet, rather than the original Hamlet that an interpreter should strive to emphasize. As for some of his other missteps, such as deleting some dialogue and setting Mr. Mace's performance style, transforming Hamlet into a furry-faced young man like an athlete rather than a profound philosopher, are another matter...
[1348] "What I'm saying is that this emphasis on the stars is a grave affront to one of the greatest playwrights in human history, Shakespeare. The same is true in film, Mr. George Arliss' portrayal of historical figures on the screen attracts crowds, but is it really the masses flocking to see Disraeli or Alexander Hamilton? No, of course not, they are merely admiring another brilliant performance by George Arliss."
[1349] "You see," Renault continued, "the emphasis is off, and the goal is impossible to achieve. The major mistake in your modern police's way of catching criminals is like the mistake made by Mr. Barrymore in playing Hamlet in a modern movie. The producers altered the original content and adjusted the original structure to accommodate Mr. Barrymore, regardless of whether the new Hamlet presented by Mr. Barrymore matched the original Hamlet written by Shakespeare. You, Inspector Sam and Prosecutor Bruno, your errors are identical. You trimmed the original content and adjusted the original structure to accommodate the conclusion that DeWitt is the murderer, without regard for whether this conclusion matches the truth of the crime. Your reasoning is not rigorous, you only collect the most superficial facts, and you ignore direct and indirect evidence that cannot be explained, and these errors accumulate, making your assumption about the killer too flexible and arbitrary. Therefore, when faced with a mountain of unshakable facts in the crime, it appears full of holes and worthless. And when a conclusion contradicts the facts, it only means that the assumption is wrong. Can you understand what I'm saying?"

[1359] Scene 10
[1360] Hamlet Manor

[1362] Rain entered the theater lounge of Hamlet Manor, accompanied by a thin, melancholic man. There was a corridor leading to the grand hall of Hamlet Manor, with a wall of glass at the entrance. [1390] Act Eleven
[1391] Law Firm of Lehman, Brooks & Chadwick

[1409] Brooks shrugged: "Well, if you must know - Mrs. DeWitt, the state's court in this situation can only make one kind of ruling, and Mr. DeWitt has the evidence to prove that he is in this situation and is requesting a divorce - Mrs. DeWitt, I regret to tell you - there is no need for any other reason, just one fact, that you had an affair with someone else."
[1410] So far, Mrs. DeWitt had remained composed, but her eyelids had dropped weakly. "What kind of evidence?"
[1411] "A signed statement by a witness. This witness signed the statement under a formal oath ceremony recognized by law, and the authenticity of the evidence cannot be doubted. On the morning of February 8th, this witness saw you and Langstroth in Langstroth's apartment, and from the situation at the time, it can be inferred that you spent the weekend in Langstroth's place. The statement further states that at 8:00 a.m. on February 8th, you were wearing a thin nightgown, and Langstroth was also disheveled. The witness saw you two in an extremely intimate state at that time, Mrs. DeWitt, do I need to go into further detail? This sworn statement also records some very embarrassing details."
[1412] "Enough, don't say anymore." Mrs. DeWitt whispered, her eyes flashing with fire, and she collapsed, returning to her normal human appearance, even trembling like a young girl. After a long time, she wrung her hands and asked, "Who is the black-hearted witness - is it a woman?"
[1413] "I cannot tell you that." Brooks became gruff, "I know what you're thinking, you're thinking it's just a scare tactic to make it up." He lowered his face, coldly pursuing, "I can assure you that I have this document, and there is a completely reliable witness who can prove the authenticity of this document. I can also assure you that we have the ability to prove that what happened between you two in Langstroth's apartment was not the first time, although it may be the last time. Mrs. DeWitt, I repeat once again, the conditions that Mr. DeWitt has proposed in this situation we both know are generous. Based on my experience in handling such cases, I advise you to accept this proposal - two thousand dollars a year until you remarry, as long as you don't cause trouble and we can handle the divorce proceedings smoothly. Please consider it carefully."
[1414] Brooks seemed to conclude the conversation and stood up, looking down at Mrs. DeWitt in the chair. Her hands were still folded on her knees, staring at the floor for a while, and then she got up silently from the chair and walked to the door. Brooks opened the door for her and accompanied her to the reception room, helping her press the elevator button. Neither of them spoke, quietly waiting for the elevator until the elevator door opened, and Brooks finally spoke: "I hope to receive your reply, or - your lawyer's reply, Mrs. DeWitt, if you don't want to have a lawyer handle it." However, like there was no Mr. Brooks in front of her, Mrs. DeWitt went straight into the elevator. The elevator attendant smiled at Brooks, but Brooks unconsciously swayed and fell into thought.
[1415]
[1416] Young partner Roger Sheardown peeked out from the reception room curiously and made a face. "Lionel, did she leave? How did it go?"
[1417] "I had to give her a heavy dose, and she swallowed it without a peep. This woman is quite resilient."
[1418] "Oh, Mr. DeWitt should be happy with this outcome. But do you think she's planning to retaliate? She's not yelling or crying or anything."
[1419] "It's hard to say, it's really hard to say. I have a feeling that she guessed our witness is Anna Platt. Platt said that when she peeped into the bedroom that morning, she thought Mrs. DeWitt saw her too. These women!"
[1420] Brooks paused for a moment, "Hey, Roger, this gives me a bad feeling. You'd better have someone keep an eye on Anna Platt. I can't be sure what her real intention is in revealing this. If Mrs. DeWitt plans to bribe her, I wouldn't be surprised; if she recants on the witness stand, things will...
[1421] Brooks shook his head: "Tough, Roger, really tough. I bet old Lyme's got a headache right now. If DeWitt's wife knew how slim the chances are of her husband being acquitted, she wouldn't worry about that divorce request. She's more likely to become a widow than a divorcée."
[1422] Scene 12
[1423] Hamlet Manor
[1424] October 4, Sunday, 3:45 PM
[1425] Rayne strolled through his English garden, his hands loosely crossed behind his back, inhaling the floral scent in the air. By his side was the brown-toothed, brown-faced old Quincy, the silent, understanding Quincy, who let his demeanor match Rayne's mood. And as Rayne's spirits seemed somewhat melancholic, Quincy, like an old hound, quietly accompanied him.
[1426] "If what I say sounds like complaining, old man," Reen said softly, without lowering his gaze to the thin and short Quincy, "please forgive me. I've been getting increasingly agitated, despite our great teacher's repeated admonitions to not rush time, not hurry time. For example,” Reen spoke in a voice of an orator, "‘Time is the eternal guardian of justice, it interrogates all the sinners of the world, so trust it and let it execute.’ No one has said a more correct thing than Lady Rosalind. 'Those who hide their mistakes and conceal their sins, time will eventually reveal their buried sins and mock and humiliate them.' Although not elegant, it is insightful. Then, old man, you see, 'The wheel of time turns, and brings retribution.' This is so correct, so you see…"

The branches above formed a circular canopy. A bench was set between the two trunks, and Reen sat down, motioning for Quincy to sit next to him.
[1428] "Quincy's tree," Reen murmured. "You see, so old and strange, we have finally found a likeness to you…" He half-closed his eyes, and Quincy sat down worriedly beside him.
[1429] "You look troubled," Quincy said softly, then fell silent, as if he had said something wrong.
[1430] "Do you think so?" Reen glanced playfully at Quincy. "It seems you know me better than I know myself… But Quincy, waiting alone cannot soothe my anxious heart. We stand at the end of the road, with no signs of a detour. I keep asking myself where the path to a bright future lies. We have witnessed the formation of a sphinx of man, John DeWitt transforming from a weak man tormented by unnamed fear to a strong man supported by unnamed power. And who knows which kind of powerful drug can suddenly give him an unshakable soul like steel? I visited him yesterday, and he was like a yogi ascetic—detached, calm, and indifferent, waiting quietly for death, like an Eastern Buddhist."
[1431] "Maybe," Quince said sharply, "he will be acquitted without guilt."
[1432] "Possibly," said the old actor, "but he seems to be resigned like a New Stoic believer of ancient Rome, willingly staying in his little iron cage. It's a strange character... As for the rest - there is no other, I am completely at a loss now, and can only retreat and play a minor role in this play... The Missing Persons Bureau is willing to help, but the report they provided is useless. The incredibly efficient Sam Inspector - Quince, a gentleman of unassuming simplicity - notified me that he had also checked all the passengers who took that ferry on the night of the crime, including their addresses, identities, occupations, and backgrounds, but still found nothing... In vain! What we need is hidden, unfindable, and unfindable... Bruno, the cunning and tenacious prosecutor, told me through Leon Brooks, DeWitt's lawyer, that DeWitt's wife has retreated to her lair - it seems that she neither agrees nor refuses her husband's divorce proposal, a clever and dangerous woman, Quince... As for my female colleague, Cherie Brown, who works in an improper theater, she constantly drifts towards the prosecutor's office, providing some information to deal with DeWitt, completely unaware that what the prosecutor needs most is her charming appearance - an undeniable sight on the witness stand, especially her beautiful legs and enticing chest... "
[1433] "Mr. Raine, if it were April," Quince suddenly interrupted, "I would think you were practicing Hamlet's soliloquy."
[1434] "And poor Charles Wood," Raine continued, sighing, "left an immortal legacy for the New Jersey government, with no one claiming it - nine hundred forty-five dollars and sixty-three cents. And the five-dollar bill that was not deposited in the account may have decayed in the archives... Oh, Quince, we live in an age full of miracles! "

[1469] The Fourteenth Scene
[1470] Criminal Court Building
[1471] October 9th, Friday, 9:30 a.m.
[1472] The short and silent Judge Green, dressed in black, solemnly entered the courtroom. The gavel struck, and a ceremonial call for silence rang out, drowning the noisy crowd in the courtroom and fading into the long hallway behind it. The fifth day of the trial for John DeWitt's alleged murder of Charles Wood officially began.
[1473] The gallery was filled with people. On either side of the judge's table and the court reporter's position were two tables, one occupied by Bruno prosecutor, Sam detective, and several assistant prosecutors, and the other by Leaman, DeWitt, Brooks, Sheldon, and several law firm employees.
