[1571] "Inspector, are you willing to swear that the bleeding wound you saw at the door was the same one you saw earlier on the top deck railing?"
[1572] Sam agreed without any objection, "Yes, the same one."
[1573] Lynam continued to question relentlessly, "There are no new wounds, not even new scratches?"
[1574] "No!"
[1575] "Inspector, I have no more questions. Mr. Bruno, the witness is yours." Lynam gave the jury a meaningful smile and turned back to his seat. Bruno shook his head impatiently, indicating that he had no problem, and Sam also left the witness stand. His expression was extremely complex - angry, surprised, and also containing some comprehension.
[1576] When Leaman approached the witness stand again to call a witness, everyone in the gallery leaned forward in anticipation, and a buzz of whispered conversation arose from all sides. The reporters in attendance hastened to record what was happening, and the bailiffs shouted to maintain order. Bruno scanned the courtroom, as if searching for someone.
[1577] Leaman appeared calm and confident, this time calling Dr. Morris to the stand. This Dr. Morris, a middle-aged man with a monkish face, worked at the Stock Exchange Club and stepped forward to take the oath, stating his full name, Hof Morris, and his address, before sitting in the witness chair.
[1578] "Are you a doctor?"
[1579] "Yes."
[1580] "Where do you work?"
[1581] "I am the club doctor of the Stock Exchange Club and also work part-time at Bellevue Hospital."
[1582] "Doctor, how long have you been a licensed practicing physician?"
[1583] "For twenty-one years, since I received my state medical license."
[1584] "Do you know the defendant?"
[1585] "Yes, I have known him for ten years, since he joined the club as a member."
[1586] "Given your position as the club's doctor and your professional knowledge, do you agree that, up to this point, every detail of the testimony presented by the other witnesses is accurate?"
[1587] "I agree."
[1588] "Why did you remind him to be careful of his wound after he refused to bandage it?"
[1589] "Because the wound is still healing, and any sudden bending of the index finger could cause the wound to re-open, especially since this wound goes through the upper two joints of the index finger and is difficult to keep still. For example, on Tuesday evening, he could have simply curled his hand, and it would have pulled on the injured area, causing the newly formed scar to split open."
[1590] "So, based on your medical expertise, you suggested that the wound should be bandaged, correct?"
[1591] "Yes, and the area is prone to contact with other objects, so bandaging it would prevent bacteria from entering and causing infection even if the wound reopens."
[1592] "Excellent, Dr. Morris. " Lehman jumped in quickly, "You have also heard the testimony of the previous witnesses describing the condition of the wound and the scar at the ship's railing. If the situation is as described by Chief Inspector Sam, is it possible that the wound has reopened? The time would be — let's estimate, just fifteen minutes before Chief Inspector Sam saw the wound. Dr. Morris, based on your professional knowledge, do you think it is possible?"
[1593] "You mean that the wound reopened and then healed back to the way Chief Inspector Sam saw and described it within fifteen minutes?"
[1594] "Yes."
[1595] The doctor spoke firmly: "Absolutely not."
[1596] "Why?"
[1597] "Even if it reopened an hour ago, the wound could not have healed back to the way Sergeant Sam described it - scabbed over, with no cracks, the entire scar forming a solid piece, and dry, which is impossible."
[1598] "So, based on Sergeant Sam's testimony, your opinion is that from when you treated the wound at the club to when the defendant opened the door on the ferry and accidentally ripped the wound open, the wound could not have opened during that time, right?"
[1599] Bruno protested vehemently at this point, while Doctor Morris answered firmly, "Yes." There was a murmur of conversation throughout the courtroom. Lerman looked thoughtfully at the jury, noticing that all the jurors were equally engaged in whispered conversation, and he smiled with satisfaction.
[1600] "Doctor Morris, I'll ask you again, based on the condition of the scar that Sergeant Sam saw while leaning on the deck railing, is it possible that within those few minutes, the defendant could have grabbed and lifted a 200-pound object over the railing and even thrown it into the river, two and a half feet away, without causing the wound to reopen?"
[1601] Bruno jumped up again, sweating profusely. He protested with all his lung capacity, but was rejected by Judge Green. Green ruled that such professional opinion was crucial to the defense's case.
[1602] Dr. Morris said, "Absolutely not, he could not have done what you said and kept the wound intact."
[1603] A victorious smile spread across Lynam's face as he said, "Mr. Bruno, it's your turn to question."
[1604] The courtroom stirred again. Bruno bit his lower lip and coldly looked at the witness stand. He paced back and forth in front of the witness stand, like an animal in a cage.
[1605] "Dr. Morris!" - Judge Green struck the gavel to silence the courtroom; Bruno stopped until the silence was complete before saying, "Dr. Morris, under oath, you just testified based on your professional knowledge and experience that if the defendant's wound matched the description of the previous witness, the defendant could not have used his right hand to throw a 200-pound object over the railing without tearing the wound -"
[1606] Lymann stood up calmly. "Objection, Your Honor. The prosecution's question does not match the witness's previous affirmative statement. The defense's statement also includes the two-and-a-half-foot extension of the deck of the Moby Dick."
[1607] "Prosecutor, please rephrase your question," Judge Green said.
[1608] Bruno had to comply.

[1614] "Of course not. He would not tear the wound if he did not use his right hand."
[1615] Bruno looked at the jury with a significant glance, as if to say, "You all heard it, the rambling before was meaningless, you can ignore it, DeWitt could do these things with his left hand." [1617] "Impossible."
[1618] "Why?"
[1619] "As a physician who has known the defendant for years, I can confirm that he is a right-handed person. People like him usually have limited strength in their left hand. DeWitt's height is quite small, weighing only 115 pounds, and from a physical perspective, he is quite weak. Based on these facts, my opinion is that it is impossible for a person weighing 115 pounds to lift a body weighing 200 pounds, as you described, using only one hand, and that hand is the less powerful left hand."
[1620] The courtroom erupted in chaos, with several reporters rushing out of the courtroom and several jurors nodding excitedly and exchanging opinions. Bruno stood on tiptoe, his face turning purple, shouting frantically, but no one paid attention to him. The court bailiffs shouted for silence with all their might. After the chaos finally subsided, Bruno pleaded with the judge to adjourn for two hours to obtain more precise medical opinions.
[1621] Judge Green frowned and said sternly, "If there are any similar disruptive scenes in the subsequent trial, I will immediately order the court to be cleared and closed. Do you hear me? The prosecution's proposal is hereby approved. The court will adjourn until 2:00 pm today."
[1622] The gavel fell, and everyone stood up. Only after Judge Green left the courtroom did the entire courtroom explode with footsteps and discussions. The members of the jury also left the courtroom. DeWitt's calm demeanor had disappeared, and he slumped in his chair, pale as a sheet, as if he had just completed a long journey. Brooks excitedly shook Leiman's hand. "Old Fred, this is the most brilliant defense I've seen in years."
[1623] Bruno and Sam seemed to be in the eye of a storm, sitting dumbfounded on the plaintiff's bench, glaring at each other. A group of reporters surrounded the defendant's stand, and it took a bailiff great effort to pull DeWitt out of the crowd.
[1624] Sam leaned forward. "Bruno," he grumbled bad-temperedly, "well, old boy, you've made a fool of yourself this time."
[1625] "We've made a fool of ourselves, Sam, we've made a fool of ourselves," Bruno grumbled bitterly. "After all, you were responsible for gathering evidence, and I was only responsible for the performance."
[1626] "I can't deny that," Sam reluctantly admitted.
[1627] "Now, we're the two biggest idiots in all of New York," Bruno said, putting his files in his briefcase and seething with anger. "All this time, the facts were right in front of you, and you couldn't even see the obvious!"
[1628] "You're right, I admit it," Sam said dejectedly. "I'm a real idiot, that's a fact, but... you know, you saw DeWitt's fingers wrapped in a handkerchief that night, but you didn't even ask."
[1629] Bruno suddenly tossed his briefcase aside, a look of sudden comprehension on his face. "That sneaky Lynam is now the big shot, damn it, it's infuriating. He has the nerve to brag about it. It's all there, just like your ugly nose on your ugly face—"
[1630] "Yeah, I got it," Sam said. "Of course, it was Rayne, that old buzzard! But we deserve it. We suspected him, and we should have."
[1631] The two of them just sat there, slumped in their chairs, looking around the empty courtroom, where Rain was nowhere to be seen.
[1632] "He's gone," Bruno said angrily, "I saw him sitting there... Yeah, you're right, we really did ask for it, he warned us not to act rashly from the start."
[1633] "Not only you, I don't understand either."
[1634] "I can't figure out why he would risk DeWitt's life for this."
[1635] "It's not that risky," Sam said dryly, "This trial was no risk for him, he knew he could get DeWitt off. So, let me tell you something, my friend, from now on, little Sam will be a good boy and listen to Mr. Rain's orders, especially when he's investigating the mysterious X."

[1640] Reen carefully observed the unfamiliar DeWitt, the stockbroker, who was mingling with his acquaintances, chatting nonstop and smiling, responding swiftly and cleverly to the harmless teasing and jokes.
[1641] Reen, on the other hand, was immersed in the warmth of ultimate satisfaction, as if a scientist who had endured arduous contemplation and investigation had finally completed his discovery. Indeed, DeWitt was the most exhilarating and terrifying page in the human study project. [1671] At around midnight, DeWitt and his group arrived at the Whipken Station on the West Side Line. The waiting room was dull and dirty, with a latticework of iron beams overhead, resembling a warehouse. The platform extended along the wall of the second floor, with only a few passengers waiting. In the corner near the yard, there was a luggage room with a clerk dozing behind the counter. The clerk at the nearby snack bar also appeared drowsy, yawning widely. The entire row of black waiting benches was empty.

[1686] Act III
[1687] Whipken-Newark Train
[1688] October 10, Saturday, midnight 12:20
[1689] The group sat in two groups: Jenny, Lord, and Impieri sat at the front of the car; DeWitt, Lane, Brooks, and Ehrn sat in two pairs of seats in the center of the car.
[1690] The train had not yet started, and Brooks stared at DeWitt for a moment before turning to Lane, who was sitting in front of him and saying, "Mr. Lane, some of the things you said tonight have deeply moved me... You mentioned that in a moment there is 'an endless age.' When a person sits in the defendant's stand, waiting for the jury's verdict - death? Or stepping out of the courtroom to start a new life? It's all decided in that brief moment. An endless age, it's said so well! Mr. Lane...".
[1691] "Yes, it's very accurate," DeWitt agreed.
[1692] "Really? You think so too?" Brooks looked at DeWitt's calm face. "It reminds me of a novel I read before - I think it was written by Ambrose Bierce, a very unique novel where a person faces the gallows, and - well, you know, at the moment of execution, this person actually relives his entire life, from beginning to end, without missing a single detail in his mind. Mr. Rayne, isn't that what you mean by endless age? I'm sure there have been many writers who have dealt with such ideas."
[1693] "I think I've read that novel too," Rayne replied, nodding beside Brooks as DeWitt sat next to him. "The concept of time, as science has told us for years, is relative. Let's take dreams as an example - we wake up feeling like we've been dreaming the whole time we were asleep... However, some psychologists tell us that the time we spend dreaming is actually extremely brief, happening in the moment we transition from unconscious sleep to conscious wakefulness, in a very short instant."
[1694] "I've heard that too," Ehrn said, sitting across from DeWitt and Brooks with his face turned towards them.
[1695] "What I really want to know," Brooks said, turning to look at DeWitt again, "is the reality of this unique psychological phenomenon. John, I can't help but be curious - I believe others feel the same - today, in that moment of judgment, what were you thinking about?"
[1696] "Perhaps," Lane said sympathetically, "perhaps Mr. DeWitt doesn't want to talk about it anymore."
[1697] "On the contrary," the short securities merchant said, his eyes shining and his expression animated, "what I experienced at that moment was the most unique experience of my life. I believe this experience fully supports the novel's theme and completely aligns with Mr. Lane's theory about dreams."
[1698] "So, was what you thought of at that moment all the things that happened in your life?" Ehren asked incredulously.
[1699] "No, no, it wasn't like that," DeWitt leaned back on the green cushion, speaking quickly. "What I thought of at that moment was something strange, something unrelated and something I shouldn't even remember…"
[1700] At that moment, the train's whistle blew, and the car jolted as it started moving. DeWitt raised his voice slightly. "To make a long story short, like the other jurors, I believed in the man's guilt based on the evidence presented by the police and voted for a guilty verdict. The jury reached a guilty verdict, and the man was sentenced to death and executed. I put the whole thing behind me."
[1701] The train departs the station. DeWitt stops, licks his lips, and the others in the room don't respond. "The strange part is this," he says, "nine years ago, I never thought of this person or this event again, but today, as the foreman stands to pronounce my fate - incredibly, just as the judge's final words about the jury's result are spoken, just as the foreman's first word is about to come out - suddenly, without reason, my brain explodes with a flash of insight, not only do I strangely recall the appearance of the man sentenced to death at that moment, but I also simultaneously remember who he is and where I have seen him - you can imagine, after nine years, my mind had completely stopped thinking about this person."
[1702] "Who is he?" Brooks curiously asked.
[1703] DeWitt chuckled, "That's why I said things were strange... It was about twenty years ago, and I was wandering around South America, and I stumbled upon a small place called Barinas—in the Samora region of Venezuela. One evening, as I was heading back to my humble abode, I passed by a dimly lit alley, and I heard intense fighting sounds. At the time, I was much younger and more adventurous than I am now.
[1704] "I had a revolver on me, so I quickly drew it from its holster and ran into the alleyway, where I saw two ragged locals attacking a white man. One of them was holding a curved knife and swinging it at the white man, so I pulled the trigger, but the bullet missed. However, the two bandits were terrified and ran away, and the white man lay on the ground, bleeding from several knife wounds. When I approached him, I thought his injuries must be severe, but he managed to get up on his own, wiped the blood from his pants, and quietly thanked me before limping away into the darkness. I only glanced at his face for a moment during this time. This man, whom I had saved twenty years ago, was the same one I later participated in the trial and sent to the electric chair. Isn't fate a cruel joke?"
[1705] Everyone was moved, and in the ensuing silence, Reine thoughtfully said, "This strange story is worthy of being included in folklore legends."
[1706] The train continues to rush through the night, with sparse lights dotting the landscape - this area is the outskirts of Whipken.
[1707] "But what I find most remarkable about this whole thing," DeWitt continues, "is that a puzzle I couldn't solve no matter how hard I tried, suddenly resolved itself in the nick of time when my own life was at stake! Remember, I had only seen this man's face once, and that was so many years ago..."
[1708] "This is one of the most incredible things I've ever heard," Brooks says, still awestruck.
[1709] "Human minds are far more mysterious and powerful than we can comprehend, especially in the face of death," Reen says. "Eight weeks ago, I read a report in the newspaper about a murder case in Vienna. The details were something like this: A man was shot dead in his hotel room, and the Vienna police quickly identified the victim. He was a small-time thug who had been recruited as an informant by various parties. The motive for the murder was clearly revenge - the victim had contacted the police to inform on someone, which had angered the killer and led to the attack. The report also stated that the victim had been staying at the hotel for several months, rarely leaving, even eating in his room, as if he were trying to evade pursuit. When the body was found, there were still unwashed dishes on the table. He was shot seven feet away from the table, the fatal bullet hitting him, but he didn't die immediately. This was deduced from the traces left at the scene: the body lay next to the table leg, and there was a long trail of blood leading from there to where he was shot. There was a very special circumstance at the scene - the sugar bowl on the table had been knocked over, and white sugar had been scattered all over the table. There was also a whole sugar in the dead man's hand."
[1710] "Interesting," DeWitt murmurs.
[1711] "It seems easy to explain: the victim was shot seven feet away from the table, struggled to crawl to the table, then rose with incredible strength, grabbed a handful of sugar from the table, and then collapsed. But why? What does this handful of sugar signify? What was the significance of the victim's desperate act at the end? The Vienna police had hit a dead end with this. I summarized the report," Rayne smiled at the three stunned listeners, "and made sense of these tantalizing puzzles. So I wrote a letter to Vienna, and a few weeks later, the local police commissioner replied to me, saying that the killer had been arrested before my letter arrived, but my deduction correctly unraveled the mysterious relationship between the victim and the sugar – even after the killer confessed, the Vienna police were still baffled.
[1712] "So what was your deduction?" Ehrn asked, "I can't think of any possible explanation just based on this handful of sugar."
[1713] "My mind was blank," Brooks said.
[1714] DeWitt's lips were pressed into a thin line, and he furrowed his brow in deep thought.
[1715] "And you, Mr. DeWitt?" Rayne smiled and asked.
[1716] "I don't think I understand the correct meaning of this sugar either," the stockbroker said, thinking aloud, "but one thing seems obvious: this should be a clue left by the victim to indicate the identity of the killer."
[1717] "Great!" Ren exclaimed, "One hundred percent correct, Mr. DeWitt, very good. But what does the sugar represent as a clue? This...oh, does the victim want to indicate that the killer – of course, this inference is the most absurd one – is a sugar-loving person? Or does it mean the killer is a diabetic? That doesn't seem right either. Of course, such an explanation is unsatisfactory, as this clue is clearly intended for the police, and a more reasonable idea is that the clue is more likely to be related to the police's usual training and the things they handle, so that the clue the victim desperately left has a greater chance of being established. Therefore, besides the above two explanations, the sugar must mean something else – what does sugar look like in shape? It's a white crystalline substance... So, I wrote to the Vienna police chief and said that sugar could certainly mean that the killer is a diabetic, but a more likely explanation is that the killer is a cocaine addict."
[1718] Everyone was still stunned, and DeWitt slapped his thigh and laughed, "Cocaine, yes, yes! White, crystalline, powder!"
[1719] "The suspect arrested," Ren said, "is what we call a drug addict. The Vienna police therefore gave me an official reply through the local police, of course with many polite words of praise, which need not be mentioned and are not worth mentioning. I believe this explanation is the simplest and most basic one. In this murder case, what really interests me is the incredible spiritual force the victim showed at the end. He couldn't think or act like a normal person at that moment, but faced with death, some special force triggered a sudden flash of inspiration in his mind, allowing him to make a last-ditch effort at that moment of no delay, successfully leaving a clue that indicated the identity of the killer. Therefore, we can understand – at that unique moment of death, the instantaneous power of the human mind is so magical and powerful, almost infinite."
[1720] "I think it's one hundred percent true," DeWitt said. "It's a very interesting story, Mr. Ren. I can't agree with your humility in calling your insights the most ordinary and basic deductions. I believe that only your extraordinary talent and vision can penetrate the surface of things and reach the core."
[1721] "If you lived in Vienna, you would help them solve more mysteries," Ehren also said.
[1722] The North Bremen station had passed and disappeared into the black curtain behind.
[1723] Reine sighed: "I often think that if the victim could leave some kind of information for us to track down the killer, no matter how obscure and unclear it is, then the problem of crime and punishment would be much simpler."
[1724] "Really obscure and unclear?" Brooks questioned.
[1725] "Of course, Mr. Brooks, any information is better than having no information at all."
[1726] At this point, a tall man with a low hat covering his eyes, a pale and tortured-looking face, appeared. He stumbled into the compartment, staggering towards the four conversing people. [1856] "Well, I guess I'm a detective too," the old trainman grinned, showing his white teeth. "Sir, you have your answer, the marks in the stubs won't lie!" He chuckled proudly.
[1857] Bruno frowned. "I bet the killer knew about DeWitt's habit of often taking this train instead of the normal commute train home."
[1858] "Seems like it," Sam straightened up. "Now, let's find out more about the circumstances. Mr. Rain, what happened before and after the incident tonight? Why did DeWitt come to this car?"
[1859] Rain shook his head. "I don't know the details of the incident, but I know that the car left the Whipken station not long ago, and Mike Collins –"
[1860] "Collins!" Sam exclaimed, and Burdell pushed his way forward. "Collins? Also on this train? Goodness, why didn't you say so earlier?"
[1861] "Please, Inspector, calm down... Collins either got off the train or is still on it. As soon as we discovered DeWitt's murder, I ordered the conductor to close all doors and windows, ensuring that no one could leave. Therefore, unless he got off before the body was found, he couldn't have gone anywhere."
[1862] "So, they came to this car?" Sam asked.
[1863] "Inspector, I didn't say that," Raine corrected him. "That's your one-sided assumption. It's possible, but all we saw was two people entering the car behind us."

[1894] "At that time, it seems impossible for him to take the train back to New York, so don't forget to ask the taxi drivers around the station."

[1898] "Maybe we could remember one or two," the old conductor's tone became lazy again, "if we meet them again, but we can't know their names, addresses, or even if they take this train every day."
[1899] "We don't know about occasional passengers," young conductor Thompson added.
[1900] Bruno said, "Sam, just like Collins disappeared without a trace, the killer may have acted without being noticed, then hid and waited for the train to stop, quietly opening the door on the side of the track rather than the platform. With only two conductors, they couldn't keep track of all the doors."
[1901] "That's true, anyone could have done it," Sam muttered under his breath, "I'd rather hope someone accidentally bumped into the killer standing over the body with a smoking gun in hand... Oh, where's the gun? Have you found it, Duffy?"
[1902] Detective Duffy shook his head vigorously.

[1908] The Swiss man slowly approached, his eyes darkened with exhaustion, even his usual sharp, short, and pointed beard was soft and limp.

I looked around but didn't see them, so I came back to my seat and felt a bit more at ease."
[1938] "Did you check the last car?"

[1940] "Whereabouts were you when you were looking for them?"
[1941] Lord shrugged. "I remember, I was so preoccupied that I didn't pay attention to where we were."

[1970] "You're absolutely right, Inspector, I didn't."
[1971] Sam stood up. The prosecutor returned to the carriage, walking along the aisle with the help of a chair.

Honestly, I've never met such a group of blind people; the passengers in the other carriages are even worse, they know nothing."
[1973] "Well, we still have to get everyone's name and address," Sam walked away, issuing orders, during which time Bruno and Lane sat silently, with Bruno lost in thought and Lane with his eyes closed.

[1983] "Find... Dafty!" Surprise appeared on Sam's big face, and Detective Dafty, a nearly square-shaped figure, appeared. He was probably the tallest and most muscular person in New York City, "Dafty! What are you still doing here? Get going!"
[1984] Dafty took off his hat and wiped the sweat off his forehead, smiling. "I was playing my own little detective game. I wondered if Cheryl Brown was still hiding out at the Grant Hotel, so I called the front desk to check if she was still there. I knew you'd be busy running around, so I made the call – I was betting that I could get this information to you before you left."
[1985] "Well, so what?"
[1986] "She's there, Chief!" Duffy exclaimed triumphantly, "She's there, and if that Polux kid isn't hiding out with her in the hotel, I'll grow horns and not be human!"
[1987] "Do you know when those two birds came back to the hotel?"

[1992] As Duffy was about to jump off the train, a group of unfamiliar men approached, led by a medium-sized man with light hair, trying to climb onto the train car.
[1993] "What are you doing?" Duffy called out to stop them.
[1994] "Move aside, officer, I am the county prosecutor of this area." Duffy muttered angrily and got off the train to attend to business. Bruno saw the man and greeted him warmly. The medium-sized man was the prosecutor of Bergen County named Cole. He complained jokingly about being woken up from a good sleep by Bruno's message. Bruno led Cole to the scene of the incident, where Cole inspected DeWitt's stiff corpse professionally. The tricky part came next - who should handle the case? The two prosecutors argued seriously. Bruno pointed out that although the murder took place in Bergen County, it was undoubtedly a related case to the Long Island and Hudson County cases, and it should be handled by the New York side. After both parties had presented their arguments, they stared at each other.

[1996] As the two walked forward, the entire train was as noisy as a market. A New Jersey hospital ambulance arrived, and two intern doctors jumped out, carrying DeWitt's corpse under Dr. Shilling's command. The forensic expert waved goodbye and got on the ambulance.
[1997] On the train, all passengers were crowded together, and Sam personally conducted the final registration of names and addresses. After the task was completed, the station had arranged a special car to send these people on their way. Soon, the special car roared out of the Tinic station.
[1998] "You two will have to handle this," Detective Duffy said after exchanging opinions with the two prosecutors in the front carriage. "Please help clear the passengers who left the train before the crime was discovered."
[1999] "Do your best, that's all I can say," Cole replied worriedly. "Honestly, I don't think there will be any satisfactory results, of course, innocent passengers unrelated to the crime will contact us voluntarily, but if there is a murderer among them, he will be too busy hiding... That's the situation."
[2000] "By the way, Cole, there's one more thing. Sam's men are searching along the railroad track to see if they can find a gun that the killer may have thrown out of the car. Can you spare some men to continue the search? The search will become easier as dawn approaches. You know, we've thoroughly searched DeWitt's six companions and other passengers, as well as the entire train, but we haven't found that gun yet."
[2001] Cole nodded and left the train.
[2002] The six people who had been traveling with DeWitt had been transferred to the front carriage. Sam struggled to put on his coat. "Oh, Mr. Rein, what do you think about this murder?" he asked. "Does it match your previous deductions?"
[2003] "Do you still believe," added Bruno, "that you know who killed Langstroth and Wood?"
[2004] Rein smiled slightly, the first smile since the discovery of DeWitt's death. "I not only know who killed Langstroth and Wood, but I also know who killed DeWitt."
[2005] Bruno and Sam stared at him, speechless. This was the second time - since Sam first met Rein - that he felt like a boxer who had been hit on the head, shaking his head vigorously to regain his senses. "Wow!" he exclaimed. "I surrender, I'm really impressed with you."
[2006] "But have you considered, Mr. Reen," Bruno questioned, "that we must act immediately? If you really know who the killer is, tell us so we can catch him right away. This is taking too long."
[2007] Reen's face hardened. He answered reluctantly: "You two, I sincerely apologize, but you must – despite being strange and unfeeling, right? – have faith in me. Believe me, there is no benefit in revealing X's true identity at this moment. Please be patient. I know I'm playing a dangerous game of murder, but haste makes waste, haste makes waste."
[2008] Bruno groaned in pain and looked despairingly at Sam, who sucked his finger thoughtfully. After a while, as if making a decision, Sam looked straight into Reen's clear eyes: "Well, Mr. Reen, I believe everything you say, but on the other hand, I must continue to fight for my duty. I understand that Bruno will also push forward in his position. If what I do is wrong, I'll have to swallow it like a man, as it's quite possible – given that I'm now – caught between your deduction and my personal way of doing things – that I don't know what to do."
[2009] Reen became agitated – the first time since he joined the murder investigation that he had such a strong reaction.
[2010] "But won't more people be harmed if we let this mad killer go free?" Bruno pleaded with his last argument.
[2011] "Mr. Bruno, you can trust my judgment completely," Mr. Rain said firmly, "there will be no more murders. Mr. X has completed all his plans."
[2012] Fourth Act
[2013] On the way back to New York
[2014] October 10, Saturday, 3:15 AM
[2015] Prosecutor Bruno, Detective Sam, and several police officers boarded the police car, which sped from the Tinick station straight to New York.
[2016] For a long time, the two men remained silent, lost in their own thoughts. Outside the window, the dark New Jersey countryside rushed by at high speed.
[2017] It was Bruno who broke the silence, but his words were inaudible, swallowed up by the roar of the exhaust pipe. Sam shouted, "What did you say?" The two had to lean in close to each other.
[2018] Bruno shouted into the ear of the detective, "Rene said he knows who killed DeWitt, what do you think?"
[2019] "Same old, same old," Sam called back, "just like he knew who killed Langstrith and Wood!"
[2020] "What if he does?"
[2021] "Oh, no, I believe he really does. This old coot has always been so confident, I can't quite figure him out... I'm trying to guess his reasoning. He probably thinks that Lestrange and DeWitt were always meant to be the targets, both of them, and that Wood's death was just an accident—that the killer had to do it—to shut him up, which means—"
[2022] Bruno slowly nodded, "That means the motive for the murder may go back to old grudges."
[2023] "Seems like it," Sam muttered, frustrated as the car hit a bumpy patch of road and the driver didn't brake, "Which is why Rayne said there won't be any more murders—get it? Lestrange and DeWitt are both dead, and the killer's job is done."
[2024] "Poor old guy," Bruno murmured. They both thought of DeWitt, who ended up dead anyway, senselessly—they sat quietly, the car still roaring forward.
[2025] After a while, Sam took off his hat and pounded his forehead. Bruno watched him.
[2026] "What - a headache?"
[2027] "I'm thinking about what that damn finger sign DeWitt left meant."
[2028] "Oh."
[2029] "That sign, Bruno, that sign, I can't make heads or tails of it."
[2030] "How do you know it was DeWitt's intentional sign?" Bruno asked, "Maybe it meant nothing, just an accident."
[2031] "A coincidence! You don't really think it's a coincidence, do you? Try doing that to your fingers, Bruno, and see how hard you have to work to keep it up for thirty seconds. I'd bet my bottom dollar it's not some kind of death spasm that made the fingers cross like that, Bruno. Old Schilling thought so too, that's why he made me try it... Hey, you know what?!"
[2032] Bruno chuckled nervously: "Well, the more I think about it, the more ridiculous it seems. No, it's just a silly thing to say—God, not like that."
[2033] "Well, it's hard to say."
[2034] "Yeah, who's to say for sure? But this theory—hey, Sam, I mean, I just can't believe... "
[2035] "I understand what you mean, no problem, I get it."
[2036] "Well, let's think this way first, DeWitt's strange gesture isn't an exorcism mark, but an attempt to convey some information, so we have a chance to think further. Okay, DeWitt died instantly after being shot, which we've already determined, so this information must have been intentionally left by DeWitt, and it happened before he was shot."
[2037] "It could also be that the killer deliberately made it after DeWitt died," Sam disagreed, "Like I said before."
[2038] "No, it's impossible," Bruno exclaimed, "The killer didn't do that before killing the first two people – why just DeWitt?"
[2039] "Okay, let's follow your lead first." Sam said loudly, "I'm just talking about the possibilities – all the ones that don't seem likely."
[2040] Bruno ignored Sam's self-deprecating remark: "If DeWitt left the information intentionally – doesn't that mean he knew who was going to kill him? Of course, that means he wanted to leave a clue about who the killer was, right?"
[2041] "Makes sense," Sam called out. "That's basic reasoning, my dear Bruno."
[2042] "Damn it, stop interrupting. On the other hand," Bruno continued, "DeWitt wasn't superstitious about the demonic symbols, he told you himself that he didn't believe in those ghostly things, which means… hey, Sam!"
[2043] "I get it, I get it," the chief of police exclaimed, suddenly sitting up straight. "What you mean is that DeWitt's strange gesture tells us the killer is superstitious! Wow – things are starting to make sense now! DeWitt was a smart businessman, his mind was quick, even in the moment the killer pulled the trigger…"
[2044] "Do you think Reen thought of what we're thinking now?" Bruno pondered.
[2045] "Reen?" The chief of police's excitement from earlier seemed to be dampened by a bucket of cold water, as he stroked his thick beard with his fingers. "Now that I've calmed down and thought about it again, it doesn't seem as exciting as before. This damn superstition…"
[2046] Bruno sighed deeply.
[2047] Five minutes later, Sam suddenly asked out of the blue, "Hey, do you know what jettatura means?"
[2048] "Evil eye – a Neapolitan-style legend, I think."
[2049] The two fell into a gloomy silence again, and the car continued to roar forward.
[2050] Scene Five
[2051] Seinegelwood Dwyte Mansion
[2052] October 10, Saturday, 3:40 am
[2053] A full moon hung high in the sky, and the whole Seinegelwood was still asleep. A large police car drove through the peaceful rural community, turning onto a narrow lane lined with withered old trees, with two motorcycle police officers riding on either side and a smaller police car filled with detectives following behind.
[2054] This group of people rushed to the Dwyte mansion, stopping in front of the lawn entrance. A group of people got out of the large police car, including Jenny Dwyte, Lord, Egan, Inverray, Brooks, and Jerry Raine, none of them speaking.
[2055] The motorcycle officers turned off their engines, turned their bikes around, and lazily lit cigarettes while sitting on the seats. Meanwhile, several detectives who jumped out of the smaller police car quickly surrounded Jenny and her group.
[2056] "Everyone inside the house," a detective announced with authority. "The coroner has ordered that no one is to act alone."
[2057] Erhun protested, claiming that his house was nearby and he didn't see the need to stay at the Singelwood Estate for the night. The group reluctantly entered the house, with Raine remaining outside. The officious detective simply shook his head, while another detective approached Erhun menacingly. Erhun shrugged and reluctantly followed the group. Raine followed behind Erhun with a calm smile, and the detectives trailed behind, their steps slow and lackadaisical.
[2058] The door was opened by the disheveled butler, Jurgens, who stared in confusion at the group arriving in the middle of the night. No one spoke to answer his questions, and he was forced to lead the group into the spacious colonial-style living room, where they sat down, looking tired and despondent. Jurgens, with one hand still fumbling with his buttons, turned on the lights. Raine sighed and sat down as well, still holding his cane tightly and staring intently at the others.
[2059] Uneasy, Jurgens approached Jan, who sat on a sofa, leaning on her boyfriend, Lord. The old butler stammered, "Miss DeWitt, may I ask...?"
[2060] Jan whispered, "What?"
[2061] The old butler took a step back, hesitant, but still managed to ask, "What happened? These people... I know I shouldn't ask, but where is DeWitt?"
[2062] Lord interrupted harshly, "Jurgens, get out of the way."
[2063] However, Jenny clearly answered, "He's dead, Jurgens, dead."
[2064] Jurgens' face paled, and he froze in a bent-over position, as if welcoming a guest. His bewildered eyes swept over the room, as if trying to confirm if the thunderous news was true. But all he saw were averted faces and vacant eyes, as if everyone's emotions had been drained by the cold-blooded murder that had occurred that night.
[2065] After a long silence, Jurgens said nothing and turned to leave.
[2066]A detective stepped out to block his path, "Where's Mrs. DeWitt?"
[2067]Jorgens looked at him, his eyes terrifyingly vacant. "Mrs. DeWitt? Mrs. DeWitt?"
[2068]"Yes, hey, tell me – where is she?"
[2069]Jorgens remained like a walking corpse, stiffly answering, "I think she's upstairs sleeping, sir."
[2070] "She's been up there all evening?"
[2071] "No, sir, no, sir, not like that."
[2072] "Where did she go?"
[2073] "Sir, I don't know."
[2074] "When did she come back?"
[2075] "She came back when I was already asleep. She forgot her key, so she rang the bell to wake me up to open the door."
[2076] "Oh, when was that?"
[2077] "I think it was about an hour and a half ago, sir."
[2078] "You don't know the exact time?"
[2079] "No, sir."
[2080] "Wait a minute." The detective turned to Janie DeWitt. While the detective and Mr. Jurgens were talking, the young lady had sat up straight, listening intently to their conversation. The detective was puzzled by the strange expression on her face, and he tried to sound courteous and enthusiastic, but he stumbled over his words: "I believe – Miss, should you be the one to tell Mrs. DeWitt about Mr. DeWitt's unfortunate demise? She needs to know this news, and Mr. Cole, the prosecutor, has ordered us to inform Mrs. DeWitt immediately."
[2081] "Let me tell her?" Jenny's head snapped back, and she burst into laughter. "Let me tell her?" Lord gently shook her, whispering in her ear. The fire in Jenny's eyes dimmed, and she shuddered, almost muttering, "Jorgens, go fetch Mrs. DeWitt downstairs."
[2082] The detective quickly said, "No problem, no problem. I'll call her. Uh, you - just show me the way to the room."
[2083] Jorgens left the living room like a zombie, followed by the detective. No one spoke, and Erhen paced, still wearing Perriera's coat and now wrapped it even tighter.
[2084] "I think," Raine said sympathetically, "it would be better to light the fireplace."
[2085] Erhen stood stiffly, looking around the room, shuddering suddenly, as if only now feeling the cold of the morning. His eyes showed helplessness, and he hesitated before walking to the fireplace, kneeling down, and trying to light the fire with shaking hands. After a while, the small pile of logs crackled, and sparks danced along the walls. Once he was sure the fire was burning brightly, Erhen stood up, brushed off the dust from his knees, and resumed pacing. Perriera took off his coat, and the lawyer Brooks, who had been sitting in a far corner in a large chair, moved his chair closer to the fire.
[2086] Suddenly, everyone looked up, and a faint sound came through the hallway and warm air. Everyone looked up in an unnatural way—curiously watching, waiting for what was about to happen, like statues. Soon, Mrs. DeWitt silently slid into the living room, followed by the detective and the still-zombified Jorgens.
[2087] Mrs. DeWitt's gliding gait and the way everyone stared intently were equally unusual, as if in a dream. But her appearance instantly dispelled the demonic curse of the night, and everyone relaxed. Perriera stood up, bowing politely; Ehrn scratched his head, making a few grunting sounds, as a greeting; Lord tightened his embrace of Jenny's shoulders; and Brooks walked over to the fireplace. Only Rayne remained in his original position, his head up, alert, and his sharp eyes not missing any small movement that could mean a sound in the room.
[2088] Fern DeWitt wore a foreign-style robe over her pajamas. Her black hair hung down her shoulders, and she looked even prettier than during the day: the makeup had been washed off, and the traces of age softened in the firelight. She hesitated, looking around like Jorgens, and when her gaze fell on Jenny, it retreated. She quickly crossed the room, bending over the limp body of the girl. "Jenny, Jenny," she croaked, "Oh, I am—I am..."
[2089] Jenny didn't even look at her stepmother, let alone lift her head, and she coldly said, "Get away from me."
[2090] Fern recoiled like she had been slapped by Jenny, and without saying a word, she turned to leave. The detective behind her stopped her: "Mrs. DeWitt, we have a few questions for you."
[2091] She stopped, looking helpless. Mr. Perriera quickly offered her a chair, and Furn obediently sat down, her eyes fixed on the fireplace.
[2092] The detective cleared his throat deliberately, breaking the suffocating silence: "What time did you come home tonight?"
[2093] She held her breath: "Why? What...?"
[2094] "Answer the question."
[2095] "Uh... around two o'clock."
[2096] "So, about two hours ago?"
[2097] "Yes."
[2098] "Where did you go?"
[2099] "Nowhere, just took a drive."
[2100] "A drive," the detective's voice rose with suspicion, "did anyone accompany you?"
[2101] "No one. Just me."
[2102] "What time did you leave?"
[2103] "Late dinner, around 7:30. Drove around, and then...I got lost, ended up in front of a church - St. John's Church."
[2104] "The intersection of Amsterdam Avenue and 110th Street?"
[2105] "Yes, I parked there, went inside, sat for a while, thinking... " She trailed off, and the detective waited patiently. She licked her dry lips and continued, "I circled around the city, and then found myself in front of a church."
[2106] "Mrs. DeWitt, what are you saying? You drove to New York City and then spent hours sitting in a church? When did you leave?"
[2107] "What's wrong?" she shrieked. "What's the problem? You think I killed him? Yes – I know that's what you all think, sitting here, looking at me, judging me..." She broke down in tears, her thick shoulders heaving.
[2108] "When did you leave?"
[2109] She sobbed for a while, then wiped away her tears and rasped, "Around 10:30 or 11:00, I didn't pay attention to the exact time."
[2110] "And then what happened?"
[2111] "I just went for a drive."
[2112] "How did you get back to New Jersey?"
[2113] "I took the ferry from the pier on 42nd Street."
[2114] The detective whistled and glared at her: "So you had to experience the terror of New York City rush hour traffic again, huh? Why didn't you just take the ferry from the pier on 125th Street?"
[2115] Furn did not respond.
[2116] "Hurry up," the detective urged relentlessly. "You need to explain this properly."
[2117] "Explain properly?" Her eyes darkened. "I have nothing to explain. I don't know how to get to the city, I was just... driving, mindlessly..."
[2118] "Oh, really," the detective shouted. "What were you thinking?"
[2119] She stood up, pulling her robe tightly around her. "I think you're being too pushy. What I think is none of your business. Please move aside. I'm going back to my room."
[2120] The detective stepped forward to block her, and she stopped, her face pale with anger. "No, you won't answer—"
[2121] Before the detective could speak, Reine gently interrupted him, "Honestly, I think Mrs. DeWitt is right. She's too tired and agitated right now, and any further questions—if necessary for her to answer—I think it would be better to wait until tomorrow morning."
[2122] The detective stared at Reine for a moment, then awkwardly cleared his throat, making way for him. "Alright, sir." His voice was still loud, but he reluctantly added, "Ma'am, I apologize."
[2123] Furn left, and the silence in the living room fell again.
[2124] At 4:15 am, Reine began a bizarre task.
[2125] He appeared alone in DeWitt's private study, where the Scottish-style cloak hung on a chair. He confidently searched the entire room, not only looking around with his eyes but also moving his hands everywhere. In the center of the room stood an elegant walnut desk with a carved top. Reine opened each drawer, not leaving any paper behind, carefully examining each document and record, but apparently finding nothing. He then gave up the desk and faced the safe embedded in the wall for the third time.
[2126] He tried the handle again, but the safe remained motionless. He reluctantly turned around and faced the bookshelves filled with books. He paid special attention to the gaps between the books and the shelves and occasionally pulled out a book to search through it.
[2127] After checking the books, he stood still for a moment, his bright eyes once again fixed on the safe on the wall.
[2128] He walked to the door of the study and peered out. A patrol officer was pacing in the hallway, who quickly noticed him.
[2129] "Is the butler still downstairs?"
[2130] "I'll check," the officer went downstairs and brought the stumbling Jorgens.
[2131] "What is it, sir?"
[2132] Rene leaned against the doorpost of the study: "Jorgens, old friend, do you know the combination to the study safe?"
[2133] Jorgens' eyes widened: "Me? No, sir, I don't know."
[2134] "Does Mrs. DeWitt or Miss DeWitt know?"
[2135] "No, sir, I don't think they do."
[2136] "That's odd," Rayne smiled, the detective lazily returning to the hallway, "how so, Jorgens?"
[2137] "Ah, sir, Mr. DeWitt... uh," the elderly butler seemed uncomfortable, "sir, yes, it's strange, but Mr. DeWitt hasn't let anyone else in the house touch this safe. There's another safe in the bedroom upstairs, where the jewelry of the Mrs. and Miss DeWitt are kept, but this one in the study... I think only Mr. DeWitt and his lawyer, Mr. Brooks, know the password."
[2138] "Brooks?" Rayne considered, "Would you mind asking him to come up?"
[2139] Jorgens left, followed by Leonard Brooks, with disheveled grey hair and red eyes that looked as if he hadn't slept.
[2140] "Mr. Rayne, you wanted to see me?"
[2141] "Yes. I know only you and Mr. DeWitt would know the combination to this safe, Mr. Brooks," - Brooks' sleepy eyes suddenly became alert - "Can you tell me?"
[2142] The lawyer stroked his chin in hesitation. "This is an unusual request, Mr. Rene. From a moral standpoint, I'm not sure if I should give you the combination, and from a legal standpoint...I don't know what to do. You know, the combination to this safe was told to me by Mr. DeWitt a long time ago, and he also said he kept a written memo at home, in case something happened to him, he wanted to open this safe through proper legal procedures..."
[2143] "Mr. Brooks, hearing you say that only makes me more curious," Rene said softly, "In this situation, I am even more eager to open the safe immediately. Of course, you understand that I have the right to make this request. If the district attorney were to make the same request, you would tell him, wouldn't you?" Rene's smile remained as her eyes locked onto the lawyer's tense jaw.
[2144] "If you want to see the will," Brooks said weakly, "this is official business..."
[2145] "No, Mr. Brooks, I don't want to see the will. By the way, do you know what's inside the safe? There must be some very important clues that can help us solve all the puzzles."
[2146] "Oh, no, no, I have no idea, of course, I'm often curious about what's inside, but I've never asked Mr. DeWitt."
[2147] "I think, Mr. Brooks," Mr. Raine's tone changed, and he spoke solemnly, "You'd better tell me the code."
[2148] Mr. Brooks still hesitated, avoiding Mr. Raine's piercing gaze... After a long time, he shrugged and whispered a long string of numbers. Mr. Raine watched his lips intently, nodded, and walked back to the study without saying a word, closing the door in front of Mr. Brooks.
[2149] The veteran actor quickly crossed the study to the safe, fiddling with the code wheel for a while before finally opening the small, heavy iron door. Mr. Raine paused for a moment, carefully examining the documents inside without disturbing the original arrangement... After fifteen minutes, he closed the safe, turned the code wheel, and came to the desk, holding a small envelope in his hand.
[2150] Mr. Raine sat down in the chair in front of the desk and carefully examined the envelope. The handwriting was ordinary, addressed to John DeWitt, with a postmark from the New York City Central Post Office and then delivered by the general post office to Mr. DeWitt. The date on the envelope indicated June 3rd of this year. Mr. Raine flipped the envelope over, but there was no return address on the back.
[2151] Rayne's fingers carefully inserted into the opening end of the envelope and pulled out a thin piece of ordinary note paper. Like the handwriting on the envelope, the text on the letter was also handwritten, with ink that was originally blue. The top of the note paper indicated the date: June 2nd. The letter omitted the usual greeting and only wrote John DeWitt's nickname: Jack.
[2152] The content was also brief:
[2153] Jack!
[2154] This is my last letter to you.
[2155] Every dog has its big day, and mine is coming soon.
[2156] Get ready to reap what you've sown, you may just be the first.
[2157] Similarly, the letter ended without the customary blessing, only signed by the sender's name: Martin Stoppes.
[2158] The sixth act
[2159] The Grant Hotel Suite
[2160] October 10th, Saturday, 4:05 am
[2161] In the Grant Hotel's twelfth floor, Detective Duffy stood with his broad back against the door of the suite where Theresa Brown was staying, talking to a man who looked worried and suspicious. At that moment, Chief Inspector Sam, accompanied by Bruno the prosecutor and a squad of men, came striding down the corridor. Duffy introduced the melancholy man as the hotel's security guard. Under Sam's sharp gaze, the melancholy guard looked even more melancholy.
[2162] "Any activity?" Sam asked grimly.
[2163] "Quiet as two sleeping mice," the melancholy guard said. "Looks like there won't be any problems, right, Inspector?"
[2164] "Yeah, no noise at all," the officer added. "I guess the lovebirds must have hit the sack early."
[2165] The security guard immediately put on a shocked expression. "Our hotel is respectable; we don't allow any shenanigans."
[2166] Detective Sam's tone remained unfriendly: "Does this suite have any other exits?"
[2167] "There's a door there," Officer Duffy waved his sturdy arm. "There's also an emergency exit, but I've had someone guarding the stairs. And there's someone watching the roof too, just in case."
[2168] "I don't think it's necessary to be so cautious," Prosecutor Bruno countered, his expression not entirely relaxed. "They probably won't try to escape, right?"
[2169] "Well, who knows?" Detective Sam said coldly. "Boys, are you all ready?" He checked the hallway, making sure there were no bystanders besides the police and hotel security guards. Two detectives stood guard at the doors of neighboring suites. Detective Sam suddenly pounded on the door with force.
[2170] There was no response from inside the suite. Detective Sam pressed his ear against the door, then knocked again. The gloomy security guard tried to speak up but quickly swallowed his words, pacing nervously down the hallway to avoid reality. Detective Sam's knocking continued for a long time, and he heard a faint sound from inside the room. He grinned and kept knocking and waiting. There was a click, the sound of a light switch, followed by a lazy shuffle and the sound of a latch being lifted. Detective Sam turned to look at his team, and the door opened cautiously by two inches.
[2171] Inside, there was a click, the sound of a light switch, followed by a lazy shuffle and the sound of a latch being lifted. Sam looked back at his men, and the door opened cautiously, two inches.
[2172] "Who is it? What do you want?" It was Theresa Brown's voice, a tense voice that did not know what had happened.
[2173] Sam's large foot first slipped into the two-inch gap in the door, and his thigh-sized hand pushed the door open with force. Inside the lit suite, there was a very beautiful but also very melancholy Theresa Brown, wearing a silk nightgown of sky blue and slippers of satin. She looked at Sam's fierce face in shock, took a deep breath, and recoiled. "Oh, it's Chief Inspector Sam!" Her voice was weak, as if she was startled by the unexpected visitor in the middle of the night, "Is... Is something wrong?"
[2174] "No, no, it's nothing serious," Sam said in a friendly manner while his eyes searched the room. He was now standing in the living room of the actress's suite, which was quite messy. There was an empty gold bottle and almost an empty whiskey bottle on the cabinet; on the table were half-smoked cigarette butts and a woman's pearl handbag, as well as unwashed glasses and an overturned chair... Theresa shifted her gaze from the chief inspector's face to the hallway outside, her eyes almost popping out. Outside stood Bruno the prosecutor and a row of detectives.
[2175] The door to the bedroom was now closed.
[2176] Sam grinned, "Prosecutor, let's go in and take a look - you all stay outside." Bruno entered the room and closed the door behind him.
[2177] It was only at this moment that Cheryl's instinctive calmness returned, her cheeks regained their color, and she brushed her hair.
[2178] "Well!" she said, "You've certainly chosen a good time to disturb a lady. Inspector, what's going on?"
[2179] "Don't worry, Miss," Sam put on a smile, "Are you alone?"
[2180] "What's it to you?"
[2181] "I mean—are you alone?"
[2182] "It's none of your business."
[2183] Bruno stands by the wall, watching the scene unfold, while Sam flashes his teeth and strides toward the bedroom door. The actress screams and rushes to block the door. She is furious, her bright Spanish eyes flashing. "How dare you!" she hisses. "Do you have a search warrant? You can't—"
[2184] Sam places a large hand on her shoulder and pushes her aside as the door opens and Broulux appears, blinking in the sudden light.
[2185] "Well, well," Broulux's gravelly voice says. "There's no need for all this commotion. What's going on?"
[2186] He wore a loose silk dressing gown, and the careful, leaf-fearing demeanor of the day was gone. His sparse hair stood on end, as if oiled, and his pointed beard hung limply, while his fish-like eyes were rimmed in black.
[2187] Cheryl Brown flung her head back in anger, plucked a cigarette from the clutter on the table, lit it with a match, and exhaled a cloud of smoke. She sat down, no longer pretending to be a lady, and swung her legs. Polux, who had just learned of the situation, stood alone, seeming to realize his own disheveled appearance and shifting his weight from one foot to the other, repeatedly.
[2188] Sam stared coldly at him, from one foot to the other. No one spoke.
[2189] After a while, Sam finally broke the silence: "Now, you two lovebirds, can you tell us where you've been tonight?"
[2190] Cheryl sneered, "What are you interrogating us about? Can you also tell us why you're suddenly so interested in our whereabouts?"
[2191] Sam's ugly, flushed face leaned in close to Cheryl's. "Listen up, miss," his voice was as cold as ice, "there will be a day when we'll be alone together, do you understand? — Stop acting, or I'll treat you well and make sure every bone in your beautiful body is broken. Answer me directly, no more nonsense, do you hear?"
[2192] Sam's polished, sharp eyes looked straight into hers. She chuckled: "Okay... After tonight's show, Polux came to find me, and we — we just came here directly."
[2193] "Don't play dumb with me," Sam said. Bruno, on the side, saw Polux wink at Cheryl over Sam's shoulder. "You came in around 2:30, where did you go before that?"
[2194] "Okay, you're so fierce, do you want to eat people? We of course came back to the hotel here, but I didn't say we came directly from the theater, I meant — I really don't want to tell you these things, we first went to a basement bar on 45th Street, and then came here."
[2195] "So you mean you couldn't possibly have taken the Whipken ferry tonight, right? Around midnight?"
[2196] Polux made a noise in the background.
[2197] "You were there too," Sam suddenly said. "You were both seen by someone on the New Jersey side when the ferry docked. You two."
[2198] Theresa and Polux exchanged a desperate glance. She remained calm, saying slowly, "Well, so what? It's illegal, isn't it?"
[2199] "A lot of things are illegal," Sam pressed. "Where did you go on the ferry?"
[2200] "Oh, nowhere, just to blow some air, enjoy the view."
[2201]Sam snorted coldly. “Good Lord,” he said, “Are you two idiots? Do you expect me to believe this?” He stamped his foot, “Damn it, it's annoying and nauseating to be polite and beat around the bush with you. Holy Sara, you took the ferry, got off in New Jersey, because you two were tailing DeWitt's group, right?”
[2202]Boluk stammered, “Cherie, let's confess to them. There's no other way.”
