[2205]Sam enjoyed watching the couple bicker, and he had been eyeing the pearl handbag on the table for a while. He took it over and weighed it in his hand... The internal conflict suddenly stopped, and Cherie watched the heavy handbag rise and fall in Sam's hand.
[2206] "Give it back," she yelled, angry.
[2207] "Heavy, isn't it?" Sam grinned. "Nearly a ton. I'm really curious..." Sam quickly opened the purse, reached in, and Cherie let out a wild animal-like scream, while Polux turned pale and instinctively tried to rush forward. Bruno, quick-witted, ran from the wall and stood next to Sam.
[2208] Sam pulled out a small caliber revolver with a pearl handle. He opened the gun, checked the chamber, and found three bullets. He then stuffed a pencil wrapped in a handkerchief into the barrel and found that it did not stick. Sam sniffed the revolver, shook his head, and put it back on the table.
[2209] "I have a license," the actress said, licking her lips.
[2210] "Show it to me."
[2211] She walked to the sideboard, opened a drawer, and quickly returned to the table. Sam checked the license and returned it to her. She sat down without saying anything.
[2212] "Now, it's your turn," Sam turned to Polux, "Let's be clear, you followed DeWitt's group, what did you want?"
[2213] "I – I don't understand what you're saying?"
[2214] Sam's gaze fell on the gun. "You know, this gun is here, and it's not good for little Cheryl, right?"
[2215] Cheryl took a deep breath. "What does that mean?" Polux was petrified.
[2216] "Tonight, on the Westside Line train, John DeWitt was shot and killed," Bruno answered, speaking for the first time since entering the room, "murder."
[2217] The four lips mechanically repeated the last two words spoken by Bruno, and then looked at each other with confusion and terror.
[2218] "Who did it?" The woman whispered.
[2219] "You two don't know?"
[2220] Cheryl's full lips trembled. Pollock suddenly lunged forward, startling Sam and Bruno - he rushed to the table and grabbed the revolver before Sam could react. Bruno shouted, Sam's hand reached for the holster, and the actress screamed. But Pollock didn't further escalate the situation; he held the gun barrel with the weapon reversed, so Sam's hand remained on the holster.
[2221] "Look!" Boulux exclaimed, passing the gun to Sam with trembling hands. "Look at the bullets inside—they're not real! They're blanks!"
[2222] Sam took the gun. "They're blanks," he whispered. Bruno noticed that Cheryl looked oddly at Boulux, as if she had never seen him before.
[2223] Boulux was excited and incoherent: "I changed the bullets last week, and I did it myself, Cheryl didn't know. I—I don't like her running around with real bullets, women don't pay attention to that kind of thing."
[2224] "Boulux, why only three bullets?" Bruno asked. "There's another possibility, isn't there? That the chamber was once filled with real bullets?"
[2225] "But I tell you there weren't any! I don't know why I didn't fill it with blanks, but I didn't. And anyway, we didn't take that train tonight—we only went to the dock and came back, took the next ferry to New York, Cheryl, didn't we?"
[2226] She nodded blankly.
[2227] Sam took the handbag again. "Did you buy train tickets?"
[2228] "No, we didn't even get close to the ticket counter or the station."
[2229] "But you were following the DeWitt group, right?"
[2230] Polux's left eyelid twitched nervously, a bit comical, and the twitching speed increased, but at this moment, he closed his mouth tightly, and Cherie lowered her eyes and stared at the carpet under her feet.
[2231] Sam walked into the dark bedroom and came out empty-handed a moment later. He then pretended to search the living room again, but no one spoke. Finally, he turned silently and walked heavily towards the door. Bruno said, "Please be available at any time, this is not a joke, both of you." He then followed Sam out of the room and down the hallway.
[2232] A group of detectives waiting outside eagerly greeted Sam and Bruno with their eyes, but Sam only waved his hand and led the way to the elevator. Bruno followed him reluctantly.
[2233] "Why didn't you confiscate the revolver?" Bruno asked.
[2234] Sam pressed the elevator button with a thick finger. "What good would that do us?" he said irritably. The hotel security guard came over and his frown deepened. Detective Duffy also came over and stood shoulder to shoulder with him. Sam added, "It's useless, Dr. Schiller said that DeWitt's gunshot wound was from a .38 caliber gun, while Theresa's gun is a .22 caliber."
[2235] The seventh scene
[2236] Mike Collins' Apartment
[2237] October 10, Saturday, 4:45 a.m.
[2238] Just before dawn, New York City is plunged into an incredible darkness. Police cars drive recklessly on the dark, winding roads, their headlights shining brightly. There are no pedestrians, no vehicles, and only the occasional taxi speeding by.
[2239] Mike Collins lives in a fortress-like apartment building on West 78th Street. As the police cars pull up, a man emerges from the shadows. Sam leads the way out of the car, followed by Bruno and several detectives. The man says, "Boss, he's still upstairs. He hasn't left the house since he got back."
[2240] Sam nods, and the group heads inside. An elderly uniformed attendant sits behind the desk, yawning. They wake the sleeping elevator attendant, who quickly escorts them upstairs.
[2241] They got off the elevator on the eighth floor, and another detective stepped forward, pointing to one of the doors. Everyone quietly gathered around, and Bruno sighed, glancing at his watch. "Everything covered?" Sam asked casually, "This guy's dangerous."
[2242] Sam stepped forward and rang the doorbell. There was a shaky sound, then the sound of dragging footsteps, followed by a man's gruff shout: "Who is it? Who's there?"
[2243] Sam barked, "Police! Open up now!"
[2244] There was a brief silence, followed by: "Fuckin' cops! You ain't gonna catch me alive!" A grunt, more shuffling feet, and then a sharp, crackling sound of a revolver firing. Finally, they heard a heavy object fall to the ground.
[2245] It was time to force entry. Sam took a step back, took a deep breath, and charged the door, but it was like hitting an iron board. The door didn't budge. Detective Duffy and a muscular detective, in perfect synchronization, jumped into a three-man dance, following Sam's lead as they took a step back and charged the door together. This time, the door shook, but it remained closed. "Again!" Sam shouted... until, on the fourth try, the door groaned and fell to the ground. The trio stumbled into the room, into a long, dark hallway leading to a lit bedroom.
[2246] At the junction of the hall and the bedroom, just at the door, there was a man in his pajamas, Mike Collins, holding a black and gray revolver in his right hand, with smoke still rising from the barrel.
[2247] Sam stepped heavily over the inlaid wooden floor and rushed over, kneeling down on one knee next to Collins, listening to his heartbeat.
[2248] "He's still alive!" Sam yelled, "Bring him to the bedroom!"
[2249] A group of people scrambled to carry the unconscious body into the lit bedroom, placing him on a long chair. Collins' face was pale, his eyes closed, and his mouth was twisted back, panting like a wolf. Blood flowed from the side of his head, which was as straw-like as his hair, covering half of his face and running down to his right shoulder, staining his pajamas. Sam touched the wound with his finger, instantly turning his hand red. "The bullet didn't penetrate his skull," Sam said softly, "It just grazed his head. I guess he's fainted. Damn it, terrible, shooting himself and missing by such a margin. Hey, call a doctor... Hey, Bruno, it looks like the show is over."
[2250] One of the detectives ran out to follow the command. Sam took three steps forward and picked up the revolver from the floor. "Good, a .38 caliber," he said with satisfaction, but his face immediately fell, "Only one shot, the one he used to shoot himself. The bullet flew somewhere."
[2251] "It's lodged in the wall," one of the detectives pointed out at the peeled white paint on the wall.
[2252] Sam dug out the bullet. Bruno said, "He ran down the hallway to the living room, firing as he went, hitting the wall with the bullet, and passing out at the same time." Sam looked at the deformed bullet and put it in his pocket, carefully wrapping the revolver in a handkerchief and handing it to one of the detectives nearby. At the other end of the eighth-floor corridor, there was a commotion, and they turned around to see a group of apartment residents in pajamas peeking out curiously and whispering to each other.
[2253] Two detectives ran out. The commotion suddenly rose, as the detective assigned to find the doctor pushed his way through the crowd, followed by an ordinary-looking man in a robe, carrying a black bag.
[2254] "Are you a doctor?" Sam asked.
[2255] "Yes, I live in this apartment. What's going on?"
[2256] It wasn't until the detectives reached the bench that the doctor noticed Collins lying on it. Without hesitation, he squatted down. "Give me hot water," he said, examining the wound for a while, waving his fingers. A detective rushed into the bathroom and brought out a large basin of hot water.
[2257] After about five minutes of treatment, the doctor stood up. "Severe abrasions," he said. "He'll regain consciousness any moment." He cleaned the wound, disinfected it, and cleaned the blood-stained right side of Collins' head. With the unconscious patient's perfect cooperation, the doctor successfully performed a second cleaning, sutured the wound, and bandaged it properly. "He needs to be taken to the hospital for further treatment as soon as possible. This is just a temporary measure. He'll have a terrible headache and feel terrible." Suddenly, Collins moaned weakly and trembled all over, then opened his eyes with a look of pain and tears.
[2258] "He's fine," the doctor said calmly, gathering his medical kit.
[2259] The doctor left. One of the detectives helped Collins sit up, propping a pillow under his head. Collins moaned again, touching his head with his pale hand and falling back onto the bench when he felt the bandage on his head.
[2260] "He's okay," the doctor said, finishing his medical kit.
[2261] "Collins," the chief of police spoke up, sitting next to the victim. "Why did you commit suicide?"
[2262] Collins licked his cracked tongue and now looked pitiful and ridiculous, with a dried bloodstain on his right cheek. "Water," he murmured.
[2263] Sam looked up, and a detective immediately brought a glass of water, lifting Collins' head, and letting the icy liquid flow down the Irishman's throat.
[2264] "Can you tell us now, Collins?"
[2265] Collins gasped: "You caught me, didn't you? You caught me, and I ruined..."
[2266] "Are you admitting guilt?"
[2267] Collins bit his tongue, nodding silently, still looking shaken, but suddenly raising his eyelids, regaining a hint of his former toughness: "Admit what guilt?"
[2268] Sam smiled slightly. "Never mind, Collins, don't put on that innocent act, you know what you did. You killed John DeWitt, that's the crime."
[2269] "I – killed –" Collins was stunned, then tried to sit up straight, only to twist in pain. Sam pushed him back down onto the couch. Collins yelled, "What are you talking about? I killed DeWitt? Who killed him? I didn't even know he was dead! You're crazy, or do you want me to be the scapegoat?"
[2270] Sam's expression was a bit perplexed. Bruno shifted, and Collins turned to him. Bruno spoke plainly, "Listen carefully, Collins. There's no point in lying. You heard the police come in and immediately shouted, 'You won't catch me alive,' and tried to commit suicide. Is that something an innocent person would say? And just a few minutes ago, you said, 'You caught me, didn't you?' That's not an admission of guilt? These all expose your lies, your words and actions confirm that you are the criminal."
[2271] "I didn't kill DeWitt, I swear it!"
[2272] "Then why were you acting like you were expecting the police?" Sam interjected sternly.
[2273] "Because... " Collins clenched his teeth, glaring at Bruno. "It's none of your business," he said dismissively. "I had no idea there was a murder. The last time I saw him, he was fine. It's not like I had a gun to his head or anything." Suddenly, a sharp pain seized him, and he clasped his head, crying out loudly.
[2274] "So you admit to seeing DeWitt tonight?"
[2275] "Of course I did. Lots of people saw me on the train. Was he killed on the train?"
[2276] "Stop acting," Sam said, "how convenient that you just happened to be on that same train to Newburgh?"
[2277] "I followed DeWitt there. I followed him all night, from when he left the Ritz with his guests, to when they all arrived at the station. I've been trying to find him for a while, even when he was in detention, I tried to visit him. So I bought a ticket and got on the same train. After the train started, I went to find DeWitt – he was sitting with his lawyer, Brooks, and two other people, one of whom was Ehring, and I don't know the other. We got into an argument right away."
[2278] "Of course, of course, we know all that," the inspector said. "What happened after you saw DeWitt on the train?"
[2279] Collins glared with bloodshot eyes. "I wanted him to pay for the damage that Lengstrith's rotten information caused me. Lengstrith made me fall flat on my face. DeWitt and Lengstrith run the company together, and DeWitt is the legal representative. I – I desperately need that money, but DeWitt wouldn't listen to me. He just kept saying no, no, no... oh, so cold like a reptile.” His voice was filled with barely contained anger, "I almost knelt down to him, but it was still no, no, no."
[2280] "Where did you have this conversation?"
[2281] "We talked in the rear car... I had to give up and get off when the train stopped at a place called Richfield Park. I jumped off the side door on the tracks and closed the door behind me. After crossing the tracks, I realized the last train back to the city had already left. I had to call a taxi and come straight back here. Damn it, I swear."
[2282] Collins leaned back on the pillow, panting heavily. "Was DeWitt still in that car when you jumped off?"
[2283] "Yes, he was looking at me...” Collins bit his lip. "I – I hate this guy,” he stammered. "But not enough to kill him – oh, no...”
[2284] "You think we're going to believe anything you say?"
[2285] "I'm telling you I didn't kill him!" Collins's voice rose to a scream. "I saw him wipe his forehead with a handkerchief and stuff it back in his pocket, pull open the door to the dark car. God can be my witness, I saw him, I swear to you!"
[2286] "Did you see him sit down?"
[2287] "No, I left right away, didn't I?"
[2288] "Why didn't you get off through the lit carriages at the front, through the door the conductor opened?"
[2289] "I didn't have time, the train had been stopped for a while."
[2290] "You said you hated him, right? So you had a big argument, right?" The officer asked again.
[2291] Collins exclaimed, "Are you trying to pin the blame on me? I told you everything, Sam, about what we talked about. Of course, I was upset, and so was DeWitt, I guess he went to the last carriage to calm down, and he was still red in the face."
[2292] "Did you take your revolver, Collins?"
[2293] "No."
[2294] "And you didn't go to the last carriage?"
[2295] "Good heavens, of course not!" The Irishman's anger flared up again.
[2296] "You said you bought a ticket at the ferry terminal to continue tracking Dewitt, show me the ticket."
[2297] "The ticket is in the pocket of the coat hanging in the closet by the aisle."
[2298] Detective Duffy went to the closet to find the ticket and soon brought it over, it was a ticket from Whipken to Seinegelwood.
[2299] "How come the ticket wasn't checked by the ticket collector, huh?" Sam asked.
[2300] "The ticket collector didn't come to collect the ticket before I got off the train."
[2301] "Alright," Sam gets up, stretches his arms, and yawns. Collins sits up straighter, looking much better, and he pulls a cigarette out of his pajama pocket. "Well, Collins, how are you feeling now?"
[2302] Collins murmurs, "Better, but my head still hurts."
[2303] "Well, you're much better, and I'm glad," Sam says sincerely, "So, we don't need to call an ambulance."
[2304] "Ambulance?"
[2305] "Of course. Now get up, put on your clothes, and come back to headquarters with me."
[2306] Collins dropped his cigarette. "You...you're arresting me on a murder charge? It's not me, I've told you! I'm telling the truth, Chief—oh, for heaven's sake..."
[2307] "Nonsense," Sam winked at Bruno. "I'm not arresting you for murdering DeWitt. We just need you as an important witness."
[2308] Act Eight
[2309] Uruguayan Consulate
[2310] October 10, Saturday, 10:45 a.m.
[2311] Rine strolled leisurely through Bedford Park, his black cape billowing behind him like a cloud. He walked with a brisk step, tapping the ground with his cane and inhaling the fresh, salty morning air. The special, pleasant ocean scent and the warm sunlight on his face made him feel good. He stopped at the park's wall, watching a flock of seagulls dive into the oily waves, pecking at the orange peels floating on the surface, mistaking them for fish. Out at sea, a sailing ship with a triangular sail tilted its hull, slowly sailing across the water. A Hudson River ferry sounded its horn. A gust of sea breeze blew, and Rine took a deep breath, pulling his cape tighter around him.
[2312] Rine sighed, glancing at his watch, and turned, walking across the park and straight to Bedford Square. Ten minutes later, he was comfortably seated in a simple room, smiling at the small, dark-skinned South American behind the desk. The diminutive, dark-skinned man named Juan Ayoze, with a small mustache and a bunch of flowers pinned to his lapel, was a typical short and bouncy type with bright white teeth contrasting sharply against his dark brown skin and black eyes that darted around.
[2313] "It's an honor, Mr. Rine," the little man spoke excellent English, "to have you grace our humble consulate. I heard of your great reputation when I was still a young attaché..."
[2314] "Dear Mr. Ayoze, you're too kind," Rine replied politely. "You must be very busy after your vacation, yet you still found time to see me. I'm here today to discuss a very special case I'm involved in. There have been a series of related murders in New York City. Have you heard about it during your time in Uruguay?"
[2315] "Mr. Rine, you're talking about murder?"
[2316] "Yes, there have been three in recent times. Out of curiosity and at the invitation of the local prosecutor, I've taken on the case unofficially. So far, my personal investigation has uncovered some delicate clues that I'm not sure will lead to the truth, but I have a strong reason to believe that your help will be crucial to the validity of these clues."
[2317] Ajo smiled. "Mr. Rene, you can count on me. Just tell me what you need."
[2318] "Have you heard of Felipe Machado? A Uruguayan?"
[2319] A glint of recognition shone in the small, cunning consul's eyes. "Web of intrigue, you say? Then, Mr. Rene, Machado is a fine gentleman. I've met him and spoken with him. What do you want to know about him?"
[2320] "I want to know how you met him and anything interesting about him."
[2321] Ayoce spread his hands. "I'll start from the beginning, Mr. Raine, and you can decide which parts may be helpful for your investigation... Felipe Machado is a staff member of the Uruguayan judiciary system, an extremely competent and reliable individual."
[2322] Raine raised his eyebrows.
[2323] "A few months ago, Machado was sent to New York to represent the Uruguayan police in tracking down an escaped convict from the Montevideo prison. The convict is a male named Martin Stops."
[2324] Raine sat up straight. "Martin Stops... I'm becoming more and more interested, Mr. Ayoce. The name sounds Anglo-Saxon, so why was this person imprisoned in Uruguay?"
[2325] "Personally—" Ayoce sniffed the flowers on his lapel, "I have a clear understanding of the criminal case, which was relayed to me by Mr. Machado himself. He came to New York with the complete case file of Martin Stops. Moreover, he shared all the details he knew with me."
[2326] "Please continue, Mr. Ayoş."
[2327] "It goes back to 1912, when a young prospector - Martin Stoppes, who had a complete geological education and may have had mechanical training - was charged with the murder of his young Brazilian wife by the Uruguayan court and sentenced to life imprisonment. The evidence was compelling because his three mining partners testified against him. At the time, they owned a mine inland, far from the capital Montevideo, which could only be reached by sailing down the river for a long distance and passing through a dense forest. His three companions testified unanimously that they witnessed the murder, and it took all three of them to subdue Stoppes, tie him up, and take him down the river on a boat to the police. The woman's body, they also carried out - left exposed to the scorching sun for days, a sight too gruesome to bear; in addition, Stoppes' two-year-old daughter, a baby, was also with them; the murder weapon was not left behind - a machete, a South American specialty. Stoppes did not deny it, and at the time, he was in a state of mental confusion, lacking the basic ability to assert his actions. He was convicted and sentenced to prison, while the two-year-old daughter was handed over to a convent in Montevideo.
[2328] "Stoppes behaved well in prison, serving as a model prisoner. He gradually recovered his sanity, seemed resigned to his prisoner status, and did not cause trouble, stir up trouble, and never mixed with other prisoners."
[2329] Lane asked, "Was there any evidence of a motive for the murder during the trial?"
[2330] "It's strange, there was none. The three companions of Stoppes speculated that the murder was due to an argument between Stoppes and his wife, and he killed her by accident. They testified that they were not present at the scene of the crime in the small wooden house when the incident occurred, but heard the cries and rushed over to witness Stoppes stabbing the woman in the head with a machete, as if Stoppes was in a state of rage and loss of control."
[2331] "Please continue."
[2332] Ayoce sighed. "After a twelve-year prison term, it was a complete surprise to the police when Stoppes managed to escape and succeeded. This escape plan was clearly the result of several years of planning, with all relevant details taken into account. Are you interested in the details of the escape, Mr. Ayoce?"
[2333] "No, thank you, Mr. Ayoce."
[2334] "But he suddenly vanished, as if the earth had opened up and swallowed him. We searched all of South America, but there was not a trace of this man. It is generally assumed that he may have escaped into the depths of the terrible forest inland, where he met his end. That's all I know about Martin Stoppes... Mr. Lane, would you like a real Brazilian coffee?"
[2335] "Oh, thank you for the offer, but no."
[2336] "Or perhaps you would like to try our Uruguayan specialty, mate tea?"
[2337] "Thank you, but no. Can you tell me more about Machado?"
[2338] "Oh, yes, according to official records, Stoppes' three companions sold their mine, which was a rich vein, during the war. I believe the mine produced high-purity manganese, which was an important military industrial material during the war. So, they sold the mine for a good price and went back to America with a lot of money."
[2339] "Mr. Ayois, you mean they went back to America?" Rayne asked, startled, "Are they Americans?"
[2340] "Oh, I'm sorry, I forgot to tell you their names. They were Harry Lenz, John DeWitt, and...oh, yes, William Crocker..."
[2341] "Please wait a moment," Rayn's eyes sparkled, "Did you know that the two victims I mentioned in the consecutive murder case were partners in DeWitt-Lunsdorf Securities, which are the two people you just mentioned, Lunsdorf and DeWitt?"
[2342] Ayoce's eyes almost popped out. "What! So the prophecy is …"
[2343] "Your meaning is —" Rayn asked eagerly.
[2344] Ayoce Consul spread his hands. "In July this year, the Uruguayan police received an anonymous letter with a postmark from New York. Later, DeWitt admitted that he wrote it. The letter indicated that the fugitive Stoppes was in New York and suggested that the Uruguayan police send someone to investigate. Of course, despite the frequent changes in the Uruguayan government, they immediately retrieved the archives from that year, and Machado was responsible for this investigation. Machado suspected that the informant was one of the three companions of Stoppes at the time, so he came here to ask me for help. After tracking, Machado found that Lunsdorf and DeWitt indeed lived in this city and had a certain social status. He also tried to find out the whereabouts of William Crocker, the third companion who mined with Stoppes at the time, but there was no news. All we know is that after returning to North America, Crocker and the other two parted ways, whether it was because of a disagreement or because he wanted to enjoy the wealth alone, we don't know — I'm completely unaware of that either. In short, that's all I know."
[2345] "So, Machado went to see DeWitt and Lunsdorf, right?" Rayn politely asked.
[2346] "Yes, he first found DeWitt, informed him of his purpose and showed him the anonymous letter. DeWitt hesitated for a moment before admitting that he wrote the letter. DeWitt invited Machado to stay at his home during the investigation in the United States and to use his home as the headquarters of the investigation. Machado, of course, had to first figure out why DeWitt knew that Stoppes was in New York. DeWitt took out a threatening letter, signed by Stoppes, threatening to seek revenge with blood for blood -"
[2347] "Please wait a moment," Rayne took out his leather folder and took out the letter he had taken from DeWitt's safe, handing it to Ayo, "Is this the letter?"
[2348] The consul looked at it and nodded heavily. "Yes, Machado showed me this letter in his report, took a photo of it as a copy, and returned it to DeWitt himself."
[2349] "DeWitt, Langstroth, and our special envoy Machado discussed several times in Westengelwood. Of course, Machado hoped to immediately contact local law enforcement to seek assistance. He would be completely alone in investigating here, but DeWitt and Langstroth strongly opposed letting the American police intervene, arguing that once the news is exposed, their past hardships and dirty murder cases would inevitably be disclosed by the media... Naturally, Machado was in a dilemma and came to consult with me. Considering their current position and social status, we finally reluctantly agreed to their request. DeWitt and Langstroth both said that they had received similar threatening letters about five years ago, from New York, but they didn't take them seriously and tore them up on the spot. But this letter made DeWitt feel uneasy, and the threat was stronger than before, so he kept it.
[2350] "I'll try to make it short, Mr. Rayne. Machado investigated here without a clue for about a month, reported his results to me, informed DeWitt and Langstroth, and then decided to terminate this investigation and return to Uruguay."
[2351] Raine thought carefully and asked, "Do you know what happened to the missing Crockett you mentioned?"
[2352] "Machado learned from DeWitt that they had just returned from Uruguay to this place, and Crockett left them without explanation. DeWitt and Lundström also said that they occasionally received information from Crockett in the early years, mostly from Canada, but they emphasized that they had not heard from him for six years. Crockett has been out of touch."
[2353] "Of course," Raine said quietly, "we can only rely on the information provided by the two deceased. Mr. Ayo, is there any mention of Stopp's daughter's later messages in your records?"
[2354] Ayo shook his head: "All we know is that she left the convent or was taken away by someone — the details are unknown — around the age of six. From then on, there has been no further news."
[2355] Raine sighed, standing up and standing in front of the small consul's desk. "Dear Mr. Ayo, your actions today are those of a brave knight defending justice. Please accept my respect."
[2356] Ayoze smiled, revealing a row of white teeth: "Mr. Rene, your praise is very flattering to me."
[2357] "If you wish, you can -" Rene straightened his shawl and continued, "help justice more effectively. Will you kindly send a telegram to the relevant government agencies in your country to request that they transmit Stopp's fingerprint data, if there was a record, as well as his file photo from that year and all his complete information? Also, regarding the missing William Crockett, I am also interested. Will you also take care of it, as mentioned earlier ..."
[2358] "I will send the telegram immediately."
[2359] "I think, with such a small but thriving country, your country should not lack such modern equipment," Rene smiled and said as they walked towards the door.
[2360] Ayoze deliberately put on an expression of surprise: "Oh, of course! The photos will be clearly transmitted to you through modern equipment, and my country has no less of the equipment you can see in other countries."
[2361] "This trip -" Raine bowed deeply, bidding farewell, "has truly been enlightening." He walked down the street towards Bedley Park.
[2362] "Enlightening." Raine repeated softly.
[2363] Act IX
[2364] Hamlet Manor
[2365] October 12, Monday, 1:30 PM
[2366] With Quincy leading the way, Sam followed the winding corridors to the concealed elevator. The elevator, resembling a lunar rocket, carried them up from the interior of the Hamlet Manor's main tower, stopping at a square platform near the top. In front of them was an ancient stone staircase, resembling the Tower of London. Sam followed Quincy up the winding stairs, which ended at an oak door with an iron latch around its waist. Quincy and the heavy iron latch struggled for a while, finally managing to open it. With a great effort, he pushed the door open, revealing the stone battlements of the tower's roof.
[2367] Raine lay almost naked on a bear skin, with his arms resting on his forehead, shielding himself from the intense midday sun.
[2368] Sam stopped, and Quincy left with a smile. Sam was stunned, almost unable to believe that the bronze, incredibly young, and muscular body lying there belonged to Philip Raine. He lay there, smooth and glistening except for a thin golden coat of hair around his lower abdomen. His bronze skin, solid muscles, and long, smooth body indicated that he was at the peak of his life. It was only when Sam's gaze slowly rose from this incredibly beautiful body to the gray hair above that he felt a jarring discord.
[2369] The only thing covering the old actor now was a white waistcloth, and his brown feet were bare. A folding chair with soft cushions was placed nearby.
[2370] Sam shook his head a little sadly, pulling his coat tighter. The weather in October New York was already quite cold, and the biting wind struck the tower's roof unprotected. Sam approached Raine's body, getting closer and seeing it more clearly. Raine's skin was indeed incredibly smooth, and in such cold wind, not a single goosebump appeared.
[2371] Some strange alertness made Ren open his eyes, or perhaps because Sam blocked the sunlight, making Ren aware of it.
[2372] "Hi, Chief Inspector!" Ren sat up and quickly came to his senses, embracing his long and solid legs, "It's really surprising to see you. Please forgive my disheveled appearance. Pull that recliner over and sit down. Of course, if you want to," he said with a smile, "take off your clothes and lie on this bear skin with me..."
[2373] "Oh, no, no, thank you," Sam hurriedly sat on the recliner, afraid of being outdone, "in such cold wind?" He smiled derisively, "It's not my business, but Mr. Ren, how old are you?"
[2374] Ren blinked in the sunlight: "Sixty."
[2375] Sam shook his head again: "I'm only forty-five, and it's really embarrassing - Mr. Ren, this is the truth - I don't have the guts to undress in front of you. Compared to you, I look like an old man."
[2376] "Inspector, maybe you're too busy to take care of your body," Rayen said lazily, "I have the time and opportunity, look around -" he waved his hand at the exquisite landscape like a fairy tale, "I can do whatever I want here, and I still wrap this waist cloth, purely because that old Quincy's brain doesn't work. He might faint on the spot if I didn't cover this - this private part of me. Poor old Quincy, I've been trying to persuade him to sunbathe with me for twenty years, you can imagine how interesting it would be to see two old men lying together! But he's a hard and stubborn old man, I believe he doesn't even know how old he is."
[2377] "You are the most peculiar person I have ever met in my life," Sam said sincerely, "Sixty years old…"
[2378] "Well, let's get back to the topic, there has been some progress, and I came here to report these new developments - especially the most important one."
[2379] "I think it's Collins, right?"
[2380] "Exactly. I think Bruno has told you about our raid on Collins' apartment last Tuesday morning, hasn't he, Inspector?"
[2381] "Yes, to let him enjoy the sweetness of life." Sam joked, then the hardened detective softened, "I feel like a rookie, coming to you, bringing you the little bit of information we've gathered in the endless fog, and I believe you've already figured out everything.”
[2382] "Dear Inspector, let's be honest, don't be offended. For a long time, you've had doubts about me, thinking I'm just bluffing, not really able to see the core of these murders. That's a very natural and reasonable idea. In fact, even now, you still can't be sure if I've been silent out of ignorance or out of hiding something. However, you have such faith in me, and that's a surprise and heavy praise for me. Inspector, I sincerely want to tell you that we have been standing together in this terrible fog, now and in the future, until we part the fog and see the light again."
[2383] "Yes, if there's ever a day like that." Sam said gloomily, "Well, let's talk about Collins. This bird, we've exposed him and found out why he went crazy trying to get back the money he lost in the stock market. He used his official position in handling tax returns to embezzle federal taxes!"
[2384] "Really?"
[2385] "Absolutely, so far, he's embezzled over $100,000, and that's not even the end of it. The exact amount will need to be further investigated, but it's not a small sum. Mr. Rayne, he seems to have 'borrowed' federal funds to play the stock market and lost, so he had to dig himself deeper and deeper. Coincidentally, Longstreet gave him that piece of bad information, telling him to buy international metal stocks, and this fool gambled his last $50,000 on it - it was his last desperate attempt to make up for the previous losses and the money he had embezzled. The IRS seems to have noticed Collins' shady dealings and has been secretly auditing his accounts, which is why the boy panicked."
[2386] "How did Collins manage to avoid the tax office's public investigation? Inspector, what kind of pull does he have?"
[2387] Sam pursed his lips: "It's easy for him. For several months, he has forged documents to avoid exposure of the embezzlement and bribed some high-ranking political figures. But this only dragged on for a while and soon ran out of options. He had no way out."
[2388] "This really provides an insight into human nature." Lane said softly, "This man is impulsive, greedy, and easily angered. Perhaps in his life, he has been successful in swindling others and using his political power to influence things... but now, he has to beg for mercy, as Bruno told me! A failure, Inspector, a completely hopeless failure, he has to pay for his crimes."
[2389] Sam didn't seem to feel sorry for him: "Maybe, but the case is enough to keep him busy - of course, it's all circumstantial evidence, but it's enough. For example, the motive, everyone knows he hates Lunsford and DeWitt. He killed Lunsford for revenge, thinking Lunsford betrayed him; as for killing DeWitt, it's because his embezzlement was about to be exposed, and DeWitt refused to take Lunsford's mess, so Collins was trapped between a rock and a hard place and decided to kill him for a quick fix. Based on all the circumstantial evidence, the police believe that Collins, with a high probability, is the one who killed Lunsford and DeWitt, and it is also possible that he killed Wood. It was easy for him to get on the Mohegan ferry, and he could have slipped off the ship unnoticed when it docked. We checked his whereabouts that night, and Collins couldn't provide a clear alibi..."
[2390] "In court, the district attorney's speech was very persuasive," Lane said, stretching his long and sturdy arms and smiling, "I have no doubt that Collins will be found guilty, but Inspector, have you seriously considered the situation at the time? At five in the morning, the police suddenly knocked on the door to arrest him, and Collins, still half-asleep, might have thought that his embezzlement had been exposed and that he would be arrested for embezzlement. If we put ourselves in his shoes at the time, his attempt to commit suicide and his shouting to prevent you from 'catching him alive' are very reasonable, isn't it?"
[2391] Sam scratched his head. "This is exactly what Collins said this morning when we interrogated him about embezzlement. How do you know that?"
[2392] "Well, Sergeant, isn't it obvious to anyone?"
[2393] "I feel," Sam said cautiously, "that you believe what Collins said is true, and you don't think he's the one we're looking for, right? To be honest, I came here today partly because Bruno asked me to ask for your opinion. You know, we're planning to charge him with murder, but Bruno is afraid of being bitten by a snake again."
[2394] "Sergeant Sam," Renner stood up with his bare legs and puffed out his tanned chest, "Bruno cannot charge Collins with murdering DeWitt."
[2395] "I knew you'd say that." Sam clenched his fist and looked at Renner with disappointment. "But you have to think about our position. Have you seen the newspaper? The scathing and sarcastic criticism about the wrongful prosecution of DeWitt? Now it's even worse, they're making a big deal out of DeWitt's murder again, making us hide like thieves from the reporters. I can tell you privately that my job is almost at risk, not just today before I came here, but this morning I was scolded by the chief of police."
[2396] Reen looked up at the distant river. "I'm doing this,” he said softly, "to help you and Bruno. You won't think I'll say everything I know, right? In fact, the game is almost over, Inspector, we'll soon hear the sound of the long horn; as for your job... If you quickly bring the real killer to the chief's face, I don't believe he can blame you."
[2397] "I'll quickly bring—"
[2398] "Yes, Inspector.” Reen leaned against the rock wall, "Tell me what progress you've made."
[2399] Sam didn't reply immediately, looking somewhat reluctant. "Mr. Reen, I have no intention of forcing you to say anything, but since the first murder case, this is the third time I've seen you be very certain about who the killer is or not, I'm curious why you're so sure Collins isn't the murderer?"
[2400] "Well,” Reen remained calm, "it's a long story, Inspector, but on the other hand, I feel that it's time to actually prove things. So, are you willing to go to the scene with me this afternoon to investigate the possible evidence of Collins' involvement?"
[2401] Sam smiled with relief. "Mr. Rene, hearing you say that, I feel much better... As for other new developments, there have been a few. First, Dr. Schiller's detailed autopsy report on DeWitt's body is out, and the bullet has been removed, which matches the caliber he initially judged. Second, the district attorney in Bergholm County has assisted in tracking down the passengers who were on the train before the body was found, but they found nothing. Both counties have searched the entire railway track, but found no trace of the gun. Of course, Bruno believes it's because the weapon was never discarded; since Collins killed DeWitt, the murder weapon would have been the revolver in Collins' hand that morning. We conducted a ballistic analysis, comparing Collins' gun to the bullet in DeWitt's body, and found that they didn't match, of course, which doesn't prove Collins' innocence, but he could have used another gun to kill DeWitt. Bruno's theory is that if Collins used another gun, he could easily have brought it with him on the taxi, and thrown it into the Hudson River while the car was on the ferry. "
[2402] "That's an interesting coincidence," Rene said. "Inspector, please continue."
[2403] "Oh, alright. We also questioned the taxi driver who took Collins back to New York that night, to see if the car took the ferry and if Collins got off. The driver couldn't provide any details, only remembering that Collins got in the car just as the train left the Richfield Park station. The third point of progress isn't much of a progress, as we didn't find anything interesting while going through Lunsford's business and personal files. Fourthly, there's something intriguing, we found a check stub while going through DeWitt's files - two checks every year for the past fourteen years - made out to a man named William Crocker."
[2404] Rene didn't seem surprised, his gray eyes growing hazy as he stared at Sam's mouth. "William Crocker, huh... Inspector, you are a major information provider. So, what about the check amounts? And the bank where they were cashed or exchanged?"
[2405] "Well, none of the checks were for less than $15,000, although the amounts varied, and they were all cashed at the same bank - the Montreal Development Trust Bank in Canada."
[2406] "Canada? That's more and more interesting, Sergeant, what about the signature on the check - was it in DeWitt's name or the company's name?"
[2407] "It was in the company's name, both DeWitt and Lansdreight signed it, and we noticed that too. We thought DeWitt was being blackmailed, now it looks like both of them were involved, and these payments were not recorded in the company's semi-annual financial records, they paid from their private accounts in a 50-50 split. Moreover, there is no record of this payment in the tax records - we checked everything."
[2408] "Did you trace this Crocker?"
[2409] "Reen sir! Sergeant, we have been driving the Canadians crazy, we found these stubs and went after them right away. The situation is also interesting, the investigation report we received from Montreal says that the drawer is a man named William Crocker - of course, he signed the back of each check... "
[2410] "There was no endorsement on the account? Was the signature the same person?"
[2411] "Absolutely the same person. As I said, we found that this Crockett person deposited the money in various places in Canada by money order and then withdrew it by check. [2412] "We of course immediately followed this lead, but the result of the investigation was that there was nothing in the mailbox, and none of the postal workers could remember who had rented the box before, only that it was now empty. We then focused our investigation on this rented mailbox, and the rent for the mailbox was prepaid for the following year - of course, by mail."
[2413] "It's really annoying, isn't it?" Reen said, "I can imagine how you and Bruno must have been frustrated at the time."

[2423] Rayne smiled again, gazing at the Hudson River winding through the trees like a blue line, took a deep breath, and put on his flat boots.

[2425] The two headed towards the stairs. Sam smiled at Rayne's handsome bare chest.

[2434] The diminutive makeup artist, still wearing his leather apron, entered the library with a large Manila envelope in his hand. [2446] Scene 10
[2447] Near Bogotá station
[2448] October 12th, Monday, 6:05 p.m.
[2449] After hours of searching without results, Sam looked dejected. Earlier, Sam's unwavering confidence in Rain's logical reasoning and prophetic abilities seemed to loosen up after hours of relentless blows. This group of people equipped with various tools, like the brave Spanish explorers of old, searched the New Jersey rivers along the West Line track all afternoon. Sam also volunteered to climb up and down with the search equipment, but his face grew longer and longer. Rain remained calm, occasionally pointing out the direction for the search team, and seemed confident in his proposal to search along the rivers along the line.
[2450] At this point, the sky had completely darkened, and some anxious staff members had searched close to a river near the Bogotá city area, hastening their search speed. Sam conjured up more equipment and high-powered spotlights sweeping the railroad tracks and the still-flowing river surface. An iron contraption like a super spoon was also moved to this area to support the search. Rain and the gloomy Sam stood side by side, staring intently at the mechanical movements of the staff.
[2451]"It's like looking for a needle in a haystack," Sam grumbled. "Looks like there's no hope, right, Mr. Rayne?"
[2452]Just as Sam's words of despair seemed to be heard by the heavens, a loud cry was heard, coming from a boatman about twenty feet away from the railroad. The shout interrupted Rayne's response. The spotlight quickly moved to the boat, and the "spoon" came over, as usual, to scoop up a pile of mud, grass, and stones. However, in the light of the spotlight, there was a glimmering thing among them.
[2453]A triumphant shout rang out instantly. Sam rushed down the slope, and Rayne calmly followed behind.
[2454]"What's that?" Sam yelled.
[2455]The boatman held the shiny thing in his hand, covered in mud. Sam turned to look at Rayne, who had arrived beside him, with a look of respect in his eyes, then shook his head and took the thing to examine it.
[2456] "Isn't it a .38 caliber?" Mr. Lane asked gently.
[2457] "That's it!" Sam exclaimed, "What a stroke of luck! Look, one of the chambers is empty. I'll bet my last doughnut that when we fire this gun, the bullet will match the one that killed DeWitt one hundred percent!"
[2458] Sam gently caressed the gun, wrapped it in a handkerchief, and put it in his coat pocket.
[2459] "Come on, everyone!" he called to the exhausted search party, "We found it! We can go home and rest comfortably now!"
[2460] Sam and Mr. Lane walked along the railway towards a row of police cars, heading towards the one that had carried them throughout the afternoon.
[2461] "Well, sir," Sam said, "I'll be straight with you, we found the murder weapon, right here in the river where the train passed that night. Based on the location of the discovery, it's not hard to conclude that the gun was thrown from the car into the river after the murder, of course by the killer himself."
[2462] "There's another possibility," Rayne added, "The killer got off the train in Bogotá or before, walked to this area, and threw the gun into the river. I'm just —" Rayne emphasized, "pointing out that possibility. Of course, the likelihood of it being thrown directly from the car is much greater."
[2463] "You're always so thorough, aren't you?" said Rayne, "Of course, I completely agree with what you're saying..." As the two reached the car, leaning against the black door to catch their breath.
[2464] Rayne reiterated, "Regardless, based on the location of the revolver's recovery, Collins' involvement is now completely ruled out."
[2465] "You mean Collins is innocent?"
[2466] "Inspector, this may be a more reasonable inference. You see, the train arrived at Richfield Park Station at 12:30, and Collins left by taxi before the train started—that's important. The taxi driver's testimony confirms this alibi. The taxi returned to New York City, in the opposite direction. The revolver was thrown out of the train, and it couldn't have been before 12:35 when the train arrived at this riverbank, even if it was thrown by the killer who walked here, the time would only be later than the train's arrival time, that's for sure. So, Collins didn't have a chance to get on the train or walk to this riverbank and throw the weapon in within the short time the train was stopped at Richfield Park Station! This river is at least a mile away from the station, which is two miles round trip. Of course, we could imagine that the gun was thrown into the river, some time after the murder, meaning Collins came back and threw it after a couple of hours, which is not impossible in general, but this scenario is too special. Moreover, since Collins took a taxi back to his apartment in New York, his whereabouts have been under the control of the police, so to speak—Collins is not the killer."
[2467] Sam raised an objection, "I think you've overlooked something, Mr. Rayne! There will be a fatal flaw in the courtroom—of course, Collins himself seems to have no chance of throwing this thing into the river, but what if there's a co-conspirator? Let's say, Collins shot and killed DeWitt, gave the gun to his good friend, and told this accomplice to throw the gun into the river five minutes after he left, and then he himself got off the train in a perfect manner, isn't that an interesting inference, Mr. Rayne?"
[2468] "Take it easy, Inspector, don't get too excited," Rayne calmly smiled. "We're talking about the basic legal aspects of Collins' detention and preparation for trial. I didn't ignore the possibility of a co-conspirator, not at all, but I have to ask you—who is this co-conspirator? Can you dig this person out before the trial? Or are you planning to convince the jury with nothing but empty theories? No, I don't think we have enough evidence to drag Collins into the DeWitt murder case at this point."
[2469] "You're right," Sam admitted, his face softening. "In fact, Bruno and I have no idea who this supposed co-conspirator is."
[2470] "Inspector, you should say, if there is indeed a co-conspirator." Rayne added straightforwardly.
[2471] The search team arrived at the parking lot, and Sam got into the police car, followed by Reane. Once everyone was ready, the convoy headed back to Weehawken, with heavy equipment on the trailer behind. Sam's expression showed that he was deep in thought, while Reane appeared relaxed and stretched his legs.
[2472] "You know, Inspector," he spoke again, "even from a psychological perspective, it's hard to argue that there's a co-conspirator.”
[2473] Sam grumbled.
[2474] "Let's follow the path that Collins killed DeWitt. This means there must be a co-conspirator, Collins gave him the gun, and he was supposed to throw it into the river five minutes after Collins got off the train from the car, here everything still makes sense. But this arrangement could only be based on a single design, that is, Collins tried to establish an unbreakable alibi; in other words, the location where the weapon was found had to be opposite the direction in which Collins was seen leaving, at the location where the train arrived five minutes later. But if the revolver was not found at a place within five minutes' drive from where he got off, this well-crafted alibi would not hold. Therefore, if Collins intended to do so, he must have been sure that the revolver would be found. However, where did we find it? In the river? Unless there is divine intervention, the gun may lie at the bottom of the river until the end of days. Can we still believe this scenario, that Collins indeed made every effort to arrange for the revolver to be found, to build his clever alibi? I guess you might say,” - at this moment, Sam did not intend to argue - "the gun accidentally fell into the river, or it was an accident or a mistake, the accomplice threw it out of the window, hoping it would fall beside the tracks. But if he really wanted the weapon to be found to prove Collins' alibi, could he have thrown it twenty feet away from the tracks? - from the tracks."
[2475] "But if this revolver had not been found at
[2476] "No, he wouldn't, if the co-conspirator just wanted to throw the gun out the window, a heavy gun like that wouldn't fly that far, it would only fall on either side of the tracks, ensuring that we would find it later."
[2477] "So, in conclusion," Sam muttered, "you've proven that the killer's intent was to make the gun disappear, which completely proves that Collins is not the killer."
[2478] "Looks like it, Chief," Rene said in a calm tone.
[2479] "Well," Sam sighed, "I admit I lost. Every time Bruno and I put all our efforts into catching someone, thinking they're the mysterious killer X you talk about, you let them go. It's almost become a routine, and I see this case is getting more and more complicated, getting more and more tangled, and getting more and more troublesome."
[2480] "On the contrary," Rene said solemnly, "we're about to reach the end."
[2481] Act XI
[2482] Hamlet Manor
[2483] October 13, Tuesday, 10:30 AM
[2484] Quincy stood by the phone in the dressing room at Hamlet Manor, which felt like his personal kingdom. Rene lay on the couch next to him. The room was dim, with only a faint beam of sunlight entering through the window.
[2485] Quincy spoke in a crisp voice on the phone: "But, Mr. Bruno, Rene instructed me to tell you to come to Hamlet Manor tonight at eleven o'clock sharp, and bring Inspector Sam along with some police officers... Excuse me, Mr. Bruno wants to know if the police officers will be in uniform... He also mentioned that it's an important matter. Please hold on." Quincy placed the receiver against his thin chest. "Rene, Mr. Bruno wants to know if the police officers will be in uniform; he also said it's an important matter."
[2486] "You can tell Mr. Bruno, the District Attorney," Raine said lazily, "that the detectives shouldn't wear uniforms. As for what we're doing, it's a very important outing to New Jersey. Tell him we're taking the West Side Line to Englewood, New Jersey, for a case-breaking operation."
[2487] Quincannon blinked and relayed the message as it was given to him.
[2488] Eleven o'clock, precisely.
[2489] Sam, the only calm and composed police officer in the Hamlet House library, seemed unperturbed by Raine's absence. Bruno, sitting in a large chair, looked anxious.
[2490] Fostat, the round, bumbling officer, rushed in and bowed repeatedly. Bruno immediately asked, "What is it?"
[2491] "Mr. Rain asked me to apologize to everyone, sir, please wait a few more minutes."
[2492] Bruno nodded puzzledly, and Sam made a face beside him.
[2493] During the wait, the detectives curiously browsed the odd furnishings in the room. The ceiling was high, and the bookshelves on three walls were full, reaching up to the ceiling, filled with thousands of books, with ladders leaning against the upper shelves. Surrounding the library were ancient high balconies, with two iron staircases spiraling up from the corners of the room, meeting in the middle. The books, neatly arranged according to the ancient English catalog, were labeled with bronze tags. A large round table dominated the bottom end of the room, but despite the lack of occupants, it exuded an aura of a guardian of the library. The only wall without bookshelves was adorned with strange objects. Bruno grew impatient and stood up, walking over to investigate. He saw that a thick oil painting, covered with glass, hung in the center of the wall, and the cursive script in the lower left corner indicated that it was a world map drawn in 1501. In addition, there were various costumes from the Elizabethan era that Rain had collected, neatly packaged and displayed on the floor beside the wall ...
[2494] Suddenly, the door to the library opened, and everyone turned around. Quasi entered first, holding the door handle wide open, with a smile of anticipation on his face that resembled an old tree stump.
[2495] A tall, sturdy, red-faced man strode in from the archway, surveying the room with pride. He had a strong chin, but his cheeks were sagging, and his dark circles under his eyes were clear signs of excessive drinking. He wore a full Scottish tweed suit - loose-fitting sports pants and a loose-fitting jacket; his hands were in his pockets, and he looked at everyone with his eyes wide open.
[2496] His appearance had an explosive effect, with Bruno the prosecutor standing frozen on the floor, with only his rapidly blinking eyes moving, as if his brain could not process the information from his visual nerves. But if Bruno's reaction was typical of surprise, Sam's was more subtle and deeper. He trembled like a startled child, his rock-hard jaw trembling and dropping; his eyes, usually cold and stern, were now filled with anxious panic. He blinked his eyes open and closed several times, his face suddenly pale.
[2497] "Good heavens," he whispered hoarsely, "Ha - Ha - Harry Lestrade!"
[2498] No one dared to move a muscle. After a long time, the figure standing by the door emitted a chilling, demonic chuckle, sending shivers down everyone's spines.
[2499] "Oh, deception always lingers in such a luxurious palace," said Mr. Philo Vance, the voice of Harry Lestrade.
[2500] That was the deep and hearty voice of Mr. Rain.
[2501] Act Twelve
[2502] On the Whipken-Newark train
[2503] October 14, Tuesday, midnight 12:18
[2504] An incredible journey...
[2505] History, like an old dog that cannot learn new tricks, repeats itself: the same train, the same pitch-black midnight, the same moment, the same clacking of the wheels over the tracks.
[2506] At midnight 12:18, Reine and a group of police officers return to that journey. The train runs between the starting point of Whipken station and the destination of Newburgh station, with Mr. Jules Reine sitting quietly in the rear car of the train, where, besides Sam, Bruno, and a few detectives, there are hardly any other passengers.
[2507] Reine is wrapped in a long coat, and a low, wide-brimmed felt hat covers his face. He sits at the window seat, turning his head to the window glass, saying nothing, as if asleep or lost in thought. Bruno and Sam sitting opposite and beside him are also silent, but they seem tense. This tense atmosphere seems to have infected the detectives scattered nearby, with few words spoken, and everyone sitting stiffly, like bars of steel; everyone waiting quietly, waiting for an action that they have no clue about, but know is crucial.
[2508] Sam can't sit still, he glances at Reine's covered face, sighs, and gets up, walking heavily out of the car. But in an instant, he rushes back, flushed with excitement. He sits down, leans forward, and whispers in Bruno's ear: "It's strange... Ehren and Imparale are in the car ahead. Should we tell Reine?"
[2509] Bruno turns to look at Reine, shrugs. "I think we should let him take charge of everything. The old man seems confident."
[2510] The train sways, stops. Bruno looks out the window, and they have arrived - he can clearly see - at the North Bergen station. He looks at his watch - it's 12:20. A few passengers board the train. The signal lamp on the platform flickers, the door clangs shut, and the train rumbles forward.
[2511] Within minutes, the conductor appeared at the front of the car, checking tickets as he came. When he reached Sam's group, he recognized him and greeted him with a friendly smile. Sam nodded coldly and took out money to buy tickets for everyone. The conductor took several two-pair tickets from the breast pocket of his overcoat, folded them skillfully, punched holes in two places, and tore them in half. He handed one half to Sam and put the other half in his other pocket...
[2512] Reine, who seemed to be sleeping or lost in thought, suddenly moved. He stood up, took off his felt hat, and took off his coat, turning to face the conductor. The conductor stared blankly at him. Reine reached into the collar of his tweed coat, took out a silver eyeglass case, opened it, and held the glasses in his hand. He didn't put on the glasses, but looked at the conductor thoughtfully and curiously. The face - fierce, slack, and with a hint of liquor - seemed to confuse the conductor.
[2513] The conductor's reaction was strange. He still held the ticket puncher in his hand, but stopped in mid-air, as if he had been attracted by something and unconsciously looked at the cold man standing in front of him. Initially, it was disbelief, then it changed to extreme fear. His mouth silently opened, his sturdy body began to tremble, and his originally grape-like red face instantly turned pale. After a long time, he opened his mouth and a hoarse voice came out, a name, "Lunster...". And while the conductor stood there like a stone pillar, paralyzed by a nightmare, Harry Lunster, disguised as himself, smiled and put down the silver eyeglass case and glasses in his hand. He reached into his pocket again, took out a heavy metal object...
[2514] Reine's smile reappeared, but this time it was directed at Bruno and Sam's two blank faces, who were still sitting in their chairs and couldn't move. They both had wrinkles on their foreheads and looked at Reine, then at the conductor. The conductor was now dejected, his trembling tongue licking his lips, leaning against the back of his chair - with desperate, ashamed, and still disbelieving eyes, he still stared at the handcuffs on his wrist.
[2515] Reine calmly said to Sam, "Inspector, do you have the ink pad I asked for?"
[2516] Sam didn't answer, but pulled out a sealed ink pad and a piece of paper from his pocket.
[2517] "Inspector, I need you to take this man's fingerprints."
[2518] Sam remained in a daze, somewhat laboriously standing up and walking towards the conductor… The conductor stood next to Lancaster, looking as if he had been drained of energy, his whole body leaning against the back of his chair. When Sam pressed his unconscious hand into the ink pad, Lancaster turned back to his seat, reaching into the pocket of his removed long coat to pull out an Manila envelope. This was the one he had received on Monday of this week. Lancaster took out the fingerprint photo that had been sent from Uruguay, smiling as he looked at the two stunned people.
[2519] "Done, Inspector?"
